The Etymology of Philosophy: Love of Wisdom
Etymology of philosophy and definition of philosopher.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Greek roots of 'falsafa' and explain how the term defines the philosopher as a lover of wisdom.

Core Idea
Philosophy is love of wisdom, not just a body of knowledge.

Before we dive into the technical definitions, we must understand what the word 'philosophy' originally meant. This lesson explores the Greek etymology of 'falsafa' and its implications for the practice of philosophy, especially within the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Break down the word 'falsafa'
Identify that 'falsafa' is a Greek compound of 'fila' (love) and 'sufa' (wisdom). Recognize that the Arabic term is a direct loanword.
The term passed into Arabic via translations from Greek works.
2
Derive the meaning of 'faylasuf'
From the word 'falsafa', the term 'faylasuf' (philosopher) is derived, meaning 'lover of wisdom'. Notice that it emphasizes love, not mere possession.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this etymology shapes the concept: philosophy is an ongoing pursuit, not a finished doctrine. This aligns with the Islamic emphasis on seeking knowledge ('ilm').
This contrasts with later systematic definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلسفة لغة واصطلاحا: الفلسفة كلمة يونانية مكونة من مقطعين: فيلا بمعنى المحب، وسوفا: العلم والحكمة. ومنها اشتقت كلمة الفلسفة. فالفيلسوف هو محب العلم والحكمة. وللفلسفة مفاهيم تختلف باختلاف المذاهب والنظم والمراحل التاريخية. ومن المفيد أن نعرض بعض التعريفات للفلسفة قبل أن نسجل ماهية العلاقة بين الفلسفة ونظرية المعرفة. أ - الفلسفة هي علم الكل. ب - الفلسفة هي علم أصعب الأشياء. ج - الفلسفة علم المبادئ والعلل الأولى. د - الفلسفة هي معرفة الأمور الإلهية والبشرية. هـ - الفلسفة هي فن الفنون وعلم العلوم. و - الفلسفة هي حكمة الحياة في ضوء ما يقضي به العقل. ز - الفلسفة مجموعة من الأفكار المترابطة في صورة مذاهب فكرية تبحث عن الحقيقة الكونية وظواهرها الطبيعية والبشرية. ح - الفلسفة: الكشف عن الحقيقة. والحقيقة معناها مطابقة الفكر للواقع.
Philosophy linguistically and terminologically: Philosophy is a Greek word composed of two parts: 'fila' meaning love, and 'sufa' meaning knowledge and wisdom. From it the word philosophy is derived. So the philosopher is the lover of knowledge and wisdom. Philosophy has concepts that differ according to schools, systems, and historical stages. It is useful to present some definitions of philosophy before we record the nature of the relationship between philosophy and the theory of knowledge. A - Philosophy is the science of the whole. B - Philosophy is the science of the most difficult things. C - Philosophy is the science of principles and first causes. D - Philosophy is knowledge of divine and human matters. E - Philosophy is the art of arts and the science of sciences. F - Philosophy is the wisdom of life in light of what reason dictates. G - Philosophy is a set of interconnected ideas in the form of intellectual schools that search for cosmic truth and its natural and human phenomena. H - Philosophy is the uncovering of truth. And truth means the correspondence of thought to reality.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'philosophy' etymologically as a Greek word meaning love of wisdom, then lists eight different definitions from various perspectives, highlighting the diversity of philosophical concepts. The passage explains that philosophy is defined as the science of wholes, the science of principles and first causes, the art of arts, etc. Each definition emphasizes a different aspect, such as universality, difficulty, or the pursuit of truth. The passage serves as an introduction to the relationship between philosophy and the theory of knowledge.

Key Terms
فلسفة
فيلسوف
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the etymological meaning of 'philosophy'?
A) A Greek word meaning love of wisdom.
B) An Arabic word meaning science.
C) A Persian word meaning knowledge.
D) A Latin word meaning art.

Key Takeaway
The term 'philosophy' originates from Greek 'love of wisdom', defining the philosopher as a lifelong lover of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a short paragraph explaining how the Greek etymology of 'philosophy' as 'love of wisdom' relates to the Islamic concept of 'ilm (knowledge) and the role of the seeker of knowledge (talib al-ilm).

Eight Definitions of Philosophy: A Survey
Author lists eight definitions of philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the eight definitions of philosophy provided in the passage according to their thematic focus.

Core Idea
Philosophy has been defined in many ways, each highlighting a different aspect of its subject or method.

After establishing the etymology, the author lists eight distinct definitions of philosophy. These definitions range from viewing philosophy as the science of the whole to the uncovering of truth. This lesson will help you organize these definitions and see how they complement each other.

Guided Path
1
Read all eight definitions
Go through definitions A to H and note the key Arabic terms used in each, such as 'al-kull' (the whole), 'al-mabadi' (principles), and 'al-haqiqa' (truth).
2
Group by subject matter
Definitions A, C, and G focus on the scope (whole, principles, cosmic truth); B and F emphasize difficulty and wisdom of life; D, E, and H concern content (divine/human, art of arts, uncovering truth).
Notice how each definition contributes to a comprehensive picture.
3
Identify the most comprehensive definition
Consider which definition(s) capture the essence of philosophy as described in the Islamic tradition. For example, definition C (science of principles and first causes) is central to kalam and falsafa.
4
Reflect on the purpose
The author states these definitions are listed before discussing the relationship between philosophy and theory of knowledge. Think about how each definition prepares the ground for that discussion.

Truth as Correspondence: The Goal of Philosophy
Author gives definition of philosophy as unveiling truth and defines truth as correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the correspondence theory of truth as presented in the passage and relate it to Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Truth is defined as the correspondence of thought to reality.

The last definition in the passage defines philosophy as 'the uncovering of truth', and then defines truth as the correspondence of thought to reality. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows its foundational role in Islamic logic and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Locate definition H
Find the last definition in the passage: 'Philosophy is the uncovering of truth'. Then note the separate sentence defining truth.
2
Analyze the definition of truth
Truth is 'mutaabaqat al-fikr li al-waaqi'' (correspondence of thought to reality). Break down each word: fikr (thought), mutaabaqa (correspondence), waaqi' (reality).
This is the classic correspondence theory of truth.
3
Connect to Islamic logic
In Islamic logic ('ilm al-mantiq), truth is a property of propositions (qadiyya). A proposition is true if it corresponds to the state of affairs. This is used in evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh.
For example, the proposition 'the world is created' is true if it corresponds to reality.
4
Apply to a kalam example
Consider the proposition 'the Qur'an is the word of God'. According to correspondence theory, this statement is true if and only if the Qur'an truly is God's speech. This underpins theological debates.

Defining Philosophy: Root Investigation and Systematic Structure
Author gives a synthesized definition and lists three main topics: cosmology, theory of values, and theory of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct the author's definition of philosophy into its genus and differentia and evaluate its comprehensiveness.

Core Idea
Philosophy is not idle speculation; it is the systematic search for the roots of ideas.

In this passage, the author synthesizes earlier definitions into a concise yet comprehensive definition of philosophy. This definition not only tells us what philosophy studies but also how it studies it. Understanding this definition is the first step in recognizing the scope of philosophical inquiry in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence that gives the 'comprehensive definition'. In the Arabic, look for 'تعريفا جامعا للفلسفة'.
The definition is in the first few lines.
2
Identify the two components
The definition has two parts: the object of investigation (جذور الأفكار) and the method (إعطاء صورة منظمة مترابطة). Write them down.
3
Determine the genus and differentia
The genus can be 'investigation into roots' and the differentia is 'providing an organized interconnected picture'. Is this a real definition (hadd) or a description (rasm)? Explain.
Consider the logical structure of a proper hadd.
4
Test the definition
Does this definition include all instances of philosophy? Does it include Islamic philosophy? Yes. Does it exclude non-philosophical thinking? Possibly.
Evaluate completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن خلال التعريفات السابقة يمكننا أن نستنبط تعريفا جامعا للفلسفة: هي البحث في جذور الأفكار وإعطاء صورة منظمة مترابطة لهذه الجذور. فنقول: ومن أهم موضوعات الفلسفة: (1) علم الكونيات: ومعناه التفكير في الكون كيف نشأ؟ وكيف تطور؟ ويسمى هذا العلم أيضا: نظرية الوجود. أي هل هو مادي فقط؟ أم روحي فقط؟ (2) نظرية القيم: أي التمييز بين القيم النسبية المتغيرة (الأعراف الاجتماعية والعادات والتقاليد والقيم المطلقة الثابتة (الحق والخير والجمال وغيرها) (3) نظرية المعرفة وهي الموضوع الأساس في أركان هذه الدراسة. 2 - نظرية المعرفة: أي (مبحث المعرفة) المعرفة: مفردة استعملت منذ القدم فقد عرفها طيطاطوس أحد فلاسفة اليونان القدماء فقال المعرفة هي الظن الصادق المصحوب بالدليل) وهذا التعريف يقودنا إلى القول أن معرفة الشيء تقوم في تحليله إلى العناصر التي يتألف منها. وقد اهتم فلاسفة المسلمين القدماء بدراسة المعرفة وإلقاء الضوء الساطع عليها ولكن اهتمامهم بنظرية المعرفة جاء في طيات البحث اللغوي والمنطقي وهو ما يختلف تماما عما عليه مصطلح نظرية المعرفة لدى حكماء أو
And from the previous definitions we can deduce a comprehensive definition of philosophy: it is the investigation into the roots of ideas and giving an organized interconnected picture of these roots. So we say: Among the most important topics of philosophy are: (1) Cosmology: meaning thinking about the universe—how did it arise? And how did it develop? This science is also called the theory of existence. That is, is it only material? Or only spiritual? (2) Theory of values: meaning distinguishing between changing relative values (social norms, customs, and traditions) and fixed absolute values (truth, goodness, beauty, and others). (3) Theory of knowledge, which is the main subject in the pillars of this study. 2 - Theory of knowledge: that is (the study of knowledge). Knowledge: a term used since ancient times; Theaetetus, one of the ancient Greek philosophers, defined it as 'true belief accompanied by evidence.' This definition leads us to say that knowledge of a thing consists in analyzing it into its constituent elements. The ancient Muslim philosophers were interested in studying knowledge and shedding bright light on it, but their interest in the theory of knowledge came within linguistic and logical research, which is completely different from what the term theory of knowledge means among the wise or...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage synthesizes the previous definitions into a comprehensive definition of philosophy as the investigation into the roots of ideas, and then enumerates the main topics of philosophy: cosmology, theory of values, and theory of knowledge. It then focuses on theory of knowledge, defining knowledge as true belief accompanied by evidence, referencing the Greek philosopher Theaetetus. The passage also notes that ancient Muslim philosophers studied knowledge within linguistic and logical research, distinguishing their approach from later philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The definition of philosophy as the investigation into the roots of ideas to produce an organized picture distinguishes it from other forms of inquiry.

?

The Three Main Topics of Philosophy
Author gives a synthesized definition and lists three main topics: cosmology, theory of values, and theory of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize philosophical questions into three main topics: cosmology, theory of values, and theory of knowledge.

Core Idea
Philosophy's domain divides into three fundamental pillars: the universe, values, and knowledge.

After defining philosophy, the author lists its three main topics. These topics represent the major concerns of philosophical thought: the nature of the universe, the foundation of values, and the possibility of knowledge. In Islamic thought, these correspond to ilahiyyat, akhlaq, and mantiq/usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three topics
From the list, extract 'علم الكونيات', 'نظرية القيم', and 'نظرية المعرفة'.
2
Determine the core question of each
For cosmology: 'كيف نشأ الكون؟' (how did the universe arise?). For values: distinguish between relative and absolute values. For knowledge: what is knowledge and how do we attain it?
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Classify: 'هل العالم محدث أم قديم؟' (Is the world created or eternal?) -> cosmology. 'هل العدل مطلق أم نسبي؟' -> theory of values. 'هل العقل مصدر للمعرفة؟' -> theory of knowledge.
4
Reflect on interconnection
How do these topics relate? For example, the question of whether values are absolute may depend on whether we can know them.

Source Text

This science is also called the theory of existence. That is, is it only material? Or only spiritual? (2) Theory of values: meaning distinguishing between changing relative values (social norms, customs, and traditions) and fixed absolute values (truth, goodness, beauty, and others). (3) Theory of knowledge, which is the main subject in the pillars of this study. 2 - Theory of knowledge: that is (the study of knowledge). The Definition of Knowledge
Author gives a synthesized definition and lists three main topics: cosmology, theory of values, and theory of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the definition of knowledge as 'true belief accompanied by evidence' and identify its three components.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not mere opinion; it requires truth, belief, and justification.

This passage delves into the theory of knowledge, presenting a classic definition from Theaetetus: knowledge is true belief accompanied by evidence. This definition has been influential in both Greek and Islamic philosophy. The author also notes how Muslim philosophers approached epistemology within linguistic and logical studies, a distinct angle.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Find the sentence defining 'معرفة' (knowledge). It is 'الظن الصادق المصحوب بالدليل'.
2
Identify the three components
Belief (ظن), truth (صادق), and evidence (دليل). Write each component.
3
Test the definition against counterexamples
A lucky guess (true belief without evidence) is not knowledge. A false belief is not knowledge. A belief without conviction? The definition uses 'ظن' which implies probability, but usually knowledge requires certainty. Note this nuance.
4
Consider the Muslim philosophers' approach
The passage says they studied knowledge within linguistic and logical research. What does that mean? For example, they analyzed the term 'علم' in the Qur'an and Hadith, and used logic to evaluate arguments.

It then focuses on theory of knowledge, defining knowledge as true belief accompanied by evidence, referencing the Greek philosopher Theaetetus. The passage also notes that ancient Muslim philosophers studied knowledge within linguistic and logical research, distinguishing their approach from later philosophers.

Parsing the Synthetic Definition of Philosophy and Its Branches
Author defines philosophy synthetically and lists its three main topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main topics of philosophy as defined in the passage and relate each to a corresponding Islamic science.

Core Idea
Philosophy investigates the roots of ideas; its three branches parallel core Islamic inquiries into existence, values, and knowledge.

This lesson unpacks a synthetic definition of philosophy that arranges its core subjects. You will learn to parse this definition and see how each branch connects to Islamic disciplines like kalam, usul al-fiqh, and mantiq.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of philosophy
Find the Arabic phrase defining philosophy. Notice it describes philosophy as a 'search in the roots of ideas' and giving an 'organized, interconnected picture.'
This is a synthetic definition; it combines elements from earlier definitions.
2
List the three main topics
The passage enumerates three subjects: cosmology, theory of values, and theory of knowledge. Write them down in Arabic and English.
3
Analyze each topic's content
For each topic, note the brief description given. Cosmology includes thinking about the universe's origin and development; theory of values distinguishes relative from absolute values; theory of knowledge is called the fundamental subject.
Pay attention to the alternative name for cosmology: 'theory of existence.'
4
Relate each topic to Islamic sciences
Connect cosmology to kalam (theology), theory of values to usul al-fiqh (principles of jurisprudence), and theory of knowledge to mantiq (logic) and usul al-fiqh. Consider how Islamic scholars addressed these questions.
For example, kalam discusses whether the universe is created or eternal; usul al-fiqh distinguishes divine commands from customary norms; mantiq provides tools for certitude.

(2) Theory of values: i.e., distinguishing between relative changing values (social norms and customs) and absolute fixed values (truth, good, beauty, and others). The author first states that philosophy is the investigation of the roots of ideas, arranging them into a coherent picture. Then he lists three major branches: cosmology (including theories of existence), value theory (distinguishing relative from absolute values), and epistemology, which he identifies as the main focus of this study. The definition emphasizes philosophy as a meta-level inquiry into foundational concepts.

Analyzing Definitions of Knowledge: Greek and Ash'ari Perspectives
Author presents Greek and Muslim definitions of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare and evaluate definitions of knowledge from Greek and Islamic sources using the logical criteria of genus and differentia.

Core Idea
Knowledge is defined differently—perception, true opinion, perfect action—each definition reveals a different aspect of knowing.

This passage presents the Greek definition of knowledge as true opinion with evidence, alongside four definitions from prominent Ash'ari theologians. Each definition has a distinct emphasis. In this lesson, we will unpack these definitions and assess them using logical tools from classical Islamic logic, particularly the requirements of a complete definition (hadd).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term is 'al-'ilm' or 'al-ma'rifa'. Note that the Greek definition uses 'al-zann al-sadiq' (true opinion). Identify the definiendum for each definition.
Al-Juwayni and al-Ghazali focus on the difficulty of defining knowledge, not providing a positive definition.
2
Extract the proposed genus and differentia
For each definition, identify the broader category (genus) and the distinguishing feature (differentia). For example, Greek: genus = 'zann' (opinion), differentia = 'sadiq ma'a al-dalil' (true with evidence). Al-Bahili: genus = 'idrak' (perception), differentia = 'ala ma huwa 'alayh' (as it is). Ibn Furak: genus = 'ma yasihhu minhu' (what from the knower), differentia = 'itqan al-fi'l' (perfecting action).
Al-Juwayni and al-Ghazali do not provide a clear genus-differentia structure.
3
Test each definition for completeness
Check if the definition includes all instances of knowledge and excludes non-knowledge. For instance, the Greek definition might exclude certain knowledge that is not 'opinion' (like 'ilm al-yaqin). Al-Bahili's definition may be too broad (perception includes many things). Ibn Furak's definition ties knowledge to action, possibly excluding theoretical knowledge.
Al-Ghazali explicitly states the difficulty of giving a complete genus-differentia definition.
4
Compare the definitions
Which definition is a hadd (complete) and which is a rasm (description)? Discuss al-Ghazali's point that most things resist precise definition. Consider the practical implications in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ - نظرية المعرفة: أي (مبحث المعرفة ) المعرفة : مفردة استعملت منذ القدم فقد عرفها طيطاطوس أحد فلاسفة اليونان القدماء فقال المعرفة هي الظن الصادق المصحوب بالدليل) وهذا التعريف يقودنا الى القول ان معرفة الشيء تقوم في تحليله الى العناصر التي يتالف منها . وقد اهتم فلاسفة المسلمين القدماء بدراسة المعرفة والقاء الضوء الساطع عليها ولكن اهتمامهم بنظرية المعرفة جاء في طيات البحث اللغوي والمنطقي وهو ما يختلف تماما عما عليه مصطلح نظرية المعرفة لدى حكماء أو فلاسفة الديار الاوربية في القرون المتأخرة. فقد اثار المتكلمون في الديار الاسلامية جدلا طويلا بخصوص تعريف العلم والمعرفة قبل ان تتقارب المفاهيم والمصطلحات لتتصادم فيما بينها من جديد ومن هذه التعريفات نقتبس الآتي : الشيخ أبو الحسن الباهلي الأشعري ت ٣٢٤ هـ قال : العلم هو ادراك المعلوم على ما هو عليه) ( أبو المعالي الجويني ت ٤٧٨ هـ قال : (إن تعريف العلم ليس ضروريا بل هو نظري ولكن يعسر تحديده ) ؛ إبن فورك الاشعري ت ٤٠٦ هـ قال : العلم ( ما يصح ممن قام به اتقان الفعل أي إحكامه وتخليته عن وجوه الخلل ) الامام الغزالي ت ٥٠٥ هـ قال ( يعسر تحديد العلم بعبارة محررة جامعة للجنس والفصل الذاتيين فان ذلك متعسر في أكثر الاشياء)
2 - Theory of knowledge: i.e., the study of knowledge. Knowledge: a term used since antiquity; Theaetetus, one of the ancient Greek philosophers, defined it as 'true opinion accompanied by evidence.' This definition leads us to say that knowledge of a thing consists in analyzing it into the elements of which it is composed. The early Muslim philosophers were concerned with studying knowledge and shedding bright light on it, but their concern with the theory of knowledge came within the folds of linguistic and logical research, which is completely different from the term theory of knowledge among the scholars or philosophers of European lands in later centuries. The theologians in Islamic lands stirred a long debate regarding the definition of knowledge and epistemology before concepts and terms converged only to clash again. From these definitions we quote the following: Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH) said: 'Knowledge is the perception of the known as it is.' Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni (d. 478 AH) said: 'The definition of knowledge is not self-evident but theoretical, yet it is difficult to determine it.' Ibn Furak al-Ash'ari (d. 406 AH) said: 'Knowledge is what, from the one who possesses it, results in perfecting an action, i.e., making it sound and free from defects.' Imam al-Ghazali (d. 505 AH) said: 'It is difficult to define knowledge with a precise expression that includes [its] genus and specific differentia, for that is difficult in most things.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage explores the concept of knowledge (al-ma'rifa) from multiple perspectives. It begins with the Greek definition by Theaetetus: knowledge is true belief with a rational account (dalin). The author notes this implies decomposition into elements. Then it contrasts the approach of early Muslim philosophers, who embedded epistemology within linguistic and logical studies, with later European philosophy. The text then lists four definitions from prominent Ash'ari theologians: Al-Bahili defines knowledge as accurate perception; Al-Juwayni asserts that knowledge is a theoretical concept, not self-evident, and hard to define; Ibn Furak ties knowledge to the ability to perform an action perfectly; and Al-Ghazali emphasizes the difficulty of providing a genus-differentia definition. These definitions reflect the richness of Islamic epistemological debates.

العلم

Evaluating Definitions of Knowledge: Al-Shirazi and Tafsir Mawdu'i
Author critiques two definitions of knowledge and the common definition of acquired knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate three definitions of knowledge by applying the author's criterion of comprehensiveness, identifying which types of knowledge each definition excludes.

Core Idea
A valid definition of knowledge must include both presential and acquired knowledge.

This lesson examines the author's critique of three definitions of knowledge. The author rejects a vague definition, then targets al-Shirazi's idealistic definition for excluding material things, and the Tafsir Mawdu'i definition for overemphasizing material phenomena and lacking clarity. Finally, he points out that even the standard definition of acquired knowledge is incomplete because it neglects knowledge that arises from within the soul. You will learn to spot such defects in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions critiqued
Locate each definition in the Arabic: the vague one ('knowledge after ignorance'), al-Shirazi's ('presence of a pure [entity]'), and the Tafsir Mawdu'i's ('transformation of external realities into mental facts'). Also note the common definition of acquired knowledge criticized at the end.
2
Analyze the author's objection to al-Shirazi's definition
The author says this definition is 'excessively idealistic' (mufriṭ fī al-mithāliyya) and does not include knowledge of material things. Ask: What type of knowledge does it exclude? (Knowledge through the senses of physical objects.)
3
Analyze the author's objection to the Tafsir Mawdu'i definition
The author says it exaggerates the value of material phenomena and, in juristic terminology, excludes both presential and acquired knowledge. Also, the term 'mirror of thought' is vague. Understand that it fails to account for knowledge that is not a reflection of external realities.
4
Understand the incompleteness of the common definition of acquired knowledge
The common definition defines knowledge as 'an image obtained from outside by the soul'. The author says it is incomplete because some knowledge arises from the soul's own essence through mental operations, not from external connection.
5
Synthesize the author's criterion
A comprehensive definition of knowledge must include both presential knowledge (direct, internal) and acquired knowledge (from external perception). Each critiqued definition fails to cover one or both types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم الحضوري وقيل : المعرفة ( العلم بعد الجهالة ) وهذا التعريف مبهم عام صدر المتألهين الشيرازي قال : العلم (حضور مجرد عند مجرد)؛ وهذا التعريف مفرط في المثالية فهو لا يشمل العلم بالماديات. اما صاحب التفسير الموضوعي لبيان القرآن فقال (المعرفة عبارة عن تبديل الواقعيات الخارجية الى حقائق ذهنية وانعكاسها كما هي في مرآة الفكر) هذا التعريف فيه مغالاة في قيمة الظواهر المادية وهو بعبارة الفقهاء لا يتضمن العلم الحضوري والعلم الحصولي ثم ان مرآة الفكر مفهوم عام غير محدد . ان تعريف العلم بكونه صورة حاصلة من الخارج لدى النفس علم حصولي تعريف ناقص لا يعم الاجزاء جميعها لان هناك علوما لا تقف النفس عليها بالاتصال بالخارج وانما تقف عليها من صميم ذاتها في ظل عمليات ذهنية خاصة .
Presential knowledge, and it was said: knowledge (knowledge after ignorance) – this definition is vague and general. Sadr al-Muta'allihin al-Shirazi said: knowledge is (a pure presence of a pure [entity]); this definition is excessively idealistic, so it does not include knowledge of material things. As for the author of al-Tafsir al-Mawdu'i li-Bayan al-Qur'an, he said: (Knowledge is the transformation of external realities into mental facts and their reflection as they are in the mirror of thought). This definition exaggerates the value of material phenomena, and in the terminology of jurists, it does not include presential knowledge and acquired knowledge. Moreover, 'mirror of thought' is a vague, undefined concept. Indeed, defining knowledge as an image obtained from outside by the soul – acquired knowledge – is an incomplete definition that does not cover all parts, for there are knowledges that the soul arrives at not through connection with the outside but from its own essence through special mental operations.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a critical evaluation of two definitions of knowledge. The author first dismisses a vague definition of knowledge as 'knowledge after ignorance' as too general. Then he critiques al-Shirazi's definition of knowledge as a pure presence of a pure entity, arguing it is overly idealistic and fails to account for knowledge of material things. Next, he addresses the definition from Tafsir Mawdu'i that describes knowledge as the reflection of external realities in the mind's mirror. The author objects that this definition overvalues material phenomena and, in the terminology of jurists (fugaha), excludes both presential knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḥudūrī) and acquired knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḥusūlī). He further notes that the term 'mirror of thought' is vague. Finally, he criticizes the common definition of acquired knowledge as an image obtained from outside the soul, stating it is incomplete because there are knowledges the soul derives from within itself through special mental processes. The critique emphasizes that a comprehensive definition must cover all types of knowledge.

العلم الحصولي
مرآة الفكر

Key Takeaway

Distinguishing Ma'rifah and 'Ilm
Author distinguishes ma'rifah as discovery and 'ilm as recording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ma'rifah and 'ilm as defined by the author and identify the functional difference between discovery and recording in Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Ma'rifah is discovery; 'ilm is the recording of that discovery as certainty.

The author provides a clear functional distinction between two terms often used interchangeably in Islamic thought. This lesson will help you see how ma'rifah and 'ilm complement each other as stages in the acquisition of certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate the Arabic terms ma'rifah and 'ilm in the passage. Note that both are translated as 'knowledge' but the author is assigning them distinct roles.
2
Extract the function of each
Ma'rifah is defined as 'the discovery of something new' (ikhtishaf shay' jadid). 'Ilm is defined as 'the fixing and recording of that discovery in the depths of the self and counting it among certainties' (tathbit wa tasjil dhalika al-ikhtishaf fi a'maq al-dhat wa 'adduh min al-musallamat).
3
Understand the relationship
The relationship is sequential: ma'rifah is the initial act of discovery, and 'ilm is the subsequent process of internalizing and confirming that discovery as certain. They are not synonyms but stages.
The author uses 'while' (fi hin anna) to contrast the two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان المعرفة كما نرى هي اكتشاف شيء جديد في حين ان العلم هو تثبيت وتسجيل ذلك الاكتشاف في اعماق الذات وعده من المسلمات .
Indeed, knowledge (ma‘rifah) as we see is the discovery of something new, while knowledge (‘ilm) is the fixing and recording of that discovery in the depths of the self and counting it among certainties.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage offers the author's own conceptual distinction between two terms often used synonymously: ma‘rifah and ‘ilm. The author redefines ma‘rifah as the initial act of discovering something new, while ‘ilm is the subsequent process of stabilizing, recording, and internalizing that discovery as a certainty. This is a definitional passage that adds precision to epistemological language within the Islamic tradition. The author does not provide examples, but one can think of fiqh: discovering a new ruling (ma‘rifah) versus memorizing it and accepting it as part of one's certain knowledge (‘ilm).

Key Terms
المعرفة
العلم

C) ‘Ilm is discovery; ma‘rifah is the recording and confirmation.
D) They are unrelated terms.

Key Takeaway
The author distinguishes ma'rifah as the initial discovery of something new from 'ilm as the subsequent fixing of that discovery as a settled certainty.

Critique of Modern Definitions of Theory of Knowledge
Author presents and critiques two definitions of theory of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the weaknesses in modern definitions of the theory of knowledge using criteria of precision and completeness.

Core Idea
A definition must capture the essence, not just list problems.

This passage examines two modern definitions of nazariyyat al-ma‘rifah. The author rejects both for different reasons: one is too vague, the other merely poses questions. You will learn how to apply these same standards of critique to definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
Identify where the first definition (from al-Mawsū‘a al-Falsafiyya al-Mukhtaṣara) and the second (from Dr. Abd al-Rahman Badawi) are presented in the Arabic.
The first begins with 'عرفتها الموسوعة الفلسفية المختصرة بأنها...' and the second with 'أما الدكتور عبد الرحمن بدوي...'.
2
Analyse the first definition's shortcomings
The author calls it 'general and vague' (عمومية وإبهام). Why? It lists many items without a unifying thread.
The list includes perception, certainty, conjecture, error, etc., which are disparate topics.
3
Analyse the second definition's shortcomings
The author says it is a set of questions, not an answer. What should a definition provide instead?
A definition should answer 'what is it?' not just raise issues.
4
Extract the criteria for a good definition
From the critique, infer what the author expects: a definition should be precise, coherent, and give the gist of the subject.
The author lacks a positive definition here, but the criticisms imply these criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن نظرية المعرفة كمصطلح حديث ظهر الى الوجود في العصور المتأخرة وله تعاريف عدة فقد عرفتها الموسوعة الفلسفية المختصرة بأنها (مجموعة متنوعة من المشكلات الفلسفية ليس بينها رباط وثيق تتعلق بأفكار من قبيل المعرفة ، الادراك ، التيقن ، التخمين ، الوقوع في الخطأ ، التذكر ، التبين ، الاثبات ، الاستدلال ، التأكيد ، التعزير ، التساؤل ، التأمل ، التخيل ، رؤية الاحلام ، وهلم جرا ولا يخفى ما في هذا التعريف من عمومية وإبهام أما الدكتور عبد الرحمن بدوي في موسوعته الفلسفية فقد بين نظرية المعرفة بأنها (دراسة المدى الذي يستطيع عقلنا من خلاله الوصول الى ادراك حقيقة الكون والطبيعة والانسان ؟ وماهي ادوات المعرفة الصحيحة ؟ وما قيمة هذه الادوات وادوارها في تحصيل المعرفة البشرية ان تعريف الدكتور بدوي هو تعريف بأهم مشكلات المعرفة وبعبارة اخرى هو طرح المجموعة من الاستفهامات وليس جوابا عن فحوى السؤال
Indeed, the theory of knowledge as a modern term came into existence in later ages and has several definitions. The Concise Philosophical Encyclopedia defined it as: (A diverse set of philosophical problems with no close connection among them, concerning ideas such as knowledge, perception, certainty, conjecture, error, memory, clarification, proof, inference, confirmation, support, questioning, reflection, imagination, dreaming, and so on). And it is not hidden what generality and vagueness this definition contains. As for Dr. Abd al-Rahman Badawi in his Philosophical Encyclopedia, he explained the theory of knowledge as: (A study of the extent to which our mind can attain the reality of the universe, nature, and man? What are the correct tools of knowledge? What is the value of these tools and their roles in acquiring human knowledge?) Dr. Badawi's definition is a definition of the most important problems of knowledge; in other words, it is a set of questions and not an answer to the gist of the question.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage critiques two contemporary definitions of the theory of knowledge (nazariyyat al-ma‘rifah). The author first notes that the term is modern and has multiple definitions. He quotes the Concise Philosophical Encyclopedia's definition, which lists a range of philosophical problems without a coherent connection. The author criticizes this definition for its generality and vagueness. Then he presents Dr. Abd al-Rahman Badawi's definition, which frames the theory of knowledge as a study of the mind's capacity to know reality and questions about the tools of knowledge. The author argues that this definition merely lists important problems of knowledge rather than providing a substantive answer about what the theory of knowledge is. Both definitions fail, in the author's view, to give a precise and meaningful definition of the field.

Key Terms
نظرية المعرفة
الموسوعة الفلسفية المختصرة

Key Takeaway
Good definitions in Islamic sciences must be precise and answer 'what is it?', not merely list related problems.

Classification of Ideas into Three Types in Descartes' Theory
Descartes' threefold classification of ideas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between innate ideas, sensations, and images as defined in Descartes' theory, and correctly classify examples of each.

Core Idea
Ideas come from three sources: innate nature, sensation, and imagination.

This passage presents Descartes' division of human ideas into three categories. Understanding this classification is crucial for grasping the rationalist epistemology that influenced later Islamic thinkers like Mulla Sadra. Let's examine each type and see how they differ in origin and certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the passage and list the three types of ideas: innate ideas, sensations, and images. Note the Arabic terms: الأفكار الفطرية, المحسوسات, الصور.
The classification appears at the end of the passage.
2
Define each category
For each type, note its source (innate nature, sense perception, imagination), its certainty (certain for innate ideas, less certain for others), and examples given.
Innate ideas are perceived intuitively (بالبداهة) and are error-free; sensations are from external objects; images are invented by the imagination.
3
Compare and contrast the categories
How do the three types differ in origin, reliability, and relation to reality? Innate ideas are from God and true, sensations are from the senses and can be deceptive, images are mental constructs.
This comparison is key to Descartes' epistemology.
4
Apply the classification
Given a list of ideas (e.g., the idea of God, the taste of honey, the image of a winged horse), classify each as innate, sensation, or image. Justify your answer.
Use the characteristics from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نظرية الافكار الفطرية
رائدها الفيلسوف الفرنسي رينيه ديكارت ومفادها أن هناك منبعين للافكار احدهما الاحساس فنحن نتصور الحرارة والنور والطعم والصوت من اجل احساسنا بها . والآخر الفطرة فالذهن البشري يمتلك معاني وتصورات لم تنبثق عن الحس وانما هي ثابتة في صميم الفطرة . سأعد ان السماء والهواء والارض والالوان والاشكال والاصوات وسائر الاشياء الخارجية ليست الا أوهاما أو احلاما . وسأعد نفسي من دون يدين ولا عينين ولا لحم ولا دم ولا أملك أية حواس واني مخدوع . ولكن كوني مخدوعا يؤكد أنني موجود . وهذه حقيقة يقينية - فأنا افكر اذن أنا موجود - إني اجد فكرة الله في ذهني فلابد من ان يكون لها من مصدر ولا يمكن اكون انا مصدرها . ان هذه الظاهرة الذهنية بما لها من كمال يمتنع ان يكون الذهن مصدرها بل لابد أن يكون سببها اكمل من النفس والذهن وهذه الفكرة فطرية وكل فكرة فطرية ليس لها مصدر الا الله وكذلك كل البدهيات وكل فكرة تنتهي الى الله هي فكرة صادقة فلو لم تكن صادقة لكان تزويد الله الانسان بها خدعة وكذبا وهو مستحيل على الله الكامل المطلق ومن ثم ان كل فكرة فطرية في الطبيعة الانسانية هي فكرة صادقة ملقاة من جانب الله. وانها معرفة صحيحة صادقة . ان الافكار الانسانية في هذه نظرية تقسم الى ثلاثة اصناف هي:
١ الافكار الفطرية : التي يدركها الانسان بالبداهة وليس للخطأ سبيل كفكرة الله والافكار الرياضية والحركة والشكل والامتداد والجهل واليقين والشك والامور الفطرية التي اقصدها هي المعلومات البدائية الاصيلة التي نتوصل بها الى جميع المعارف فالافكار السرمدية توجد فينا بالفطرة إذ يولد الانسان مزودا بها منذ الولادة يدركها الانسان دفعة واحدة بكل جلاء ووضوح وفي ضوء النور الداخلي الساطع الذي ينطلق من الداخل الى الخارج ومن الذهن الى الاشياء هي موجودة قبل التجربة على هيئة امكان وان التجربة ضرورية لتحصيل هذه الافكار. ان العقل يعاين الفكرة ولكن الارادة هي التي تقرر حقيقة هذه الفكرة والعلم هو في جوهره اقرار الارادة بالافكار الضرورية والسرمدية التي جاءت الى العقل من الله
٢ المحسوسات : كالصوت والرائحة والضوء والطعم والحرارة واللون
٣ الصور : وهي التصورات التي تخلقها القوة المتخيلة في الذهن كتصور رجل برأسين وفرس بجناحين
The theory of innate ideas. Its pioneer is the French philosopher René Descartes, and its purport is that there are two sources of ideas: one is sensation, for we conceive heat, light, taste, and sound due to our sensation of them. The other is innate nature (fitra), for the human mind possesses meanings and conceptions that do not originate from sense but rather are fixed in the core of innate nature. I will consider that the sky, air, earth, colors, shapes, sounds, and all external things are only illusions or dreams. And I will consider myself without hands, eyes, flesh, blood, and possessing no senses, and that I am deceived. But my being deceived confirms that I exist. And this is a certain truth - I think, therefore I am. I find the idea of God in my mind, so it must have a source, and I cannot be its source. This mental phenomenon, due to the perfection it possesses, precludes the mind from being its source; rather, its cause must be more perfect than the soul and mind. And this idea is innate, and every innate idea has no source except God, and likewise every self-evident truth (badihiyyat), and every idea that ends with God is a true idea. For if it were not true, then God's providing it to man would be deception and falsehood, and that is impossible for the perfect absolute God. Hence, every innate idea in human nature is a true idea cast from God, and it is correct and true knowledge. Human ideas in this theory are divided into three types:
1. Innate ideas: those that man perceives intuitively (badaha) and error has no way in, such as the idea of God, mathematical ideas, motion, shape, extension, ignorance, certainty, doubt, and the innate matters I mean are the original primitive information by which we arrive at all knowledge. For eternal ideas exist in us by innate nature, as man is born supplied with them from birth. He perceives them all at once with complete clarity and distinctness, in light of the bright inner light that proceeds from inside to outside and from the mind to things. They exist prior to experience in the form of potentiality, and experience is necessary for the acquisition of these ideas. The intellect contemplates the idea, but the will is what determines the truth of this idea. Knowledge is in its essence the affirmation by the will of the necessary and eternal ideas that have come to the intellect from God.
2. Sensations: such as sound, smell, light, taste, heat, and color.
3. Images: they are the conceptions that the imaginative faculty creates in the mind, such as the conception of a man with two heads or a horse with wings.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a modern exposition of Descartes' theory of innate ideas, presented in Arabic. It begins by stating that there are two sources of ideas: sensation (e.g., heat, light, taste) and innate nature (fitra), which contains meanings not derived from the senses. The author then adopts Descartes' method of doubt: he considers all external things as illusions and even doubts his own body and senses. From this doubt, he discovers the certainty of his own existence through the cogito ergo sum ("I think, therefore I am"). Next, he argues for the existence of God: the idea of God in the mind is perfect, and since the imperfect mind cannot be its source, it must come from a perfect being—God. This leads to the conclusion that all innate ideas, including mathematical truths and self-evident propositions, are true because God, who is perfect, would not deceive us. Finally, human ideas are classified into three types: (1) innate ideas, known intuitively and free from error; (2) sensations, derived from external senses; and (3) images, created by the imagination (e.g., a man with two heads). The text emphasizes that innate ideas are potential before experience and require experience to be actualized, and that the will plays a role in affirming their truth. Throughout, the explanation follows a rationalist epistemology rooted in divine guarantee.

Key Terms

البداهة
الافكار الفطرية
المحسوسات
الصور

Key Takeaway

The Flaw in the Law of Association of Ideas
Critique of the law of association of ideas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the law of association of ideas fails as an account of knowledge by citing its self-undermining effect.

Core Idea
If applied fully, associationism dissolves the very laws it relies on.

This passage opens with a powerful critique of the law of association of ideas, arguing that it cannot explain the source or mechanism of knowledge. The author warns that applying this law would destroy all rational, scientific, logical, and philosophical laws — including its own foundation. Let us unpack this criticism and see what it reveals about the requirements for a sound theory of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target theory
Locate the term 'law of association of ideas' (قانون تداعي المعاني) in the Arabic. What is this theory claiming about knowledge?
This is a modern psychological theory, not classical mantiq.
2
Extract the author's objection
Read the phrase 'ذوبان جميع القوانين العقلية الأولية والعلمية والمنطقية والفلسفية' — dissolution of all primary rational, scientific, logical, and philosophical laws. What does this mean for the status of the law of association itself?
3
Analyze the self-undermining argument
If the law of association dissolves all rational laws, then it also dissolves itself, because it is itself a claim about how the mind works. This makes the law self-defeating. Can you explain why?
4
Link to Islamic epistemology
Consider how Islamic scholars like al-Ghazali or Ibn Taymiyya used similar self-refutation arguments against philosophical or rationalist claims. For example, Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' first principles.
This is an expansion probe: the text uses this critique to motivate Aristotle's alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان قانون تداعي المعاني لا يفسر مصدر المعرفة ولا آليتها ولو طبقنا هذا القانون فمعناه ذوبان جميع القوانين العقلية الاولية والعلمية والمنطقية والفلسفية بوجه يمنعها من ان تتخذ أي صفة لها ومنها نظرية تداعي المعاني
۱۷ - نظرية القوة الكامنة
رائدها ( الفيلسوف اليوناني الكبير ارسطو طاليس ٣٨٤) - ٣٢٢ ق م) الذي وصفت مؤلفاته بأنها نهر ذهبي يفيض بالبلاغة واذا صحت مقولة ان القديم في الفلسفة لا يعتق لمجرد انه قديم فاننا نعد هذه النظرية في المعرفة من احدث النظريات لاصالتها وعمقها على ما فيها من الثغرات والنظرة الاحادية العقلية للامور التي سنأتي على ذكرها تباعا في الصفحات الآتية . لقد احتلت نظرية ارسطو موقعا مهما عند الفلاسفة الاوربيين في العصور الوسطى وعند الفلاسفة المسلمين ولاسيما ابن رشد . ولا يمكن ان نعطي هذه النظرية حقها في بحث مقتضب كهذا فهي تحتاج الى كتاب كامل ولكن وانسجاما مع الخط العام لهذه الدراسة سنكتفي بعرض العناصر المركزية فيها . تقول نظرية القوة
الكامنة
۱ هو اعظم فيلسوف في تاريخ البشرية على الاطلاق جمع كل فروع المعرفة الانسانية في زمانه. امتاز بدقة المنهج واستقامة البراهين والاستناد الى التجربة الواقعية وهو واضع علم المنطق ومن هنا لقلب بالمعلم . تعاطى صناعة الطب . ولد في اسطاغيرا من مقدونيا في بلاد اليونان ثم شخص الى أثينا في عصر ازدهار الفلسفة والتحق باستاذه افلاطون نحو عشرين سنة ثم اعتزله . له كتب في المنطق والطبيعة والميتافيزيق والاخلاق والشعر . لقد كان ارسطو رجل العقل والحس معا . يعلم مدى قوة تفكيره واستعماله العقل واستخدامه الحس في المعرفة من ملاحظة موضوعات كتبه . فإنها تنقسم على الاقسام الآتية -۱ الكتب المنطقية -٢ الكتب الطبيعية ٣ - الكتب الميتافيزيقية ٤- الكتب الاخلاقية -٥- الكتب الشعرية فكل هذا يكشف عن كونه رجلا منهجيا معترفا بالحس والعقل، الموسوعة الفلسفية، بدوي، مصدر سابق
The law of association of ideas does not explain the source of knowledge nor its mechanism. If we apply this law, it means the dissolution of all primary rational, scientific, logical, and philosophical laws in such a way that prevents them from taking any quality, including the theory of association of ideas itself. 17 - Theory of Potentiality: Its pioneer is the great Greek philosopher Aristotle (384-322 BC), whose works have been described as a golden river overflowing with eloquence. If the saying that the old in philosophy is not believed merely because it is old is true, then we consider this theory of knowledge to be among the newest theories due to its originality and depth, despite the gaps and one-sided rational view of things that we will mention successively in the following pages. Aristotle's theory occupied an important position among European philosophers in the Middle Ages and among Muslim philosophers, especially Ibn Rushd. We cannot give this theory its due in such a brief discussion; it requires a complete book. But in harmony with the general line of this study, we will suffice with presenting its central elements. The theory of potentiality says: 1 He is the greatest philosopher in the history of humanity absolutely, gathering all branches of human knowledge of his time. He was distinguished by precision of method, soundness of proofs, and reliance on empirical experience. He is the founder of logic, hence called the Teacher. He practiced medicine. He was born in Stagira in Macedonia in Greece, then went to Athens in the age of flourishing philosophy and joined his teacher Plato for about twenty years, then left him. He has books on logic, physics, metaphysics, ethics, and poetry. Aristotle was a man of reason and sense together. One knows the extent of his thinking and his use of reason and sense in knowledge from observing the subjects of his books. They are divided into the following sections: 1- Logical books, 2- Physical books, 3- Metaphysical books, 4- Ethical books, 5- Poetic books. All this reveals that he was a systematic man acknowledging sense and reason. Philosophical Encyclopedia, Badawi, previous source.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage begins by rejecting the law of association of ideas (قانون تداعي المعاني) as an adequate explanation for the source and mechanism of knowledge. The author argues that if fully applied, this law would dissolve all primary rational, scientific, logical, and philosophical laws, rendering them unable to have any property—including the theory itself. This sets up the need for an alternative. The author then introduces Aristotle's theory of potentiality (نظرية القوة الكامنة) as a superior account. He notes that Aristotle is the pioneer, a philosopher of unparalleled stature whose works are like a golden river. Despite any gaps and a one-sided rational perspective, the theory is considered original and profound. The author remarks that Aristotle's theory was influential in both medieval Europe and the Islamic world, especially with Ibn Rushd. Because space is limited, only the central elements will be presented. A long footnote provides a biographical sketch of Aristotle, emphasizing his systematic method, his role as the founder of logic, his empirical orientation, and his classification of works into logical, physical, metaphysical, ethical, and poetic categories. This footnote underscores Aristotle's dual reliance on reason and sense, which is reflected in his theory of knowledge.

Key Terms
قانون تداعي المعاني
قوانين عقلية أولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It would explain the source of knowledge perfectly.
B) It would dissolve all primary rational, scientific, logical, and philosophical laws.
C) It would lead to a theory of potentiality.
D) It would confirm Aristotle's theory.

Key Takeaway
The law of association of ideas is self-defeating because it would dissolve the rational laws that give it any claim to truth.

Aristotle's Theory of Potentiality as an Epistemological Foundation
Introduction of Aristotle's theory of potentiality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the core claim of Aristotle's theory of potentiality and its significance for Islamic philosophy.

Core Idea
Knowledge exists potentially in the mind; experience actualizes it.

After rejecting associationism, the author turns to Aristotle's theory of potentiality (نظرية القوة الكامنة). This theory holds that knowledge and other actualities exist in a latent state (quwwa) and are brought to actuality (fi'l) through a process. It was influential among Muslim philosophers like Ibn Rushd. Let us examine its central elements as presented in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Find the name of the theory
Locate the term 'نظرية القوة الكامنة' in the Arabic. This is Aristotle's concept of potentiality (dynamis).
In Islamic philosophy, al-quwwa corresponds to potentiality.
2
Identify the key proponent
The passage identifies Aristotle as the pioneer and adds a biographical footnote. Note his method: reliance on empirical experience (التجربة الواقعية) and reason.
3
Extract the theory's core claim
Though the passage does not give a detailed definition, it states that the theory says knowledge potentially exists in the mind. The author promises to present central elements. Based on the footnote, Aristotle combined sense and reason.
4
Connect to Islamic philosophers
The passage mentions Ibn Rushd as a key Muslim thinker who used this theory. Recall that Ibn Rushd wrote commentaries on Aristotle and defended the potentiality doctrine against al-Ghazali.

ان قانون تداعي المعاني لا يفسر مصدر المعرفة ولا آليتها ولو طبقنا هذا القانون فمعناه ذوبان جميع القوانين العقلية الاولية والعلمية والمنطقية والفلسفية بوجه يمنعها من ان تتخذ أي صفة لها ومنها نظرية تداعي المعاني
۱۷ - نظرية القوة الكامنة
رائدها ( الفيلسوف اليوناني الكبير ارسطو طاليس ٣٨٤) - ٣٢٢ ق م) الذي وصفت مؤلفاته بأنها نهر ذهبي يفيض بالبلاغة واذا صحت مقولة ان القديم في الفلسفة لا يعتق لمجرد انه قديم فاننا نعد هذه النظرية في المعرفة من احدث النظريات لاصالتها وعمقها على ما فيها من الثغرات والنظرة الاحادية العقلية للامور التي سنأتي على ذكرها تباعا في الصفحات الآتية . لقد احتلت نظرية ارسطو موقعا مهما عند الفلاسفة الاوربيين في العصور الوسطى وعند الفلاسفة المسلمين ولاسيما ابن رشد . ولا يمكن ان نعطي هذه النظرية حقها في بحث مقتضب كهذا فهي تحتاج الى كتاب كامل ولكن وانسجاما مع الخط العام لهذه الدراسة سنكتفي بعرض العناصر المركزية فيها . تقول نظرية القوة
الكامنة
۱ هو اعظم فيلسوف في تاريخ البشرية على الاطلاق جمع كل فروع المعرفة الانسانية في زمانه. امتاز بدقة المنهج واستقامة البراهين والاستناد الى التجربة الواقعية وهو واضع علم المنطق ومن هنا لقلب بالمعلم . تعاطى صناعة الطب . ولد في اسطاغيرا من مقدونيا في بلاد اليونان ثم شخص الى أثينا في عصر ازدهار الفلسفة والتحق باستاذه افلاطون نحو عشرين سنة ثم اعتزله . له كتب في المنطق والطبيعة والميتافيزيق والاخلاق والشعر . لقد كان ارسطو رجل العقل والحس معا . يعلم مدى قوة تفكيره واستعماله العقل واستخدامه الحس في المعرفة من ملاحظة موضوعات كتبه . فإنها تنقسم على الاقسام الآتية -۱ الكتب المنطقية -٢ الكتب الطبيعية ٣ - الكتب الميتافيزيقية ٤- الكتب الاخلاقية -٥- الكتب الشعرية فكل هذا يكشف عن كونه رجلا منهجيا معترفا بالحس والعقل، الموسوعة الفلسفية، بدوي، مصدر سابق
The law of association of ideas does not explain the source of knowledge nor its mechanism. If we apply this law, it means the dissolution of all primary rational, scientific, logical, and philosophical laws in such a way that prevents them from taking any quality, including the theory of association of ideas itself. 17 - Theory of Potentiality: Its pioneer is the great Greek philosopher Aristotle (384-322 BC), whose works have been described as a golden river overflowing with eloquence. If the saying that the old in philosophy is not believed merely because it is old is true, then we consider this theory of knowledge to be among the newest theories due to its originality and depth, despite the gaps and one-sided rational view of things that we will mention successively in the following pages. Aristotle's theory occupied an important position among European philosophers in the Middle Ages and among Muslim philosophers, especially Ibn Rushd. We cannot give this theory its due in such a brief discussion; it requires a complete book. But in harmony with the general line of this study, we will suffice with presenting its central elements. The theory of potentiality says: 1 He is the greatest philosopher in the history of humanity absolutely, gathering all branches of human knowledge of his time. He was distinguished by precision of method, soundness of proofs, and reliance on empirical experience. He is the founder of logic, hence called the Teacher. He practiced medicine. He was born in Stagira in Macedonia in Greece, then went to Athens in the age of flourishing philosophy and joined his teacher Plato for about twenty years, then left him. He has books on logic, physics, metaphysics, ethics, and poetry. Aristotle was a man of reason and sense together. One knows the extent of his thinking and his use of reason and sense in knowledge from observing the subjects of his books. They are divided into the following sections: 1- Logical books, 2- Physical books, 3- Metaphysical books, 4- Ethical books, 5- Poetic books. All this reveals that he was a systematic man acknowledging sense and reason. Philosophical Encyclopedia, Badawi, previous source.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage begins by rejecting the law of association of ideas (قانون تداعي المعاني) as an adequate explanation for the source and mechanism of knowledge. The author argues that if fully applied, this law would dissolve all primary rational, scientific, logical, and philosophical laws, rendering them unable to have any property—including the theory itself. This sets up the need for an alternative. The author then introduces Aristotle's theory of potentiality (نظرية القوة الكامنة) as a superior account. He notes that Aristotle is the pioneer, a philosopher of unparalleled stature whose works are like a golden river. Despite any gaps and a one-sided rational perspective, the theory is considered original and profound. The author remarks that Aristotle's theory was influential in both medieval Europe and the Islamic world, especially with Ibn Rushd. Because space is limited, only the central elements will be presented. A long footnote provides a biographical sketch of Aristotle, emphasizing his systematic method, his role as the founder of logic, his empirical orientation, and his classification of works into logical, physical, metaphysical, ethical, and poetic categories. This footnote underscores Aristotle's dual reliance on reason and sense, which is reflected in his theory of knowledge.

Key Terms
نظرية القوة الكامنة
التجربة الواقعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Muslim philosopher is especially associated with Aristotle's theory of potentiality, according to the passage?
A) Al-Ghazali
B) Ibn Sina
C) Ibn Rushd
D) Al-Farabi

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's theory of potentiality provides an account of knowledge that preserves both reason and empirical experience, and it was vital for later Islamic philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain how the concept of potentiality (quwwa) applies to the process of memorizing the Quran. According to this theory, the Quran initially exists potentially in the heart and is actualized through repetition and understanding.
?
Compare Aristotle's theory of potentiality with the Islamic Ash'ari theory of acquisition (iktisab). How do they differ on the role of human agency in knowledge?

Innate Curiosity and the Seed of Knowledge
Author explains Aristotle's theory of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of innate potentiality for knowledge as presented in Aristotle's analogy.

Core Idea
Knowledge is latent in the human mind like a tree in a seed.

This lesson unpacks Aristotle's claim that the love of inquiry is innate and exists in potential. He uses a powerful analogy: just as a tree exists potentially in a seed, so does our desire for knowledge exist in the mind, driving a progressive movement toward actualization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find the sentence stating that the love of inquiry is innate (فطري كامن). What does innate mean here?
2
Examine the seed analogy
Notice the comparison: الشجرة بالقوة في البذرة. What does this tell you about the nature of knowledge acquisition?
The seed is a potential tree; the mind holds potential knowledge.
3
Understand the movement
The text says we progress toward what is better. What drives this movement? How is pleasure involved?
Pleasure combines cognitive and motive aspects.
4
Connect to Islamic philosophy
Consider how this idea of potential intellect (العقل الهيولاني) appears in Ibn Sina's psychology. How is the seed analogy similar?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن حب الاستطلاع شيء فطري كامن فينا . فالمعرفة شيء نشتهيه بالفطرة ( ۱ ) فالناس بطبيعتهم يرغبون في المعرفة والدليل هو تلك اللذة التي تنشأ عن المعلومات الحسية التي نحصل عليها ولاسيما حس البصر لانه الاقدر على جعلنا نحصل على قدر اكبر من المعرفة ) ) (ومثلما توجد الشجرة بالقوة في البذرة فان حب الاستطلاع موجود بالقوة في ذهن الانسان وان ما يأتي به الغد خير مما لدينا اليوم وما لدينا اليوم هو خير مما بالامس اننا سائرون سيرا تقدميا الى الامام هكذا هو الكون يرتقي الى ما هو احسن وكذلك انفسنا ومعارفنا انها حركة دائمة غايتها الارتقاء من خلال الاستزادة ومحركها اللذة التي تجمع الناحية الادراكية والناحية التحريكية فعل وهي تحقيق لفعل وهي صفة مستمرة خارجة عن الزمان اساسها الميل او الشهوة او الرغبة في تحقيق الفعل فاذا ما تحقق الفعل المعرفي انتج ذلك لذة وإذا لم يتفق مع غاية الطبيعة انتج ذلك الما) (ان) مصدر كل معرفة هو التجربة الحسية وان الاحساسات تستمر حتى تصبح عادة بعدها تتحول الى مفهوم عام أو تصور و ان هناك مباديء اولية وقوانين فكرية ثابتة في الذهن هي عبارة عن جوهر يوضح لنا الكمية والكيفية والزمان والمكان والاجناس والانواع يمكن ارجاعها الى الميتافيزيق نفهم من خلالها صفات الاشياء السالبة والموجبة) ° (أما الفكر فله قسمان هما العقل المنفعل الذي يستقبل الصور الحسية والعقل الفعال الذي
۱ تاريخ الفلسفة اليونانية، يوسف كرم، مصدر سابق ص ۲۲۰ الموسوعة الفلسفية، عبدالرحمن بدوي، مصدر سابق ج ۱ ص ۱۲۱
المصدر نفسه
ع المصدر نفسه ص ۱۲۰ ٤
ه تاريخ الفلسفة اليونانية، يوسف كرم، مصدر سابق ص ۲۲۰
That the love of inquiry is something innate latent in us. For knowledge is something we desire by nature (1). For people by nature desire knowledge, and the proof is that pleasure that arises from sensory information we obtain, especially the sense of sight because it is most capable of making us obtain a greater amount of knowledge. (And just as the tree exists potentially in the seed, so love of inquiry exists potentially in the human mind, and that what tomorrow brings is better than what we have today, and what we have today is better than what we had yesterday. We are proceeding progressively forward. Thus the universe rises to what is better, and likewise our souls and our knowledge. They are a perpetual movement whose goal is progress through increase, and whose mover is pleasure that combines the cognitive and the motive aspect. It is act and actualization of act, and it is a continuous quality outside time, based on inclination or desire or wish to actualize the act. So when the cognitive act is actualized, it produces pleasure, and if it does not agree with the purpose of nature, it produces pain.) (That) the source of all knowledge is sensory experience, and sensations continue until they become habit, then they transform into a universal concept or conception. And that there are primary principles and fixed intellectual laws in the mind, which are a substance that explains to us quantity, quality, time, place, genera, and species, which can be traced back to metaphysics. Through them we understand the positive and negative attributes of things. (As for thought, it has two parts: the passive intellect which receives sensory forms, and the active intellect which [continues in footnotes] 1 History of Greek Philosophy, Yusuf Karam, previous source, p. 220; Philosophical Encyclopedia, Abd al-Rahman Badawi, previous source, vol. 1, p. 121; same source; same source p. 120; History of Greek Philosophy, previous source, p. 220.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents central elements of Aristotle's theory of knowledge. It begins by stating that the love of inquiry (حب الاستطلاع) is innate (فطري) and that knowledge is desired by nature. The proof is the pleasure derived from sensory information, especially sight, which yields the most knowledge. The author then uses an analogy: just as a tree exists potentially in a seed, so does the love of inquiry exist potentially in the human mind. This potentiality drives a progressive movement toward a better future, both for the universe and for human souls and knowledge. The movement is perpetual, aiming at progress through increase, and is motivated by pleasure—which combines cognitive and motive aspects. This pleasure is conceptualized as an act (فعل) and a realization of act, a continuous quality outside time, based on inclination or desire. When the cognitive act is realized, it produces pleasure; if it contradicts nature's purpose, it produces pain. The author then asserts that the source of all knowledge is sensory experience (التجربة الحسية). Sensations, through repetition, become habit, and then transform into a universal concept (مفهوم عام) or conception (تصور). There are primary principles and fixed intellectual laws in the mind, which are a substance (جوهر) that explains quantity, quality, time, place, genera, and species—traceable to metaphysics, allowing understanding of positive and negative attributes. Finally, thought is divided into two parts: the passive intellect (العقل المنفعل) which receives sensory forms, and the active intellect (العقل الفعال) which presumably actualizes them (the text cuts off). This distinction is central to Aristotle's noetics and was influential in Islamic philosophy, especially through Ibn Rushd's interpretation. The footnotes cite sources in Greek philosophy.

Key Terms
حب الاستطلاع
قوة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the seed analogy illustrate?
A) That knowledge is acquired through experience alone
B) That the love of inquiry exists potentially in the human mind
C) That the universe is perfect
D) That the passive intellect receives forms

Key Takeaway
The desire for knowledge is innate and exists in potential, like a tree in a seed; it drives a progressive actualization through pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the seed analogy, explain how the ruling on a new case exists potentially in usul al-fiqh before it is derived. (Hint: consider the concept of qiyas and how the 'illah is discovered.)

From Sensations to Universals: Sensory Experience as Source of Knowledge
Author explains Aristotle's theory of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the process by which sensory experience generates universal concepts.

Core Idea
All knowledge begins with the senses; universals are habits formed from repeated sensations.

This lesson focuses on Aristotle's claim that sensory experience is the source of all knowledge. The passage explains how repeated sensations become habit, then transform into universal concepts. It also mentions fixed intellectual laws that help us understand attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source
Find the sentence that states the source of all knowledge (مصدر كل معرفة هو التجربة الحسية). What is being asserted?
This is an empiricist claim.
2
Follow the transformation
How do sensations become universals? Trace the steps: sensations → عادة (habit) → مفهوم عام (universal concept). Explain each step.
3
Note the fixed principles
The passage mentions primary principles and fixed intellectual laws. What are they? How do they relate to universals?
These are categories like quantity, quality, etc.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In usul al-fiqh, continuous observation of a phenomenon (tajriba) leads to a general rule. For instance, observing that all intoxicants lead to intoxication leads to the universal 'all intoxicants are forbidden.' How does this parallel Aristotle's process?

The Two Intellects: Passive and Active in Aristotle's Noetics
Author explains Aristotle's theory of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the passive and active intellect as described in the passage.

Core Idea
The passive intellect receives; the active intellect actualizes – together they make knowledge possible.

The passage concludes with a brief mention of the division of thought into two intellects: the passive (العقل المنفعل) which receives sensory forms, and the active (العقل الفعال) which presumably actualizes them. This distinction was central to later Islamic philosophy, especially in Ibn Rushd's interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Find the two intellects
Locate the sentence: 'الفكر فله قسمان هما العقل المنفعل الذي يستقبل الصور الحسية والعقل الفعال الذي'. What is the function of each?
The active intellect is cut off, but it is traditionally the agent that actualizes knowledge.
2
Understand passive intellect
The passive intellect receives sensory forms. What does this suggest about its role? Is it active or receptive?
It is like a wax tablet receiving impressions.
3
Infer the active intellect's role
Based on the unfinished sentence and the context, what do you think the active intellect does? How does it relate to the passive?
It actualizes the potential knowledge.
4
Connect to Islamic philosophy
In Islamic philosophy, Ibn Sina identified the active intellect (العقل الفعال) as a separate celestial intelligence that bestows forms. How does this differ from the passage's implication that it is part of the human mind?
This is a shift_alert from the source.

That the love of inquiry is something innate latent in us. For knowledge is something we desire by nature (1). For people by nature desire knowledge, and the proof is that pleasure that arises from sensory information we obtain, especially the sense of sight because it is most capable of making us obtain a greater amount of knowledge. (And just as the tree exists potentially in the seed, so love of inquiry exists potentially in the human mind, and that what tomorrow brings is better than what we have today, and what we have today is better than what we had yesterday. We are proceeding progressively forward. Thus the universe rises to what is better, and likewise our souls and our knowledge. They are a perpetual movement whose goal is progress through increase, and whose mover is pleasure that combines the cognitive and the motive aspect. It is act and actualization of act, and it is a continuous quality outside time, based on inclination or desire or wish to actualize the act. So when the cognitive act is actualized, it produces pleasure, and if it does not agree with the purpose of nature, it produces pain.) (That) the source of all knowledge is sensory experience, and sensations continue until they become habit, then they transform into a universal concept or conception. And that there are primary principles and fixed intellectual laws in the mind, which are a substance that explains to us quantity, quality, time, place, genera, and species, which can be traced back to metaphysics. Through them we understand the positive and negative attributes of things. (As for thought, it has two parts: the passive intellect which receives sensory forms, and the active intellect which [continues in footnotes] 1 History of Greek Philosophy, Yusuf Karam, previous source, p. 220; Philosophical Encyclopedia, Abd al-Rahman Badawi, previous source, vol. 1, p. 121; same source; same source p. 120; History of Greek Philosophy, previous source, p. 220.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents central elements of Aristotle's theory of knowledge. It begins by stating that the love of inquiry (حب الاستطلاع) is innate (فطري) and that knowledge is desired by nature. The proof is the pleasure derived from sensory information, especially sight, which yields the most knowledge. The author then uses an analogy: just as a tree exists potentially in a seed, so does the love of inquiry exist potentially in the human mind. This potentiality drives a progressive movement toward a better future, both for the universe and for human souls and knowledge. The movement is perpetual, aiming at progress through increase, and is motivated by pleasure—which combines cognitive and motive aspects. This pleasure is conceptualized as an act (فعل) and a realization of act, a continuous quality outside time, based on inclination or desire. When the cognitive act is realized, it produces pleasure; if it contradicts nature's purpose, it produces pain. The author then asserts that the source of all knowledge is sensory experience (التجربة الحسية). Sensations, through repetition, become habit, and then transform into a universal concept (مفهوم عام) or conception (تصور). There are primary principles and fixed intellectual laws in the mind, which are a substance (جوهر) that explains quantity, quality, time, place, genera, and species—traceable to metaphysics, allowing understanding of positive and negative attributes. Finally, thought is divided into two parts: the passive intellect (العقل المنفعل) which receives sensory forms, and the active intellect (العقل الفعال) which presumably actualizes them (the text cuts off). This distinction is central to Aristotle's noetics and was influential in Islamic philosophy, especially through Ibn Rushd's interpretation. The footnotes cite sources in Greek philosophy.

Key Terms
العقل المنفعل
العقل الفعال
صور حسية

Key Takeaway
The passive intellect receives sensory forms, while the active intellect actualizes them – a dual-process theory of knowledge.
