Defining Ma'rifa: Reflection on Effects
Author defines ma'rifa as perception of a thing by reflection on its effect, distinguishing it from ilm and inkar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ma'rifa in Quranic terms and distinguish it from 'ilm and inkar using linguistic and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Ma'rifa is inferential knowledge from signs, more specific than 'ilm and opposed to inkar.

This passage from a classical work on Islamic epistemology defines ma'rifa from a Quranic perspective. It is not a synonym for 'ilm; rather, it is a specific kind of perception that requires reflection on the effects of the thing known. Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting Quranic usage and for grasping how humans can know Allah without comprehending His essence.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of ma'rifa
Find the exact definition phrase: 'إدراك الشيء بتفكر وتدبر لأثره' (perception of a thing by reflection and deliberation on its effect). Identify the genus (إدراك) and differentia (بتفكر وتدبر لأثره).
The differentia emphasizes that ma'rifa is not immediate but requires active contemplation of effects.
2
Compare with 'ilm
The text states ma'rifa is 'أخص من العلم' (more specific than 'ilm). Note the linguistic example: one says 'يعرف الله' but not 'يعلم الله' with Allah as direct object, because humans know Allah through His effects, not His essence.
This shows ma'rifa involves indirect, inferential knowledge, whereas 'ilm can be direct or general.
3
Identify the opposite of ma'rifa
The text says ma'rifa is opposed by 'الإنكار' (denial), not 'الجهل' (ignorance) which is the opposite of 'ilm. Cite the Quranic verses: يعرفون نعمة الله ثم ينكرونها and فلمّا جاءهم ما عرفوا.
Denial can coexist with recognition (ma'rifa), unlike ignorance which is lack of knowledge.
4
Analyze the dual use of 'arif
The term 'arif has two meanings: (1) one specialized in knowledge of Allah and His kingdom, (2) one who knows people and makes them known (like a tribal chief). This is shown through poetry and linguistic usage.
This demonstrates that the root 'r.f. conveys recognition and familiarity, whether of God or of people.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معرفة النفس كما ورد عن الامام علي بن ابي طالب (ع) قوله من عرف نفسه فقد انتهى الى غاية كل معرفة وعلم والجاهل بمعرفة نفسه جاهل بكل شيء مصطلح المعرفة في المنظور القرآني المعرفة والعرفان إدراك الشيء بتفكر وتدبر لأثره وهو أخص من العلم ويضاده الانكار ويقال فلان يعرف الله ولا يقال يعلم الله متعديا الى مفعول واحد لأن معرفة البشر الله هي بتدبر آثاره دون إدراك ذاته قال تعالى ( فَلَمّا جَاءَهُمْ مَا عَرَفُوا ) فَعرفهم وهم لَهُ منكرون ويضاد المعرفة الانكار والعلم والجهل قال ( يَعْرِفُونَ نِعْمَةَ اللهِ ثُمَّ يَنكُرُونَهَا ) والعارف في تعارف قوم هو المختص بمعرفة الله ومعرفة ملكوته وحسن معاملته تعالى، والعريف من يعرف الناس ويعرفهم . قال الشاعر: بعثوا إلي عريفهم يتوسم وقد عرف فلان عرافة إذا صار مختصا بذلك . فالعريف السيد المعروف قال الشاعر بل كل قوم وإن عزوا وإن كثروا عريفهم بأثافي الشر مرجوم
Knowledge of the self, as reported from Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib (peace be upon him), his saying: 'Whoever knows his self has reached the utmost of every knowledge and science.' And 'The ignorant of knowledge of his self is ignorant of everything.' The term 'knowledge' in the Quranic perspective: 'al-ma'rifa' and 'al-'irfan' are perception of a thing by reflection and deliberation on its effect. It is more specific than 'al-'ilm' and is opposed by 'al-inkar'. It is said: 'so-and-so knows (ya'rifu) Allah' and it is not said: 'so-and-so knows (ya'lamu) Allah' transitive with one object, because human knowledge of Allah is by reflection on His effects without perceiving His essence. He, exalted, said: 'When there came to them what they recognized (ma 'arafu), they disbelieved in it.' So He recognized them while they were deniers of it. And 'ma'rifa' is opposed by 'inkar', 'ilm', and 'jahl'. He said: 'They recognize (ya'rifuna) the favor of Allah, then they deny (yankiruna) it.' And 'al-'arif' in the terminology of a people is the one specialized in knowledge of Allah and knowledge of His kingdom and good conduct with Him, exalted. And 'al-'arif' is the one who knows the people and makes them known. The poet said: 'They sent to me their 'arif, trying to discern.' And so-and-so became an 'arif' if he became specialized in that. So 'al-'arif' is the noble lord. The poet said: 'But every people, though mighty and numerous, their 'arif is pelted with the stones of evil.'
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage begins with a quote from Imam Ali (AS) emphasizing the importance of self-knowledge: knowing oneself is the ultimate goal of all knowledge. This sets the stage for defining 'ma'rifa' itself. The author then presents the lexical and technical definition of 'al-ma'rifa' and 'al-'irfan' from the Quranic perspective. He defines them as 'perception of a thing by reflection and deliberation on its effect' (idrak al-shay' bi tafakkur wa tadabbur li-atharihi). This is a crucial distinction: ma'rifa is not mere sensory perception but a reflective understanding that considers the effects or signs of the thing known. It is more specific (akhas) than 'ilm (general knowledge/science). Furthermore, it is opposed to 'inkar (denial). This is illustrated by the linguistic usage: one says 'ya'rifu Allah' (he knows Allah) but not 'ya'lamu Allah' with Allah as direct object, because humans can only know Allah through reflecting on His effects (athar), not by comprehending His essence (dhat). The author then cites Quranic verses to support the contrast between ma'rifa and inkar: 'When there came to them what they recognized (ma 'arafu), they disbelieved in it' (Quran 2:89) and 'They recognize (ya'rifuna) the favor of Allah, then they deny (yankiruna) it' (Quran 16:83). This shows that ma'rifa can coexist with denial, whereas 'ilm typically does not. The passage then explains the terms 'arif and 'arif. In the parlance of some people, 'al-'arif' is one specialized in knowledge of Allah and His kingdom and good conduct with Him. 'Al-'arif' is also the one who knows people and makes them known (like a tribal chief or genealogist). Poetry is cited to show the usage: 'They sent to me their 'arif, trying to discern.' Thus, the 'arif is a lord or knowledgeable person. The passage contrasts two types: the spiritual 'arif who knows Allah, and the social 'arif who knows people. This lexical analysis grounds the Quranic concept of ma'rifa.

Key Terms
المعرفة
العلم
الإنكار
العارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Ma'rifa is more specific (akhas) than 'ilm and involves reflection on effects
C) They are synonyms
D) 'Ilm is only about God, ma'rifa about creation

Key Takeaway
Ma'rifa is a specific type of knowledge gained through reflective inference from effects, narrower than 'ilm and opposed to inkar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a definition of 'iman (faith) in light of the distinction between ma'rifa and 'ilm. Is iman a type of ma'rifa or 'ilm? Use the criteria from this lesson: iman involves reflection on God's signs (Quran, creation) and is opposed to denial (kufr), not ignorance. Determine whether the definition of iman should include the differentia of reflective inference.
?

Why? Then apply the same analysis: what would be the genus? (demand) differentia? (from superior, regarding future action). Compare to the definition of ma'rifa.

Definition of the Tongue: Organ, Power, and Language
Author defines the tongue as an organ and its power of speech, citing Quranic verses and hadiths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the physical tongue (jariha) and its power of speech (quwwa), and identify the semantic range of 'lisan' in Quran and hadith.

Core Idea
The tongue is not just an organ — it is a faculty of speech and a source of language and accountability.

In this passage, the author lays out a definition of the tongue that goes beyond anatomy. He distinguishes the physical organ from its power (quwwa), which is speech. He then shows how the term 'lisan' extends to mean 'language' (lugha) in Quranic usage. The passage also includes moral exhortation from hadith about the tongue's role in faith and divine punishment. By parsing these definitions and sources, you will learn how classical scholars handled polysemy and how they tied linguistic precision to religious practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of 'lisan'
Find where the author defines the tongue as both organ (jariha) and power (quwwa). Notice the shift from physical to functional.
The first line says 'al-lisan: al-jariha; wa quwwatuha' — the organ and its strength.
2
Parse the Quranic evidence
Look at the verse 'loosen the knot from my tongue.' The author explains the knot is in the power, not the organ. What does this tell you about the distinction?
The knot is in the quwwa (speech power), showing the definition includes the faculty.
3
Recognize the third meaning: language
The passage says 'lisan' can mean 'lugha.' Cite the verse 'in a clear Arabic tongue.' How does this expand the definition?
Here 'lisan' refers to language, not the organ.
4
Connect to hadith
The Prophet links the tongue to submission of the heart (iman) and to punishment in Hell. How does this moral dimension relate to the definition?
The hadiths show the tongue's actions have serious consequences, highlighting its power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللسان ؛ الجارحة ؛ وقوتها وقوله ( واحلل عقدة من لساني) يعني به من قوة لسانه فان العقدة لم تكن في الجارحة وانما كانت في قوته التي هي النطق به ويقال لكل قوم لسان ولسن بكسر اللام أي لغة قال فإنما يسرناه بِلِسَانِكَ) (بلسان عربي مُبِينٍ)؛ قال تعالى (أَلَمْ نَجْعَلْ لَهُ عَيْنينِ . وَلَسَانًا وشفتين وورد في الحديث الشريف قول النبي الاكرم محمد (لايسلم عبد حتى يسلم قلبه ولسانه) كما ورد إن النبي ( ص ) قال مخاطبا معاذ بن جبل ( وهل يكب الناس على مناخرهم في النار إلا حصاد ألسنتهم)
The tongue: the organ, and its strength. And His saying: 'And loosen the knot from my tongue' means by it the strength of his tongue, for the knot was not in the organ but in its strength which is speech. And it is said for every people: lisan and lusun (with kasra of the lam) meaning language. He said: 'So We have made it easy in your tongue,' 'In a clear Arabic tongue.' He said: 'Have We not made for him two eyes, and a tongue and two lips?' And it is reported in the noble hadith the saying of the Noble Prophet Muhammad: 'No servant submits until his heart and tongue submit.' And also the Prophet (s) said addressing Mu'adh ibn Jabal: 'What casts people on their noses into the Fire except the harvest of their tongues?'
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the tongue (lisan) from two perspectives: as a physical organ (al-jariha) and as a faculty or power (quwwa). The author distinguishes between the organ itself and its power, which is speech (nutq). He cites the Qur'anic verse where Prophet Moses asks to loosen the knot from his tongue, explaining that the knot was in the power of speech, not the physical tongue. The term lisan can also refer to language (lugha), as in 'a clear Arabic tongue' (lisānun ‘arabiyyun mubīn). The author then lists Qur'anic blessings of eyes, tongue, and lips. Two prophetic hadiths conclude the passage: the first states that a servant's faith is incomplete until both heart and tongue submit; the second warns that the tongue is the main cause of people being cast into Hell. The passage thus combines definition, linguistic clarification, and moral exhortation.

The Tongue: Organ and Power of Speech
Author defines the tongue and its power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the tongue as an organ and its power (quwwa) of speech, and explain why the knot in Musa's tongue was a speech impediment.

Core Idea
The tongue's true power is speech, not taste.

In this passage, the author dissects the tongue into two components: the physical organ and its inherent power (quwwa) which is speech. He uses the Quranic verse of Prophet Musa to illustrate that the 'knot' was in the power, not the organ. This distinction is crucial for understanding how the tongue functions as an epistemological instrument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
Find the author's explicit statement that the tongue consists of the organ (al-jāriḥa) and its power (quwwa). The power is speech (al-nuṭq).
2
Analyze the Quranic example
Look at the verse 'And loosen the knot from my tongue' (Q. 20:27). The author argues the knot was not in the organ but in its power. Why? Because a physical knot would be visible, but the impediment was in speech ability.
3
Understand 'lisān' as language
The author notes that 'lisān' with a kasra on the lām means language. This shows the term shifts between the physical organ and the abstract linguistic system.
4
Synthesize the definition
Combine these observations: the tongue's essence is not just the flesh but the power of articulation that enables knowledge expression.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللسان الجارحة وقوتها وقوله ( واحلل عقدة من لساني) يعني به من قوة لسانه فان العقدة لم تكن في الجارحة وانما كانت في قوته التي هي النطق به ويقال لكل قوم لسان ولسن بكسر اللام أي لغة قال فإنما يسرناه بِلِسَانِكَ) (بلسان عربي مُبِينٍ)؛ قال تعالى (أَلَمْ نَجْعَلْ لَهُ عَيْنينِ . وَلَسَانًا وشفتين وورد في الحديث الشريف قول النبي الاكرم محمد (لايسلم عبد حتى يسلم قلبه ولسانه) كما ورد إن النبي ( ص ) قال مخاطبا معاذ بن جبل ( وهل يكب الناس على مناخرهم في النار إلا حصاد ألسنتهم) قال الشاعر ابن أبي سلمى : لسان الفتى نصف ونصف فؤاده فلم يبق إلا صورة اللحم والدم وقال الشافعي: لسانك لا تذكر به عورة امريء فكلك عورات وللناس ألسن وقال شاعر ليس القدود ولا البرود فضيلة ما المرء إلا قلبه ولسانه إن اللسان له دور حيوي في الحس ف ( الألسن تترجم عما تجنه الضمائر ) و ( ما الإنسان لو لا اللسان إلا صورة ممثلة أو بهيمة مهملة ) وقد كشفت الدراسات العلمية في السنوات الاخيرة إن اللسان مقسم على اجزاء عديدة تتوزع اختصاصاتها بالحلاوة والحموضة والملوحة ... لقد وردت في الشريعة الاسلامية الغراء نصوص عديدة بخصوص أهمية اللسان فهو لا يمثل حاسة للتذوق وحسب بل هو احد الادوات الاساسية لاخراج الكلام أي تجسيد القرار المعرفي. قال النبي الاكرم محمد عليه افضل الصلاة والسلام ) إذا أصبح ابن آدم اصبحت الاعضاء كلها تستكفي اللسان أي تقول : اتق الله فينا فإنك ان استقمت استقمنا وان اعوججت اعوججنا ( يعذب الله اللسان بعذاب لا يعذب به شيئا من الجوارح فيقول : أي رب عذبتني بعذاب لم تعذب به شيئا من الجوارح : فيقال له خرجت منك كلمة فبلغت مشارق الارض ومغاربها فسفك بها الدم الحرام وانتهب به المال الحرام وانتهك به
The tongue: the organ and its power. And His saying 'And loosen the knot from my tongue' means by the power of his tongue, for the knot was not in the organ but in its power which is speech. And it is said for every people a tongue (lisān) and a language (lisan, with kasra of lām), meaning language. He said 'We have made it easy by your tongue' (Quran 19:97) and 'in a clear Arabic tongue' (Quran 26:195). He said 'Have We not made for him two eyes, a tongue, and two lips?' (Quran 90:8-9). And it is reported in the noble hadith the saying of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him): 'A servant does not become a believer until his heart and tongue are safe.' Also it is reported that the Prophet (s) said addressing Muadh ibn Jabal: 'Nothing casts people on their faces in the Fire except the harvest of their tongues.' The poet Ibn Abi Salma said: A young man's tongue is half, half his heart, and nothing remains except the image of flesh and blood. Al-Shafi'i said: Do not mention the fault of a person with your tongue, for you yourself have faults and people have tongues. Another poet said: Neither tallness nor garments are virtues; a person is only his heart and tongue. The tongue has a vital role in sensation, for 'tongues translate what consciences conceal' and 'Man without the tongue is nothing but an image or a neglected beast.' Scientific studies in recent years have revealed that the tongue is divided into many parts whose specializations are distributed between sweetness, acidity, and saltiness... Numerous texts have been reported in the noble Islamic law regarding the importance of the tongue, as it not only represents a sense of taste but is one of the basic tools for producing speech, i.e., embodying the cognitive decision. The Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) said: 'When the son of Adam wakes up, all his limbs reproach the tongue, saying: Fear Allah concerning us, for if you are straight we are straight, and if you are crooked we are crooked.' 'Allah will punish the tongue with a punishment with which He does not punish any of the limbs. It will say: O Lord, You have punished me with a punishment with which You have not punished any of the limbs. It will be said to him: A word came out of you that reached the east and west of the earth, and thereby unlawful blood was shed, unlawful property was plundered, and unlawful honor was violated.'
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a multi-faceted exposition on the tongue (lisān). The author begins by distinguishing between the organ and its power, using the Quranic verse 'loosen the knot from my tongue' (Quran 20:27) to show that the knot was a speech impediment, not a physical defect. The term 'lisān' also means language. He then cites several Quranic verses and hadith emphasizing the tongue's importance in faith and speech. Poetry from Ibn Abi Salma and al-Shafi'i reinforces the dual nature of the tongue as both a potential source of virtue and vice. The author then turns to modern science, mentioning the tongue's taste zones, but quickly returns to an Islamic perspective, citing hadith that the tongue's straightness affects all limbs and that it can cause severe punishment due to speech that leads to bloodshed and violation of rights. The passage emphasizes the epistemological aspect: the tongue is essential for expressing knowledge (al-qarār al-maʿrifī).

الحاسة

A) Because the organ was physically fine.

Key Takeaway
The tongue is not just an organ; its essential power is speech (nuṭq), making it a tool for knowledge expression.

The Tongue and Knowledge: Its Power to Guide or Destroy
Author discusses the tongue's ethical and epistemological role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain, using hadith evidence from the passage, how the tongue can either uphold faith or lead to destruction in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
The tongue reveals the cognitive decision of the heart and can cause grave punishment.

After defining the tongue's power, the author delves into its ethical and epistemological role. He cites a series of hadiths showing that the tongue is the measure of faith and that its misuse leads to the worst punishments. The tongue is not merely a taste organ but the primary tool for expressing knowledge, and with that comes responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Extract the hadith on faith
Find the hadith: 'A servant does not become a believer until his heart and tongue are safe.' This links the tongue's safety to the soundness of faith.
2
Analyze the Muadh hadith
The Prophet said: 'Nothing casts people on their faces in the Fire except the harvest of their tongues.' Reflect on what 'harvest' implies—the accumulated results of speech.
3
Interpret the limbs' reproach
In the hadith, all limbs ask the tongue to fear Allah, declaring that if the tongue is straight, they are straight. This shows the tongue's central influence over the body's actions.
4
Connect to punishment
The tongue will be punished uniquely because a single word can cause widespread harm (bloodshed, property violation). This emphasizes the gravity of speech.
5
Summarize the epistemological role
The author states the tongue is essential for 'embodying the cognitive decision.' How do these hadiths support that the tongue translates inner knowledge into outward action?

Natiq and Samit in the Quran
Author defines natiq and samit with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which entities are natiq and which are samit according to the Quranic perspective given in the passage, and explain the relativity of these terms.

Core Idea
In the Quran, natiq (speaking) is relative to understanding – anything that conveys meaning is natiq to the one who understands.

This lesson unpacks the Quranic definitions of 'speaking' (nāṭiq) and 'silent' (ṣāmit). The author challenges the classical logical association of nāṭiq exclusively with rational humans, showing through Quranic examples that any sign or sound that conveys meaning is 'speaking' to its interpreter, while even a vocal sound can be 'silent' to the one who does not comprehend. Understanding this relativity sharpens our interpretation of divine communication in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate 'al-nāṭiq' and 'al-ṣāmit' in the Arabic passage. The author begins by asking 'What is the speaking and the silent?' and then defines them.
2
Extract the initial definitions
The author first says: 'the informative indications and the admonishing lessons.' Note that these are broad categories – not just vocal speech.
3
Analyze the Quranic examples for natiq
The passage gives three examples: (1) idols that 'do not speak' (Q 21:65) – they lack intellect, so they are not natiq in the human sense. (2) Bird voices called 'speech' (Q 27:16) because Solomon understood them – here speech is extended to non-human sounds. (3) The book 'speaks' (Q 45:29) – its speech is perceived by the eye, not the ear.
Notice the shift: speech includes any conveyance of meaning, not just vocalization.
4
Understand the relativity
The author explicitly states: 'Whoever understands a meaning from something, then that thing is speaking relative to him even if it is silent. And relative to the one who does not understand from it, it is silent even if it speaks.' This is the core insight – natiq and samit are observer-relative.
This contrasts with classical logic where natiq is an intrinsic property of humans.
5
Recognize the Quranic essence of speech
The final Quranic citation (Q 41:21) shows that Allah makes everything speak. Speech is a divine honor for humans to manifest what reason determines, using the tongue as a tool. But the essence is the expression of meaning, not the physical instrument.
Thus, anything that communicates meaning can be considered natiq in the Quranic worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما الناطق الصامت ؟ فقال : الدلائل المخبرة والعبر الواعظة . وقوله (لَقَدْ عَلَمْتَ مَا هَؤُلَاءِ يَنطِقُونَ إشارة الى انهم ليسوا من جنس الناطقين ذوي العقول . وسميت اصوات الطير نطقا اعتبارا بسليمان الذي كان يفهمه (علمنا منطقَ الطَّيْرِ ) فمن فهم من شيء معنى فذلك الشيء بالاضافة اليه ناطق وان كان صامتا . وبالاضافة الى من لايفهم عنه صامت وان كان ناطقا وقوله (هذا كِتَابَنَا يَنطِقُ عَلَيْكُم بِالْحَقِّ) فان الكتاب ناطق لكن نطقه ندركه بالعين كما ان الكلام كتاب لكن يدركه السمع وقوله (وَقَالُوا لِجُلُودِهِمْ لِمَ شَهِدتُمْ عَلَيْنَا قَالُوا أَنْطَقَنَا اللَّهُ الَّذِي أنْطَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ ( ٤٤ ان حقيقة النطق في منظور القران الكريم هي قوله تعالى ( أنطقنا الله الذي أنطق كل شيء ) فهو كرامة ربانية لبني البشر لاظهار ما يقرره العقل ويكون وضع اللسان كأداة تنفيذية ليس غيره
What is the speaking and the silent? He said: the informative indications and the admonishing lessons. And His statement: 'Indeed you know that these do not speak' is an indication that they are not of the speaking people who have intellect. And the voices of birds were called 'speech' considering Solomon who understood them: 'We have been taught the speech of birds.' So whoever understands a meaning from something, then that thing is speaking relative to him even if it is silent. And relative to the one who does not understand from it, it is silent even if it speaks. And His statement: 'This is Our book that speaks against you with truth.' So the book is speaking, but its speech is perceived by the eye just as speech is a book but is perceived by the ear. And His statement: 'And they will say to their skins, "Why did you testify against us?" They will say, "Allah has made us speak, He who makes everything speak."' So the reality of speech in the perspective of the Noble Quran is His statement: 'Allah has made us speak, He who makes everything speak.' So it is a divine honor for humans to manifest what the mind determines, and the tongue is placed as an executive tool, nothing else.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'natiq' (speaking/rational) and 'samit' (silent) from a Quranic perspective. The author begins by posing the question 'What is the speaking and the silent?' and answers that they are informative indications and admonishing lessons. He then provides Quranic evidence. He cites the verse about the idols that do not speak (Q 21:65) to show that they are not of the 'speaking' class of rational beings. He then discusses how birds' sounds are called 'speech' because Prophet Solomon understood them (Q 27:16), indicating that anything from which a meaning is understood is considered 'speaking' relative to the one who understands, even if it is physically silent. Conversely, something may be 'silent' relative to someone who does not understand it, even if it makes sounds. He then uses the verse 'This is Our book that speaks against you with truth' (Q 45:29) to show that the Quran itself speaks, but its speech is perceived by the eye (reading) just as spoken words are perceived by the ear. Finally, he quotes the verse about the skins speaking on the Day of Judgment (Q 41:21) to illustrate that Allah gives speech to everything. The essence of 'speech' in the Quran is that it is a divine honor for humans to express what reason determines, with the tongue as an instrument. This passage uses Quranic examples to expand the definition of speech beyond mere vocalization to any conveyance of meaning.

Definition of Jild (Skin) and Its Quranic and Scientific Significance
Author defines 'jild' and expounds its Quranic role in sensation and testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the lexical definition of 'jild' (skin) and explain its expanded role as a sensory organ in the Quran backed by scientific evidence.

Core Idea
The skin is not just a covering; it is a witness and a sophisticated sensory organ in Quran and science.

In this lesson, we examine how the author defines 'jild' (skin) and then expands its meaning using Quranic verses and modern science. This is not merely a lexical exercise; it shows how a term can be given theological and physical depth. You will learn to identify the core definition and the layers of expansion that support the Quranic view of the skin as a primary sense organ and a testifier on the Day of Judgment.

Guided Path
1
Extract the lexical definition
Find the explicit definition of 'jild' given by the author. What genus does he assign it to? (The covering of the body)
The author says: 'الجلد : قشر البدن' (The skin: the covering of the body).
2
Identify the Quranic role
List the Quranic verses cited and note how each highlights the skin's sensory function. Verses: Q 4:56 (replacement of skins for pain), Q 39:23 (shivering and softening of skins), Q 41:20-21 (skins testify).
3
Analyze the logical sequence
In Q 39:23, the author notes a sequence: shiver, then soften, then hearts. What does this imply about the order of sensory and emotional impact? The skin reacts first, then the heart.
4
Correlate scientific data
Match the scientific description of nerve endings and receptors (53 million pain devices, 200,000 heat/cold devices, etc.) with the Quranic depiction. How does the author use science to support the Quranic claims?
The author cites medical findings to show skin's sophisticated sensation capabilities.
5
Synthesize the skin's testimony
Using Q 41:20-21, explain how the author argues that the skin will speak on Judgment Day, and what this reveals about its created nature. This combines the lexical definition with theological expansion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجلد : قشر البدن وجمعه جلود . اهتم القرآن بالجلد باعتباره من اهم حواس اللمس فقال كُلَّمَا نَضِجَتْ جُلُودُهُم بَدَلْنَاهُمْ جُلُودًا غَيْرهَا لِيَذُوقُوا الْعَذَابَ اللهُ نَزَلَ أَحْسَنَ الْحَدِيثِ كِتَابًا مُتَشَابِهَا مَثَانِيَ تَقْشَعِرُ مِنْهُ جُلُودُ الَّذِينَ يَخْشَونَ ربهم ثُمَّ تَلِينُ جُلُودُهُم وقُلُوبُهُمْ إِلَى ذِكْرِ اللهِ ) ٦ نلاحظ هنا ان التأثير الاول سيكون في الجلود قبل القلوب فجاء التسلسل المنطقي ( تقشعر منه جلودهم ) (ثُمَّ تَلِينُ جُلُودُهُمْ ( الى ان ينتقل التاثير الى القلوب سواء أكانت المشاعر ايجابية كما هي (جلود الذين يخشون ربهم أم سلبية لاصحاب النار ( حَتَّى إِذَا مَا جَاءُوهَا شَهِدَ عَلَيْهِمْ سمعهُمْ وَأَبْصَارَهُمْ وَجُلُودُهُم بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ . وَقَالُوا لِجُلُودهمْ لِمَ شَهِدتُّمْ عَلَيْنَا قَالُوا أَنْطَقَنَا اللَّهُ الَّذِي أَنْطَقَ كُلَّ شيء۱ لو جئت بابرة وزرقتها في جسم الانسان فانها بعد ان تدخل تصل اللحم فان الانسان في هذا الموضع لايتألم وقد وجد الاطباء ان الاعصاب تتركز في الجلد كما وجدوا ) ان هناك جسيمات خاصة في الجلد بانماط الحس للحر والبرد والالم والضغط واللمس تكون بشكل سلاسل ودوائر إذ يحوي الجلد على ٥٣ ملايين جهازا للاحساس بالالم و ٢٠٠ الف جهاز للحر والبرد و ٥٠٠ الف جهاز للمس والضغط وان الجلد يرسل اشارات البرد والحر بسرعة ۲۰۰ميل في الساعة من خلال الياف دقيقة بحجم ١ / ٤٠٠٠ من البوصة وهناك اشارات اسرع واكثر كفاءة للشعور بالالم . فالجلد ينقل الى الدماغ اخبار جميع العضلات والمفاصل والعظام وتفاصيل حياتها اليومية لغرض تحليلها ومعالجتها
The skin: the covering of the body, its plural is skins. The Quran paid attention to the skin as one of the most important senses of touch, saying: 'Whenever their skins are cooked thoroughly, We will replace them with other skins so that they may taste the punishment.' Allah sent down the best of speech, a book consistent in its pairs, from which the skins of those who fear their Lord shiver, then their skins and their hearts soften to the remembrance of Allah. We notice here that the first effect will be on the skins before the hearts, so the logical sequence came: 'shiver from it their skins' then 'their skins soften' until the effect transfers to the hearts, whether the feelings are positive as with the skins of those who fear their Lord, or negative for the inhabitants of the Fire: 'Until, when they come to it, their hearing, their sight, and their skins will testify against them about what they used to do. And they will say to their skins, "Why did you testify against us?" They will say, "Allah has made us speak, He who makes everything speak."' If you took a needle and pricked it into a human body, after it enters and reaches the flesh, the human does not feel pain in that spot. The doctors have found that the nerves are concentrated in the skin, and they have found that there are special particles in the skin for patterns of sensation for heat, cold, pain, pressure, and touch, arranged in chains and circles. The skin contains 53 million devices for sensing pain, 200,000 devices for heat and cold, 500,000 devices for touch and pressure. The skin sends signals of cold and heat at a speed of 200 miles per hour through fine fibers 1/4000 of an inch in size, and there are faster and more efficient signals for feeling pain. So the skin transmits to the brain news of all the muscles, joints, and bones and the details of their daily life for analysis and processing.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines 'jild' (skin) and discusses its significance in the Quran and in science. The author first gives a lexical definition: skin is the covering of the body. Then he emphasizes the Quran's attention to the skin as a primary sense of touch, citing verses about the punishment of changing skins (Q 4:56) and the effect of the Quran on the skins of the God-fearing (Q 39:23). He notes a logical sequence: the skin reacts first, then the heart. He then cites the verse from Q 41:20-21 about the skin testifying on the Day of Judgment, showing that Allah gives speech to the skin. The author then supports this with scientific evidence: the concentration of nerves in the skin, the different receptors for heat, cold, pain, pressure, and touch, and the speed of signal transmission. The purpose is to show that the skin is a sophisticated sensory organ that constantly communicates with the brain. This passage combines Quranic and scientific testimony to affirm the skin's role in sensation and its ability to 'speak' as mentioned in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Four Meanings of 'Aql from the Imams
Author lists four meanings of intellect from Imam traditions plus a common definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four meanings of 'aql presented in this passage and explain how each relates to moral and intellectual virtue.

Core Idea
In Islam, 'aql is not merely cognitive but also practical wisdom, moral conduct, and a tool for ethical discernment.

This passage from a Shi'i philosophical work presents four distinct yet complementary meanings of 'aql, drawn from the teachings of the Imams. These definitions go beyond the purely cognitive to include self-restraint, the ability to choose the lesser evil, good character, and the knowledge faculty. Understanding these nuances is essential for grasping how intellect functions in Islamic ethics and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify each meaning and its source
Locate the four meanings in the Arabic. The first is from Imam Ali: 'the intelligent one is he who overcomes his desires.' The second is also from Imam Ali: 'the intelligent is he who knows the better of two evils.' The third from Imam al-Sadiq: three conditions for intelligence. The fourth from Imam al-Kadhim: manners and refraining from harm increase intellect.
Each meaning is attributed to a different Imam, showing the depth of the tradition.
2
Extract the defining characteristic for each meaning
For each meaning, identify the key attribute: (1) self-control and prioritizing the afterlife; (2) practical discernment between two evils; (3) ethical conduct: giving right, treating others as oneself, forbearance; (4) the knowing faculty enhanced by adab and harm avoidance.
Meanings 1-3 are moral/behavioral; meaning 4 is cognitive but tied to action.
3
Compare the moral meanings with the common definition
The common definition describes intellect as a light that reveals intelligibles, analogous to vision. Contrast this epistemological definition with the earlier ethical ones. Note that the common definition focuses on cognition, while the Imams emphasize moral action.
The common definition is a philosophical addition, not from the Imams.
4
Evaluate the range of the term 'aql
Reflect on why 'aql encompasses both moral and cognitive aspects. In Islamic thought, true intellect manifests in righteous action. This integration is crucial for understanding concepts like taklif (moral responsibility) and hikmah (wisdom).
Ignoring the moral dimension could lead to a reductionist view of 'aql.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاقل وقال الامام علي عليه السلام ) العاقل من غلب هواه ولم يبع آخرته بدنياه) ٢ ( التثبت رأس العقل والحدة رأس الحمق)۳ وفي المعنى الثاني (العقل بمعنى اداة التمييز بين الصالح والطالح قال الامام علي بن ابي طالب عليه السلام ) ليس العاقل من يعرف الخير من الشر . ولكن العاقل من يعرف خير الشرين ( ٤ ) كثرة الصواب تنبىء عن وفور العقل ٥ وفي المعنى الثالث (العقل بمعنى العمل الحسن نذكر قول الامام جعفر بن محمد الصادق عليه السلام ) لا يعد العاقل عاقلا حتى يستكمل ثلاثا : اعطاء الحق من نفسه على حال الرضا والغضب ، وأن يرضى للناس ما يرضى لنفسه . واستعمال الحلم عند العثرة )٦ وفي المعنى الرابع : العقل بمعنى القوة العالمة ( نذكر قول الامام موسى بن جعفر الكاظم عليه السلام ) آداب العلماء زيادة في العقل وكف الأذى من كمال العقل) ومن التعاريف الشائعة ( العقل هو نور يلمع في افق النفس تنكشف عنه غواشي الحجب فتتجلى فيه صور المعقولات كما تتجلى في العين صور المحسوسات)
The intelligent one, and Imam Ali said: 'The intelligent one is he who overcomes his desires and does not sell his hereafter for his world.' (2) 'Steadfastness is the head of intellect, and hastiness is the head of foolishness.' (3) And in the second meaning: intellect as a tool for distinguishing good from evil. Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib said: 'The intelligent is not he who knows good from evil, but the intelligent is he who knows the better of two evils.' (4) 'Abundance of correctness indicates abundance of intellect.' (5) And in the third meaning: intellect as good conduct. We mention the saying of Imam Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq: 'The intelligent is not counted as intelligent until he completes three things: giving right from himself in states of satisfaction and anger, to be pleased for people with what he is pleased for himself, and using forbearance at the time of stumbling.' (6) And in the fourth meaning: intellect as the knowing faculty. We mention the saying of Imam Musa ibn Ja'far al-Kadhim: 'The manners of the scholars increase in intellect, and refraining from harm is from the perfection of intellect.' And among the common definitions: 'Intellect is a light that shines in the horizon of the soul, through which the veils of obstacles are removed, and forms of intelligibles appear in it just as forms of sensibles appear in the eye.'
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a definitional passage that enumerates various meanings of 'aql (intellect) from the teachings of the Imams, followed by a common philosophical definition. The author begins with a hadith from Imam Ali defining the intelligent person as one who overcomes desires and prioritizes the hereafter. Then follows another concise hadith: 'Steadfastness is the head of intellect, and hastiness is the head of foolishness.' This presents an ethical dimension of intellect. The second meaning explains intellect as a tool for distinguishing good from evil. A nuanced hadith from Imam Ali states that the truly intelligent person is not merely one who knows good and evil, but one who can choose the better of two evils—indicating practical wisdom. The third meaning presents intellect as good conduct, requiring three qualities: giving right in both comfort and anger, treating others as oneself, and using forbearance when others err. The fourth meaning treats intellect as the knowing faculty, with a hadith emphasizing that the manners of scholars increase intellect and refraining from harm is perfection of intellect. Finally, a common definition describes intellect as a luminous faculty that enables the soul to perceive intelligible forms, analogous to visual perception. This passage thus provides both a moral-religious and a philosophical-gnostic understanding of intellect.

The Quranic 'Lubb': Pure Intellect as the Kernel of Understanding
Author explains the Quranic term for intellect as 'lubb', the pure essence of reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'aql and 'lubb' based on the Quranic usage and explain why 'lubb' indicates a purified intellect.

Core Idea
'Lubb' is the pure kernel of intellect; not every 'aql qualifies as 'lubb'.

In the Quran, the term 'aql is often replaced by 'lubb' when referring to the highest form of understanding. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and conceptual distinction, showing that 'lubb' denotes intellect stripped of impurities, much like the kernel of a fruit. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping why certain Quranic rulings are addressed to 'uli al-albab'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and genus
The term being defined is 'lubb'. The author places it under the genus 'aql (intellect), stating that 'lubb' is a type of 'aql.
2
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes 'lubb' from ordinary 'aql? The author mentions 'khulas' (pure) and 'zaka' (refined). 'Lubb' is the purest essence, like the kernel of a fruit.
The differentia is purity or refinement.
3
Analyze the subset relationship
The author explicitly states: 'fakullu lubbin 'aqlun wa laysa kullu 'aqlin lubbun' — every lubb is intellect, but not every intellect is lubb. This means lubb is a proper subset of aql.
This is a key logical relationship: inclusion but not equality.
4
Apply to Quranic context
Allah attaches certain rulings that only pure intellects perceive to 'uli al-albab'. This shows the practical significance: the term lubb identifies those capable of grasping deep divine commandments.
Connects definition to tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل في القرآن الكريم وفي لغة القرآن الكريم يسمى العقل اللب { وهو العقل الخالص من الشوائب وسمي بذلك لكونه خالص ما في الانسان من معانيه كاللباب واللب من الشيء . وقيل هو ما زكا من العقل فكل لب عقل وليس كل عقل لبا ولهذا علق الله تعالى الأحكام التي لايدركها الا العقول الزكية بأولي الالباب
Intellect in the Noble Quran: In the language of the Noble Quran, intellect is called 'lubb', which is intellect purified of impurities. It is so named because it is the purest of what is in man of its meanings, like the pulp and the kernel of a thing. It is said that it is the refined part of intellect, so every 'lubb' is intellect but not every intellect is 'lubb'. For this reason, Allah Most High attached the rulings that only pure intellects perceive to 'uli al-albab' (people of understanding).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines the Quranic term 'lubb' as the purest form of intellect. The author explains that 'lubb' is the intellect free from impurities, analogous to the kernel or pulp of a fruit. It is the refined essence of human intellect. Importantly, there is a distinction: every 'lubb' is intellect, but not every intellect is 'lubb'. This means that 'lubb' refers to a higher level of intellectual purity. Because of this, Allah attaches certain rulings—those that only pure intellects can grasp—to 'uli al-albab', the people of understanding. Thus, the Quranic usage emphasizes a purified, penetrating intellect capable of comprehending divine commandments.

Key Terms
لبّ (lubb): The pure intellect, free from impurities, the kernel of understanding. [Note: In the Quran, 'lubb' often refers to the core of human intellect; later philosophical usage may not preserve this distinction.]

Key Takeaway
The Quranic term 'lubb' designates a purified, kernel-like intellect that is a subset of 'aql, and this distinction explains why certain divine rulings are addressed specifically to 'uli al-albab'.

Defining Ilham: Inspiration in Islamic Epistemology
Author defines ilham as knowledge without teaching, experience, or reasoning, citing lexicographical and Quranic sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the lexical and technical definitions of ilham and distinguish it from knowledge acquired through teaching, experience, or reasoning.

Core Idea
Ilham is divinely inspired knowledge swallowed by the heart without instruction.

This passage introduces the concept of ilham (inspiration) from its Arabic root meaning 'to swallow' to its technical sense as knowledge received from the Preserved Tablet. We will explore how the author defines ilham as knowledge without teaching, experience, or reasoning, and see how this fits into Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Trace the lexical root of ilham
The author begins with the root 'l-h-m', meaning to swallow. Ilham is like something thrown into the heart that is swallowed, metaphorically indicating immediate reception.
Notice the shift from physical swallowing to intellectual reception.
2
Extract the technical definition
Identify the key phrase: 'حدوث علم في النفس من دون تعليم ولا تجربة ولا تفكير' (occurrence of knowledge in the soul without teaching, experience, or thinking). This is the core definition that separates ilham from other sources.
Memorize this phrase as it encapsulates the essence.
3
Analyze the subcategories of ilham
The author distinguishes between intuitive (وجداني) ilham, like necessary or presential knowledge, and evidence-based (عن دليل) ilham, like empirical or theoretical knowledge. Both share the absence of external instruction.
This shows ilham is not limited to mystical experiences; it can involve reasoning but without a teacher.
4
Contrast ilham with other knowledge sources
Compare ilham with teaching (تعليم), experience (تجربة), and thinking (تفكير). Ilham excludes all three. In Islamic sciences, this distinguishes it from rational deduction (ijtihad) and empirical observation.
Think of examples: learning fiqh from a scholar is teaching; observing natural phenomena is experience; deriving rulings by qiyas is thinking.

- Ilham (inspiration)
It indicates the swallowing of something, and from this category is al-ilham, as if something is thrown into the heart and it swallows it. The mulham (inspired one) is the one who understands, whose opinion is correct in things, angels speak on his tongue, so correctness occurs from him without intention. Commentary
This passage defines the concept of ilham (inspiration). The author begins by giving the lexical meaning: swallowing. Ilham is like something thrown into the heart that is swallowed. The inspired person is one whose opinions are correct, as if angels speak through him. The root is swallowing. The author then quotes al-Raghib al-Isfahani, who defines ilham as an emanation from the Preserved Tablet, through which the soul acquires knowledge by the illumination of the universal soul. He mentions that ethical principles are presential knowledge. Then a Quranic verse is cited: Allah inspires the soul with its wickedness and righteousness. A hadith clarifies that God shows the soul what to do and avoid. The author then states that ilham is specifically knowledge that occurs without teaching, experience, or reasoning; it is knowledge without evidence, both intuitive and reasoned. The term is precise for psychological meanings and was rare in pre-Islamic Arabic. It derives from 'swallowing'. The passage ends with a reference to jurists, but it is cut off.

Key Terms
الإلهام (al-ilham): Knowledge that occurs in the soul without teaching, experience, or thinking; it can be intuitive or based on evidence but is received without external instruction. [Note: In classical Islamic thought, ilham is often contrasted with wahy (revelation); here it is more broadly defined as emanation from the Preserved Tablet.]

Key Takeaway

Defining Ilham: Multiple Approaches in Islamic Thought
Author compiles multiple definitions of ilham from various Islamic sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the common characteristics of ilham as defined by different Islamic scholars and distinguish it from wahy and rational inference.

Core Idea
Ilham is direct divine prompting to the soul, known by chest expansion and certainty.

This passage brings together definitions of ilham from lexicography, theology, and poetry. While no single definition is complete, together they reveal a coherent picture: ilham is a form of non-prophetic divine guidance that bypasses intermediaries and rational deduction. In this lesson, you will analyze these definitions to extract the essential features of ilham.

Guided Path
1
Trace the lexical root
The passage roots ilham in the verb 'to swallow' (l-h-m). Consider how this metaphor suggests receiving truth passively, like drinking. What does this etymology imply about the nature of ilham?
Etymology often reveals the core imagery of a concept.
2
List theological definitions
Read definitions such as 'middle term between creation and legislation', 'inner sciences without intermediary', 'quick notification in a hidden manner'. What common thread do they share?
Look for terms like 'direct', 'without intermediary', 'hidden'.
3
Examine epistemological criteria
Shaykh Muhammad Abduh describes ilham as something the soul is certain of, driven without awareness of source. How does this distinguish ilham from knowledge acquired through study?
Abduh compares it to sensations like hunger, which are immediate and certain.
4
Identify practical signs
The passage states that true ilham causes chest expansion and lacks contradiction. Why are these signs important? How do they help avoid mistaking personal whims for divine guidance?
These signs align with other Islamic discussions of spiritual states.
5
Compare ilham to other sources
Contrast ilham with wahy (prophetic revelation) and rational inference. The passage emphasizes 'without intermediary' for ilham, whereas wahy involves an angel. Rational inference requires intellectual effort. How does this clarify the distinct role of ilham?
This comparison is crucial for proper application in theology.

معرفة العلة هو إلهام (جاء في لسان العرب: أنها من مادة : لهم، على وزن فهم، أي ابتلاع أو شرب الشيء، والالهام يعني التلقين الالهي ومع الى أصل هذه الكلمة يمكن العثور على سبب الاطلاق، وكأن الروح تفتح فم الانسان وتلقي فيه حقيقة فيمضغها ( ( (الالهام أن يلقى الله في النفس أمرا يبعثه على الفعل او الترك) ٢ ( العلامة النائيني قال : الإلهام هو الحد الوسط بين التكوين والتشريع ( ٣ (الإلهام) علوما ذاتية حاصلة عن إخبار من الله بلا واسطة بين المخبر والمخبر (له الإلهام وحي (باطن) (الالهام : النكث في القلوب) (الإلهام إعلام سريع على وجه الخفاء)) (الإلهام : ما يلقى في الروع بطريق الفيض ) ( الإلهام هو العلم اللدني الذي لا واسطة في حصوله بين النفس وبين الباري * وقيل إن الإلهام خاطر حق من الحق تعالى فمن علامته أن ينشرح له الصدر ولا يعارضه معارض من خاطر آخر أما الشيخ محمد عبده فقال الإلهام) ماتستيقنه النفس وتنساق إلى ما يطلب على غير شعور منها من أين أتى، وهو أشبه بوجدان الجوع والعطش والحزن والسرور) كما عرف الالهام بانه عبارة عن معنى يلقى في نفس الإنسان في حالة وجدانية خاصة يثق بها ويعمل بموجبها غير متردد) " وعرف الإلهام) بمعنى الإدراك الصحيح لواقع الأشياء أي الافهام. يحصل من صفاء النفس وحدة الذهن قال النبي- ص- لحصير بن منذر الخزاعي لما أسلم قل اللهم ألهمني رشدي وقني شر نفسي) ٣ وفي الدعاء أسألك رحمةً من عندك تُلْهِمني بها رشدي) والإلهام يسمى توقيفا و التوقيف ان تقول إن الفاعل مرفوع والمفعول به منصوب هذا ازرق وذاك احمر قال الشاعر عبدالستار الزعبي طافوا طواف العاشقين بكعبة وقف الجمال بيابها البسام ... طافوا بها سبعاً فطاف جمالها ... في الكون حتى خلتها قدامي فهممت بالحجر الكريم مزاحماً ... علي أفوز بقبلة الأحلام فافهم رموز الحج فهي بليغة ... إن الرموز مفاتح الإلهام وقال آخر : كأن الشيخ ينظر في هدوء ......... إلى الإلهام من خلف السجوف
Knowledge of the cause is inspiration. (It came in Lisan al-Arab: it is from the root 'l-h-m', on the pattern of fahima, meaning to swallow or drink something, and ilham means divine prompting. And looking at the origin of this word, one can find the reason for the application, as if the spirit opens the mouth of the human and throws into it a truth which he chews.) (Ilham is that God casts into the soul a matter that prompts it to action or abstinence.) (Allamah Na'ini said: Inspiration is the middle term between creation and legislation.) (Inspiration: inner sciences obtained from informing by God without an intermediary between the informer and the informed; for him inspiration is inner revelation.) (Inspiration: what is cast into hearts.) (Inspiration: quick notification in a hidden manner.) (Inspiration: what is cast into the mind by way of emanation.) (Inspiration is the knowledge from the Divine which has no intermediary in its attainment between the soul and the Creator. And it was said that inspiration is a true thought from the Truth, exalted, and its sign is that the breast expands for it and no opposing thought opposes it. As for Shaykh Muhammad Abduh, he said: Inspiration is what the soul is certain of and is driven to what it seeks without being aware of where it came from, and it resembles the feeling of hunger, thirst, sadness and joy.) And also inspiration was defined as a meaning cast into a person's soul in a special emotional state which he trusts and acts accordingly without hesitation. And inspiration was defined as correct perception of the reality of things, i.e., understanding. It occurs from purity of soul and sharpness of mind. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Haseen ibn Mundhir al-Khuza'i when he became Muslim: 'Say: O Allah, inspire me with my right conduct and protect me from the evil of my soul.' And in the supplication: 'I ask You for mercy from You, with which You inspire me to my right conduct.' And inspiration is called tawqif (stopping/instruction), and tawqif is like saying: The subject is nominative and the object is accusative, this is blue and that is red. The poet Abd al-Sattar al-Zu'bi said: They circumambulated the Kaaba like lovers, / and beauty stood at its smiling door... They circumambulated it seven times, then its beauty circumambulated / the universe until I thought it was in front of me. So I intended to crowd towards the Black Stone / so that I might gain the kiss of dreams. So understand the symbols of Hajj, for they are eloquent / indeed, symbols are keys to inspiration. And another said: It is as if the shaykh looks in tranquility / at inspiration from behind veils.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of definitions of ilham (inspiration) from various Islamic sources, including lexicography (Lisan al-Arab), theology (e.g., Allamah Na'ini, Shaykh Muhammad Abduh), and poetry. The author begins by stating that knowledge of the cause is inspiration, then traces the etymology from the root meaning 'to swallow' or 'to drink', likening the soul to a mouth that receives truth. The subsequent definitions emphasize that ilham is a divine casting into the soul, a middle term between creation and legislation, inner knowledge without intermediary, quick hidden notification, direct emanation, and knowledge from the Divine. The sign of true inspiration is expansion of the chest and absence of contradiction. Shaykh Muhammad Abduh's definition highlights its self-evident and non-reflective nature, akin to basic sensations. The passage also includes a hadith and a supplication where the Prophet asks for inspiration. Finally, inspiration is equated with tawqif (instruction), exemplified by grammatical rules and colors. The poetry illustrates that symbols, like the rituals of Hajj, are keys to inspiration, and that inspiration can be perceived from behind veils, suggesting a subtle, intuitive grasp.

Key Takeaway
Ilham is a direct, intermediary-free divine inspiration known by chest expansion, certainty, and absence of contradiction, distinct from both prophetic revelation and rational deduction.

Definitions of Ilham in Islamic Thought
Author presents multiple definitions of ilham from scholars and poets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify, differentiate, and analyze the multiple definitions of ilham provided by scholars and poets, and recognize the common elements that characterize ilham as a form of divine inspiration.

Core Idea
Ilham is a certainty that comes to the soul without conscious reasoning, often likened to innate feelings.

The concept of ilham (inspiration) is central to Islamic epistemology and spirituality. In this passage, we encounter definitions from the reformer Shaykh Muhammad Abduh, unnamed scholars, and even poets. Each definition emphasizes a different aspect: the unconscious certainty, the emotional state, or the purity of soul required. By analyzing these definitions, you will gain a nuanced understanding of how Islamic scholars have understood non-prophetic inspiration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first definition
Locate the definition by Shaykh Muhammad Abduh. What does he say ilham is? Notice that he describes it as a certainty in the soul that arises without awareness of its source, comparing it to basic feelings.
He used examples like hunger and thirst.
2
Extract the key components
Break down the definition: it involves certainty (yaqeen), inclination towards something, and lack of conscious awareness of the source. Also note the analogy with physical sensations.
3
Identify the second definition
Find the definition that describes ilham as 'a meaning cast into the soul in a special emotional state'. Notice the emphasis on trust and action without hesitation.
This definition highlights the emotional and trust aspects.
4
Identify the third definition
Find the definition that describes ilham as 'correct perception of reality attained through purity of soul and sharpness of mind'. This is more cognitive. Identify the genus and differentia: genus is 'correct perception' (idrak sahih), differentia is 'attained through purity of soul and sharpness of mind'.
This is a rasm (description) type definition.
5
Compare and contrast
What do all three definitions have in common? (e.g., they all involve a form of knowledge or certainty that is not derived from ordinary reasoning). What distinguishes them? (e.g., Abduh's emphasizes lack of awareness, the second emphasizes emotional state, the third emphasizes purity of soul). Also note the poetic definitions: how do they relate to the scholarly ones?
The poetic definitions illustrate ilham as a source of understanding and beauty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشيخ محمد عبده فقال الإلهام) ماتستيقنه النفس وتنساق إلى ما يطلب على غير شعور منها من أين أتى، وهو أشبه بوجدان الجوع والعطش والحزن والسرور) كما عرف الالهام بانه عبارة عن معنى يلقى في نفس الإنسان في حالة وجدانية خاصة يثق بها ويعمل بموجبها غير متردد) " وعرف الإلهام) بمعنى الإدراك الصحيح لواقع الأشياء أي الافهام. يحصل من صفاء النفس وحدة الذهن قال النبي- ص- لحصير بن منذر الخزاعي لما أسلم قل اللهم ألهمني رشدي وقني شر نفسي) ٣ وفي الدعاء أسألك رحمةً من عندك تُلْهِمني بها رشدي) والإلهام يسمى توقيفا و التوقيف ان تقول إن الفاعل مرفوع والمفعول به منصوب هذا ازرق وذاك احمر قال الشاعر عبدالستار الزعبي طافوا طواف العاشقين بكعبة وقف الجمال بيابها البسام ... طافوا بها سبعاً فطاف جمالها ... في الكون حتى خلتها قدامي فهممت بالحجر الكريم مزاحماً ... علي أفوز بقبلة الأحلام فافهم رموز الحج فهي بليغة ... إن الرموز مفاتح الإلهام وقال آخر : كأن الشيخ ينظر في هدوء .......... إلى الإلهام من خلف السجوف
As for Shaykh Muhammad Abduh, he said: 'Ilham is what the soul is certain of and inclines toward what it seeks without awareness of where it came from, and it resembles the feeling of hunger, thirst, sadness, and joy.' It was also defined as 'a meaning cast into the soul of a person in a special emotional state that he trusts and acts upon without hesitation.' And it was defined as 'correct perception of the reality of things, i.e., understanding, attained through purity of soul and sharpness of mind.' The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Haseer ibn Mundhir al-Khuza'i when he became Muslim: 'Say: O Allah, inspire me with my right conduct and protect me from the evil of my soul.' And in supplication: 'I ask You for mercy from You by which You inspire me with my right conduct.' Ilham is also called tawqif, and tawqif is to say: 'The subject is nominative, the object is accusative, this is blue and that is red.' The poet Abdul Sattar al-Za'bi said: 'They circumambulated like lovers around a Ka'ba, beauty stood at its smiling door... They circumambulated seven times, then its beauty circumambulated the universe until I thought it was in front of me. So I tried to push towards the noble stone, perhaps I would win a kiss of dreams. So understand the symbols of Hajj, for they are eloquent, for symbols are keys to ilham.' And another said: 'It is as if the old man gazes in tranquility... at ilham from behind the veils.'
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'ilham' (divine inspiration) as understood by various scholars. It begins with the definition by Shaykh Muhammad Abduh, who describes ilham as a certainty that the soul experiences and inclines toward without conscious awareness of its origin, comparing it to innate feelings like hunger and thirst. Then a second definition is given: ilham is a meaning cast into the soul in a special emotional state, which the person trusts and acts upon without hesitation. A third definition describes ilham as correct perception of the reality of things, achieved through purity of soul and sharpness of mind. The Prophet's supplication for inspiration (ilham) of right conduct and the prayer for inspiring mercy are cited as religious evidence. The passage further explains that ilham is also called 'tawqif,' which is exemplified by linguistic conventions such as 'the subject is nominative, the object is accusative'—these are conventional facts known by inspiration. Finally, two poetic excerpts illustrate ilham: one from Abd al-Sattar al-Za'bi, who likens understanding Hajj symbols to keys of inspiration, and another describing the elder's contemplation of ilham behind veils.

Key Takeaway
Ilham is a multifaced concept defined by various scholars as a form of divine inspiration characterized by certainty, emotional trust, or cognitive insight attained through spiritual purity.

Six Characteristics of Ilham
Author enumerates six distinctive features of ilham as a source of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain each of the six characteristics of ilham as a source of knowledge, and distinguish ilham from acquired knowledge (ilm husuli).

Core Idea
Ilham is immediate, soul-specific, uninherited, and divine—nothing like human learning.

The passage lists six distinctive features that set ilham apart from other types of knowledge. Understanding these characteristics helps you recognize what counts as ilham and what does not—a crucial skill in Islamic epistemology, especially when evaluating claims of inner inspiration.

Guided Path
1
Identify ilham's first feature
Read the first characteristic: ilham enters the soul directly after creation. The author cites Quran 91:7-8. Note that this immediacy distinguishes ilham from knowledge acquired later through senses or reason.
The Quranic verse is about inspiration of right and wrong, not specific knowledge.
2
Notice the second feature
Ilham is specific to the individual soul—it is not shared. Each person's ilham is private to them, unlike public revelation or common knowledge.
3
Recognize the third feature
Ilham is 'ilm huduri (present knowledge)—it occurs without any acquisition (kasb). There is no teacher, book, or reasoning involved. This contrasts with 'ilm husuli (acquired knowledge).
The term 'ilm huduri is a key technical term in Islamic philosophy.
4
Understand the fourth feature
Ilham is described as 'wahy batin' (inner revelation). This means it comes from within, not through external prophets or angels. It is limited to the individual and not a message for others.
This differentiates ilham from prophetic wahy.
5
Analyze the fifth feature
Ilham is not hereditary. If it were, a doctor's son would automatically become a doctor. The fact that knowledge must be taught proves ilham is not inherited. This also implies ilham is not a substitute for learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمات الالهام (1) يدخل الى النفس مباشرة بعد الخلق (وَنَفْسٍ وَمَا سَواهَا ، فَأَلْهَمَهَا فجورها وتقواها) فيميزه الطفل بوقت مبكر من حياته. ۲ ) يختص بالنفس دون سواها كما يوحي نص الآية المباركة. ۳ ) يشرق على الانسان من دون وساطة (علم حضوري يقع بلا كسب ٤ ) إنه وحي باطن. (٥) لا ينتقل من طريق الوراثة إن معلومات الالهام لا تنتقل من أحد إلى أحد من هذا الطريق وإلا أصبح ابن الطبيب طبيباً بالوراثة وابن العالم عالماً بالوراثة حتماً دون أن يتلقى العلم أو الطب من أحد (٦) منشأ الالهام غير أرضي وخارج عن طاقة الانسان )
Characteristics of ilham: (1) It enters the soul directly after creation (reference to Quran 91:7-8: 'And the soul and the One who proportioned it, and inspired it with its wickedness and its righteousness'), so the child distinguishes between them at an early stage of his life. (2) It is specific to the soul alone, as the blessed verse suggests. (3) It shines upon a person without mediation (present knowledge that occurs without acquisition). (4) It is inner revelation. (5) It is not transmitted through heredity; the information of ilham is not transmitted from one to another through this path, otherwise the son of a doctor would become a doctor by heredity, and the son of a scholar would become a scholar by heredity necessarily without receiving knowledge or medicine from anyone. (6) Its source is non-earthly and beyond human capacity.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage lists six characteristics of ilham (divine inspiration) as a source of knowledge. First, it enters the soul directly after creation, citing the Quranic verse about inspiration of wickedness and righteousness, and noting that a child distinguishes between them early in life. Second, ilham is specific to the individual soul, not shared. Third, it occurs without any intermediary, described as 'present knowledge' (ilm huduri) that is not acquired through effort. Fourth, it is inner revelation, meaning it comes from within. Fifth, it is not inherited; if it were, sons of professionals would automatically inherit their fathers' knowledge. Sixth, its origin is non-earthly and beyond human capacity, emphasizing its divine source.

Key Terms
علم حضوري (ilm huduri): Knowledge that is immediate and present to the knower without any mediated acquisition; ilham is of this type. [Note: In later Islamic philosophy, 'ilm huduri is contrasted with 'ilm husuli (acquired knowledge); this usage is consistent.]
وحي باطن (wahy batin): Inner revelation; ilham is considered a form of inner revelation distinct from prophetic revelation. [Note: Not commonly used; standard distinction is between wahy (prophetic) and ilham (non-prophetic).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It enters the soul directly after creation.

C) It is inner revelation.
D) Its source is non-earthly.

Key Takeaway
Ilham is a direct, soul-specific, unmediated, inner, non-inherited, and divinely sourced knowledge—distinct from all forms of acquired learning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Two Types of Ilham: General and Special
Author classifies ilham into general and special.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general and special ilham and determine the authority of each type.

Core Idea
Ilham is not monolithic — its authority depends on whether it is general or prophetic.

This passage divides ilham into two types, clarifying a common confusion about the status of inspiration. The distinction between general ilham, which all humans possess, and special ilham, granted to certain individuals, has significant implications for epistemology and religious authority. As you study this classification, you will learn how to evaluate claims of inspiration in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Locate the two numbered categories in the Arabic. The first is 'إلهام عام' (general ilham) and the second is 'إلهام خاص' (special ilham). Notice the terms used.
2
Compare the attributes of each type
For general ilham, note that it is permanent, effortless, and universal—given to all humans. For special ilham, note that it is temporary, intermittent, given to specific groups (prophets, saints, imams, jurists, poets), and varies in degree.
3
Examine the tradition on prophetic ilham
Read the reported tradition carefully. It distinguishes between the Prophet's rulings from revelation (wahi) and his choices from ilham. It then states that the Prophet's ilham is a decisive proof (حجة قاطعة), while others' ilham is not.
4
Apply the distinction to authority
Based on the passage, determine the authority of each type: general ilham allows distinction between good and evil but does not produce binding rulings; special ilham is only authoritative when it comes from the Prophet, not from others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الالهام (۱) إلهام عام لجميع أفراد النوع البشري من دون سعي منهم أو كسب وهو إلهام دائم يصاحب الانسان من البداية حتى النهاية كألهام معرفة الفجور والتقوى والقدرة على التمييز بينهما . (۲) إلهام خاص لفئات من الناس ويكون على درجات متفاوتة فإلهام النبي أو الولي أو الامام يختلف نوعا وكما عن إلهام الفقيه أو الشاعر أو العاشق وهو إلهام آني متقطع حسب التقدير الالهي ورد في الاثر ( إن رسول الله عليه السلام موصول بمصدر الوحي ومنبثق الإلهام، فما كان يعينه النبي من الأحكام فذاك مصدره الوحي ، وما كان يختاره صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم فمصدره الإلهام إنْ إلْهَامَ النّبي عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ حَجَةٌ قَاطِعَةٌ لَا يَسَعُ مُخَالَفَتْهُ بوجه ، وإِلْهَامُ غَيْره لَيس بحجة . ) قال النبي محمد - ص - وأعطاني الوحي،
Divisions of ilham: (1) General ilham for all individuals of the human species without effort or acquisition on their part; it is a permanent ilham that accompanies man from beginning to end, like the ilham of knowing wickedness and piety and the ability to distinguish between them. (2) Special ilham for categories of people, and it comes in varying degrees. Thus the ilham of the prophet, the saint (wali), or the imam differs in kind and quantity from the ilham of the jurist (faqih), the poet, or the lover. It is temporary and intermittent according to divine decree. It is reported in the tradition: 'Indeed, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is connected to the source of revelation and the emanation of ilham. Whatever the prophet prescribed from rulings, its source is revelation (wahi), and whatever he chose (peace be upon him and his family), its source is ilham. Indeed, the ilham of the prophet (peace be upon him) is a decisive proof that cannot be contradicted in any way, while the ilham of others is not a proof.' The Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) said: 'And I was given revelation.'
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage divides ilham into two types: general and special. The first type is general ilham, which all human beings possess without effort. It is permanent and is exemplified by the innate knowledge of good and evil (fujur and taqwa) and the ability to distinguish between them, as referenced in the Quranic verse. The second type is special ilham, given to specific groups of people, such as prophets, saints, and imams, and varies in degree. The ilham of a prophet differs qualitatively and quantitatively from that of a jurist, poet, or lover. This special ilham is temporary and intermittent, relying on divine will. The passage then cites a tradition stating that the Prophet Muhammad (PBUH) is connected to both revelation (wahi) and ilham: what he prescribed as rulings came from revelation, while his personal choices came from ilham. Importantly, the Prophet's ilham is considered decisive proof (hujja qati'a) that must be followed, whereas the ilham of others is not authoritative proof.

Key Terms
إلهام عام
إلهام خاص
وحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the Prophet's ilham compared to the ilham of others?
A) It is not a proof and can be contradicted.
B) It is a decisive proof that cannot be opposed.
C) It is the same as the ilham of a jurist.
D) It is considered revelation (wahi).

Key Takeaway
The authority of ilham depends on its source: prophetic ilham is binding, all other ilham is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Characteristics and Divisions of Inspiration (al-Ilham)
Lists six characteristics and two divisions of inspiration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six characteristics of inspiration and distinguish between general and specific inspiration as defined in this passage.

Core Idea
Inspiration is innate, uninherited, and only the Prophet's inspiration is conclusive proof.

This passage from an Islamic epistemology text defines inspiration (ilham) as a form of presential knowledge (ilm huduri) that enters the soul directly. It lists six distinguishing characteristics and divides inspiration into two types: a general one common to all humans and a specific one granted to select individuals like prophets, saints, and jurists. Understanding these categories is essential for evaluating the epistemological weight of claims based on inspiration, especially in distinguishing prophetic guidance from personal insight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic. It is 'al-ilham' (inspiration). Notice that the passage begins with 'Characteristics of inspiration'.
The term is defined through a list of characteristics rather than a single genus-differentia formula.
2
Extract the six characteristics
Read the six numbered points in the Arabic. List each one: (1) enters soul directly after creation, (2) specific to soul, (3) unmediated presential knowledge, (4) inner revelation, (5) not inherited, (6) non-earthly source. Paraphrase each in your own words.
Charactistic (5) is argued by a reductio: if inherited, sons of scholars would automatically be scholars.
3
Distinguish ilham from wahy
Characteristic (4) says ilham is 'inner revelation' (wahy batin). The passage later distinguishes the Prophet's wahy (external revelation) from his ilham (personal choices). Note that ilham is a type of wahy but batin (inner).
This hierarchical distinction is crucial: the Prophet receives both, but others receive only ilham.
4
Analyze the two divisions
Identify general inspiration: for all humans, permanent, includes knowledge of good and evil. Identify specific inspiration: for categories (prophet, saint, imam, faqih, poet, lover), varies in degree, intermittent. Note the tradition about the Prophet's ilham being hujja qati'a (conclusive proof) while others' is not.
The division shows that not all inspiration carries equal epistemic authority.
5
Apply the definitional framework
Check if this definition of ilham covers all instances: does it include the child's discernment, the prophet's choices, the poet's inspiration? Test whether it excludes what it should: inherited knowledge, acquired knowledge, external revelation.
The author's definition is descriptive (rasm), listing properties, not a strict logical definition (hadd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمات الالهام
(1) يدخل الى النفس مباشرة بعد الخلق (وَنَفْسٍ وَمَا سَواهَا ، فَأَلْهَمَهَا فجورها وتقواها) فيميزه الطفل بوقت مبكر من حياته. (۲) يختص بالنفس دون سواها كما يوحي نص الآية المباركة. (۳) يشرق على الانسان من دون وساطة (علم حضوري يقع بلا كسب). (٤) إنه وحي باطن. (٥) لا ينتقل من طريق الوراثة إن معلومات الالهام لا تنتقل من أحد إلى أحد من هذا الطريق وإلا أصبح ابن الطبيب طبيباً بالوراثة وابن العالم عالماً بالوراثة حتماً دون أن يتلقى العلم أو الطب من أحد. (٦) منشأ الالهام غير أرضي وخارج عن طاقة الانسان.
أقسام الالهام
(۱) إلهام عام لجميع أفراد النوع البشري من دون سعي منهم أو كسب وهو إلهام دائم يصاحب الانسان من البداية حتى النهاية كألهام معرفة الفجور والتقوى والقدرة على التمييز بينهما.
(۲) إلهام خاص لفئات من الناس ويكون على درجات متفاوتة فإلهام النبي أو الولي أو الامام يختلف نوعا وكما عن إلهام الفقيه أو الشاعر أو العاشق وهو إلهام آني متقطع حسب التقدير الالهي ورد في الاثر ( إن رسول الله عليه السلام موصول بمصدر الوحي ومنبثق الإلهام، فما كان يعينه النبي من الأحكام فذاك مصدره الوحي ، وما كان يختاره صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم فمصدره الإلهام إنْ إلْهَامَ النّبي عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ حَجَةٌ قَاطِعَةٌ لَا يَسَعُ مُخَالَفَتْهُ بوجه ، وإِلْهَامُ غَيْره لَيس بحجة . ) قال النبي محمد - ص - وأعطاني الوحي، و أعطى عليا الإلهام.
Characteristics of inspiration: (1) It enters the soul directly after creation (and a soul and what proportioned it, and inspired it with its wickedness and its righteousness), so the child distinguishes it early in his life. (2) It is specific to the soul alone, as the blessed verse suggests. (3) It shines upon the human without mediation (presential knowledge that occurs without acquisition). (4) It is inner revelation. (5) It is not transmitted by inheritance; the information of inspiration is not transmitted from anyone to anyone by this path, otherwise the son of a doctor would become a doctor by inheritance and the son of a scholar would become a scholar by inheritance without receiving knowledge or medicine from anyone. (6) The source of inspiration is non-earthly and beyond human capacity. Divisions of inspiration: (1) General inspiration for all individuals of the human species without effort or acquisition, and it is a permanent inspiration accompanying the human from beginning to end, like the inspiration of knowledge of wickedness and righteousness and the ability to distinguish between them. (2) Specific inspiration for categories of people, and it is of varying degrees; the inspiration of the prophet, the saint, or the imam differs in type and quantity from the inspiration of the jurist, the poet, or the lover, and it is a temporary, intermittent inspiration according to divine decree. It is related in the tradition: 'Indeed, the Messenger of God was connected to the source of revelation and the source of inspiration. What the prophet decreed from rulings, its source is revelation, and what he chose, its source is inspiration. Indeed, the inspiration of the prophet is a conclusive proof that cannot be opposed in any way, and the inspiration of others is not a proof.' The Prophet Muhammad said: 'I was given revelation, and Ali was given inspiration.'
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies inspiration (ilham). It begins by listing six distinguishing characteristics. First, it is innate, entering the soul directly after creation, as indicated by Quran 91:7-8, enabling even a child to discern good and evil. Second, it is specific to the soul, not the body. Third, it is unmediated, a form of presential knowledge (ilm huduri) acquired without effort. Fourth, it is inner revelation (wahy batin), distinct from external revelation. Fifth, it is not inherited; if it were, children of scholars would automatically be scholars. Sixth, its source is divine, not earthly. The passage then divides inspiration into two types. The first is general, common to all humans from birth to death, like the ability to distinguish right from wrong. The second is specific, granted to certain groups at varying levels: prophets, saints, imams receive a higher type, while jurists, poets, and lovers receive a lower, intermittent form. It quotes a tradition that the Prophet's inspiration is a conclusive proof (hujja qati'a) that cannot be opposed, whereas the inspiration of others carries no such authority. The passage ends by citing the Prophet's statement that he received revelation (wahy) while Ali received inspiration (ilham), reinforcing the distinction.

Key Terms
الإلهام
الوحي
الحجة القاطعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of inspiration is a conclusive proof (hujja qati'a) that cannot be opposed?
A) General inspiration common to all humans
B) The specific inspiration of the prophet
C) The specific inspiration of the jurist
D) All types of inspiration

Key Takeaway
Inspiration (ilham) is defined by six characteristics and divided into general (universal, permanent) and specific (graded, intermittent); only the Prophet's inspiration carries absolute authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
