Distinguishing Basīrah (Inner Insight) from Basar (Physical Sight)
Definition of basā'ir as the inner faculty of perception, contrasted with physical sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between basīrah and basar in terms of their referents and functions, using Quranic evidence and linguistic analysis.

Core Idea
Basīrah is not physical sight but the faculty that perceives inner realities.

This passage from the logical text defines a crucial epistemological term: basīrah (insight). It is distinguished from basar (physical sight) by its object of perception: inner realities vs. outward appearances. This distinction is fundamental to Islamic epistemology, as it grounds the possibility of knowledge beyond the senses. We will break down the definition and its linguistic underpinnings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find where the author states the definition of basā'ir/basīrah. It is after the Quranic verses, where he says 'Basā'ir is the plural of basīrah: it is the faculty that sees the realities of things and their inner aspects.'
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'al-malaka' (faculty). The differentia is that it perceives the haqā'iq (realities) and bawāṭin (inner aspects) of things. This differentia distinguishes it from other faculties like physical sight or imagination.
The author uses a functional definition: what it does, not what it is made of.
3
Contrast with basar
Compare the definition of basīrah with that of basar. Basar is defined as the physical organ (al-jāriḥa al-nāẓira) that sees outward appearances (ẓawāhir). This contrast sharpens the understanding of basīrah as non-physical and inward-looking.
4
Analyze Quranic usage
Examine the Quranic verses cited: they use 'basā'ir' in the sense of clear proofs or insights from Allah. The author also quotes verses that use the verb 'absara' (from basar) and 'baṣura' (from basīrah) to show different linguistic roots.
The Quranic evidence supports that basīrah implies knowledge and certainty, not mere sight.
5
Apply the definition
Re-state the definition of basīrah in your own words, incorporating both its genus (faculty) and differentia (perceiving inner realities). Confirm that it excludes physical sight and metaphorical extensions.
A complete re-statement: 'Basīrah is the inner faculty that apprehends the essences and hidden aspects of things, as opposed to the outward appearances perceived by the eye.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - البصائر قال تعالى ( قَدْ جَاءَكُمْ بَصَائِرُ مِنْ رَبِّكُمْ فَمَنْ أَبْصَرَ فَلِنَفْسِهِ وَمَنْ عَمِي فَعَلَيْهَا وَمَا أَنَا عَلَيْكُم بحفيظ) (هَذَا بَصَائِرُ مِنْ رَبِّكُمْ وَهُدًى وَرَحْمَةً لِقَوْمٍ يُؤْمِنُونَ) ان الفقهاء المسلمين وعلماء اللغة العربية قد ابدعوا في شرح مضامين الآيات القرآنية الكريمة حتى يمكننا القول انهم اجلسوا الحقيقة على كرسي وقام كل منهم بتصويرها من زاويته الخاصة وهكذا تداخلت تفسيراتهم الرائعة في شرح معنى البصائر :. بصائر جمع بصيرة: وهي الملكة التي ترى حقائق الأشياء وبواطنها ، كما يرى البصر ظواهر الأشياء المادية) ٣ البصر : يقال للجارحة الناظرة نحو قوله تعالى : كلمح البصر ، وجمع البصر أبصار ، وجمع البصيرة : بصائر . ولا يكاد يقال للجارحة بصيرة ويقال من الاول أبصرت ومن الثاني أبصرته وبصرت به . قال تعالى : وابصر فسوف يبصرون بصرت بما لم يبصروا به ، أدعوا الى الله على بصيرة أنا ومن أتبعني . أي على معرفة وتحقق . وقوله تعالى : بل الانسن على نفسه بصيرة . أي تبصرة فتشهد له وعليه . والباصرة : الناظرة كقوله تعالى : وجعلنا آية النهار مبصرة . أي مضيئة » ٤ قال الشاعر الأشعر الجعفي
5. The Insights (al-Basā'ir): He said, the Most High: 'There have come to you insights from your Lord; so whoever sees clearly, it is for his own benefit; and whoever is blind, it is against himself; and I am not a guardian over you.' (This is insights from your Lord and guidance and mercy for a people who believe.) Muslim jurists and Arabic language scholars excelled in explaining the contents of the noble Quranic verses, to the point that we can say they seated truth on a chair and each of them depicted it from his own angle, thus their brilliant interpretations interwove in explaining the meaning of insights. Basā'ir is the plural of basīrah: it is the faculty that sees the realities of things and their inner aspects, just as basar (sight) sees the outward appearances of material things. Al-Basar: it is said of the organ that looks, as in His saying: 'like the glance of the eye'; the plural of basar is absār; and the plural of basīrah is basā'ir. It is hardly ever said to the organ 'basīrah'. And one says from the first 'absartu' (I saw) and from the second 'absartuhu' and 'basurtu bihi'. He said, the Most High: 'And see, so they will see', 'I saw what they did not see', 'I invite to Allah on insight, I and whoever follows me', i.e. on knowledge and certainty. And His saying: 'Rather, man is against himself, a witness (basīrah)', i.e. an insight, so it bears witness for him and against him. And al-bāsirah: the seeing eye, as in His saying: 'And We made the sign of the day bright (mubsirah)', i.e. illuminating.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines al-basā'ir (insights) and provides a linguistic analysis. It begins by quoting two Quranic verses (al-An'am 6:104 and al-A'raf 7:203) that mention 'basā'ir'. The author then comments that Muslim jurists and linguists have excelled in explaining these verses from different angles. The definition follows: basā'ir is the plural of basīrah, which is a faculty (malaka) that perceives the inner realities and essences of things, just as basar (physical sight) perceives external appearances. The passage then distinguishes between basar and basīrah linguistically: basar is the physical organ of sight (al-jāriḥa al-nāẓira), as in the Quranic expression 'ka lamḥ al-baṣar' (like a glance). The plural of basar is absār, and the plural of basīrah is basā'ir. It notes that the term basīrah is rarely applied to the physical organ. The verbs differ: 'absartu' from basar, and 'absartuhu' or 'basurtu bihi' from basīrah. Several Quranic verses are cited to illustrate the usage of basīrah in the sense of knowledge and certainty, such as bringing evidence. Finally, the term bāsirah (the seeing eye) is explained as meaning illuminating. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that basīrah is an inner vision that grasps truths, as opposed to superficial sight.

Key Takeaway
Basīrah is an inner faculty that perceives hidden truths, distinct from physical sight.

Distinguishing Basira from Basar: The Inner Faculty of Perception
Author defines basira and distinguishes it from basar, citing Quran and classical exegetes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish basira from basar based on the definition and Qur'anic evidence, and identify the defining features (genus and differentia) of basira.

Core Idea
Basira is the inner eye that perceives unseen realities, distinct from physical sight.

In this passage, the author carefully defines a key term in Islamic epistemology: basira. You will learn how basira is a faculty (malaka) of inner perception, and how it is distinguished from ordinary sight (basar). This distinction is crucial for understanding the Islamic theory of knowledge, especially in tafsir and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term being defined is 'basira' (plural: basa'ir). The author states it is a 'malaka' (faculty). Note that the passage begins with the plural form and then defines the singular.
2
Extract the genus
The genus (jins) is 'malaka' (faculty) – a stable disposition of the soul that enables perception. This places basira in the category of cognitive faculties.
In later logic, malaka often denotes an intellectual habit; here it remains spiritual.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia (fasl) is 'that sees the realities of things and their inner aspects'. This distinguishes basira from other faculties like sensory perception.
The differentia specifies basira's unique function: perceiving hidden truths.
4
Contrast with basar
The author contrasts basira with basar: basar sees external appearances of material things, while basira sees inner realities. This contrast clarifies the definition by negation.
Basar is the physical eye; basira is not an organ but a spiritual faculty.
5
Verify with Qur'anic evidence
The author quotes several Qur'anic verses to support the definition. Identify how each verse illustrates the concept: e.g., 'Ad'u ila Allahi 'ala basira' means calling with knowledge and certainty.
The verses show basira as insight that is both personal and evidential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بصائر جمع بصيرة: وهي الملكة التي ترى حقائق الأشياء وبواطنها، كما يرى البصر ظواهر الأشياء المادية. البصر: يقال للجارحة الناظرة نحو قوله تعالى: "كلمح البصر"، وجمع البصر أبصار، وجمع البصيرة: بصائر. ولا يكاد يقال للجارحة بصيرة ويقال من الأول أبصرت ومن الثاني أبصرته وبصرت به. قال تعالى: "وابصر فسوف يبصرون" "بصرت بما لم يبصروا به"، "أدعوا إلى الله على بصيرة أنا ومن أتبعني" أي على معرفة وتحقق. وقوله تعالى: "بل الإنسان على نفسه بصيرة" أي تبصرة فتشهد له وعليه. والباصرة: الناظرة كقوله تعالى: "وجعلنا آية النهار مبصرة" أي مضيئة.
Basa'ir is the plural of basira: it is the faculty that sees the realities of things and their inner aspects, just as sight (basar) sees the external appearances of material things. Al-Basar: it is said for the seeing organ, as in His statement: 'like the blink of an eye,' and the plural of basar is absar, and the plural of basira is basa'ir. It is hardly ever said for the organ (that it is) basira. From the first (basar) one says 'absartu' (I saw with the eye) and from the second (basira) one says 'absartuhu' and 'basirtu bihi' (I perceived him). He said: 'And see, they will see' and 'I have seen what they did not see' and 'I call to God with insight (basira), I and those who follow me,' i.e., with knowledge and certainty. And His statement: 'Rather, man is a witness against himself,' i.e., an insight that testifies for or against him. And al-basira: the seeing (organ), as in His statement: 'And We made the sign of the day an illuminating sight,' i.e., bright.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines 'basa'ir' (plural of 'basira') as a technical term in Islamic epistemology, contrasting it with 'basar' (physical sight). The author begins by stating that basa'ir is the faculty (malaka) that perceives the inner realities (haqa'iq) and hidden aspects of things, just as physical sight (basar) perceives the external appearances of material objects. This establishes a clear distinction: basar is sensory perception, while basira is intellectual or spiritual insight. The author then elaborates on the Arabic usage: 'basar' is used for the physical organ of sight, citing the Qur'anic phrase 'like the blink of an eye' and noting its plural 'absar'. 'Basira', however, is almost never used for the physical organ; its plural is 'basa'ir'. The verb forms differ: from 'basar' comes 'absartu' (I saw with the eye), while from 'basira' comes 'absartuhu' or 'basirtu bihi' (I perceived or understood). Several Qur'anic verses are quoted to support this: 'And see, they will see' and 'I have seen what they did not see' illustrate the perceptual use; 'I call to God with insight' is interpreted as calling with knowledge and certainty (ma'rifa wa tahqeeq). The verse 'Rather, man is a witness against himself' is glossed as 'an insight that testifies for or against him'. Finally, 'al-basira' is also used for the seeing organ in some contexts, as in 'We made the sign of the day an illuminating sight' (mubsira), meaning bright or clear. This careful lexical and exegetical analysis shows that basira is a cognitive faculty of inner vision, essential for understanding deeper truths, a key concept in Islamic epistemology.

Key Terms
بصيرة (basīra)
بصر (basar)
ملكة (malaka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Basira is the faculty of inner perception, basar is physical sight of material appearances.
C) Basira is the plural of basar.
D) Basira is a type of eye disease.

Key Takeaway
Basira is a stable faculty of the soul that perceives inner realities, distinguished from physical sight (basar) which sees only external appearances.

Defining Basirah as Inner Sight
Author defines basirah as inner light of the heart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define basirah as an epistemological concept and distinguish it from physical basar using Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Basirah is the heart's light that sees truth, while basar is the eye's sight that sees shapes.

In this lesson, we explore the primary definition of basirah as given by classical scholars. The text compiles Quranic and exegetical sources to establish basirah as inner vision essential for true knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Arabic term and its root
The term 'البصيرة' derives from 'البصر' (sight). Note how the text contrasts it with physical sight.
Root: ب-ص-ر
2
Extract the core definition
Find the phrase: 'الدلالة التي توجب العلم الذي يبصر به الشيء نفسه على ما هو به'. This defines basirah as an indication necessitating knowledge of a thing as it is.
3
Contrast with basar
The text states: 'البصيرة نور القلب الذي به يستبصر، كما أن البصر نور العين الذي به تبصر'. Note the functional equivalence but different organs.
4
Locate the Quranic evidence
The verse 'فإنها لا تعمى الأبصار ولكن تتعمى القلوب' is quoted to emphasize that true blindness is of the heart, not the eyes.
Surah Al-Hajj 22:46
5
Conclude the epistemological function
Basirah enables the soul to perceive truth and divine proofs, making it a key tool in distinguishing haq from batil.

حَمَلُوا بَصَائِرَهُمْ عَلَى أَكْتَافِهِمْ... وَبَصِيرَتِي يَعْدُو بِهَا عَتَد وأى) (البصيرة) وهي الدلالة التي توجب العلم الذي يبصر به الشئ نفسه على ماهوبه والمراد ههنا قد جاء كم القرآن الذي فيه الحجج والبراهين وتكون البصيرة: طريق الدم والبصيرة الرأس ايضا. وجمعها بصائر، ومعناه ظهور الشئ وبيانه) (البصيرة من البصر بمعنى الرؤية ، ولكنها في الغالب رؤية ذهنية وعقلانية، وقد تطلق على كل ما يؤدي إلى الفهم والإدراك، وهذه الكلمة في هذه الآيات تعني الدليل والشاهد أصل البصائر الأبصار وهو ظهور الشيء حتى يبصره الإنسان)) (البصيرة نور القلب الذي به يستبصر ، كما أن البصر نور العين الذي به تبصر البَصَائِرُ : الْهُدَى، بَصَائِرُ مَا فِي قُلُوبِهِمْ لدينهم، وليست بِبَصَائِرِ الرُّءُوس، وقرأ: فَإِنَّهَا لا تَعْمَى الأَبْصَارُ وَلَكِن تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ) (البصيرة هي النور الذي به تستبصر النفس كما أن البصر نور به تبصرُ العين ) قال بعض العارفين : البصائر كلمات الله التي تجلى منها لذوي الحقائق وبرزت من تحت سرادقاتها أنوار نعوته الأزلية) { بصائر : أي حجج وبينات يبصر بها وتتضح منها الأشياء الخفيات على أمر جلي منكشف ( ۳ ) بصائر من ربكم يستبصر بها في جميع المطالب الإلهية والمقاصد الإنسانية وهي الدليل والمدلول) قال الزمخشري وقال ابن عطية ( البصيرة هي ما ينقب عن تحصيل العقل للأشياء المنظور فيها بالاعتبار) (معنى بصائر : أي بينات وحجج ظاهرة يعرف بها الهدى من الضلال والحق من الباطل ( البصيرة : هي الدلالة التي توجب إبصار النفوس للشيء) { قَدْ جَاءكُم بَصَائِرُ مِن رَّبِّكُم } البصيرة للنفس كالبصر للبدن سميت بها للدلالة لأنها تجلي لها الحق وتبصرها به (البصيرة : هي الدلالة التي توجب البصر بالشيء والعلم به أصل البصائر من الإبصار
They carried their insights on their shoulders... and my insight proceeds with a prepared steed. (Al-Basirah) is the indication that necessitates knowledge by which the thing itself is seen as it is, and the intended meaning here has come like the Quran which contains proofs and evidences. And basirah can be: the way of blood, and basirah also means head. Its plural is basair, and its meaning is the manifestation and clarification of a thing. (Basirah is from basar meaning vision, but it is mostly mental and rational vision, and it may be applied to everything that leads to understanding and perception, and this word in these verses means proof and witness. The origin of basair is absar, which is the appearance of a thing until a person sees it.) (Basirah is the light of the heart by which one gains insight, just as basar is the light of the eye by which one sees. Al-basair: guidance, insights in their hearts for their religion, not insights of the heads, and he recited: 'For indeed it is not the eyes that are blinded, but the hearts in the chests that are blinded.') (Basirah is the light by which the soul gains insight, just as basar is the light by which the eye sees.) Some gnostics said: Basair are the words of God from which He manifested to the people of realities and from under their veils emerged the lights of His eternal attributes. {Basair: meaning proofs and evidences by which one sees and by which hidden things become clear on a manifest matter.} (3) Basair from your Lord by which one gains insight in all divine demands and human purposes, and they are the proof and the proven. Al-Zamakhshari said and Ibn Atiyyah said: (Basirah is what uncovers the acquisition of the intellect for things considered by reflection.) (Meaning of basair: clear proofs and evidences by which guidance from misguidance and truth from falsehood are known.) (Basirah is the indication that necessitates the soul's sight of the thing.) {There have come to you basair from your Lord} Basirah for the soul is like basar for the body; it is named for indication because it reveals truth to it and makes it see by it. (Basirah is the indication that necessitates sight of the thing and knowledge of it; the origin of basair is from sight.)
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of definitions of the term 'البصيرة' (basirah) from various classical Quran exegetes and philologists, presented by the author to establish its epistemological meaning. The author begins by quoting a Quranic phrase and then defines basirah as 'the indication that necessitates knowledge by which the thing itself is seen as it is', noting that it appears in the Quran containing proofs and evidences. He mentions that basirah can also mean 'way of blood' and 'head', but the primary sense is inner vision. The plural 'بصائر' means manifestation and clarification. He then cites that basirah derives from 'بصر' (physical sight) but predominantly refers to mental and rational vision, applied to anything leading to understanding. In Quranic context, it means proof and witness. The author quotes that basirah is 'the light of the heart by which one gains insight', contrasting it with physical sight (basar). He references the Quranic verse 'فإنها لا تعمى الأبصار ولكن تعمى القلوب' to emphasize that true blindness is of the heart. Continuing, he notes that basirah for the soul is like basar for the body; it reveals truth. Various exegetes (al-Zamakhshari, Ibn Atiyyah) define basirah as that which uncovers intellectual acquisition through reflection, or as clear proofs distinguishing guidance from misguidance. The passage concludes that basirah is the indication necessitating sight and knowledge, its origin being 'إبصار' (sight). Thus, the author establishes basirah as a key epistemological concept: inner spiritual vision through divine proofs, distinct from and superior to physical eyesight.

Key Terms
البصيرة
البصر
بصائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary definition of 'البصيرة'?
A) Physical sight of the eye
B) Inner light of the heart that enables true perception, distinct from physical sight
C) A type of blood vessel in the body
D) A synonym for 'head'

Key Takeaway
Basirah is the heart's inner light that enables true knowledge, distinct from and superior to the eye's physical sight.

Identify the definition of basirah in the passage and write two sentences explaining how it differs from basar.

Lexical and Qur'anic Dimensions of Basirah
Author compiles lexical and exegetical meanings of basirah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the multiple linguistic meanings of basirah and explain why the Quranic usage emphasizes 'proofs and evidences'.

Core Idea
Basirah's roots in physical sight expand to encompass divine proofs and spiritual insight.

While basirah is often understood as inner sight, classical lexicographers also list meanings like 'blood vessel' and 'head'. This lesson explores how the Quranic usage elevates the term to denote clear proofs and evidences that guide to truth.

Guided Path
1
List the literal meanings
The passage mentions 'طريق الدم' (way of blood) and 'الرأس' (head) as secondary meanings. Note these are physical.
2
Identify the shift to epistemological meaning
The text states that in Quranic context, basirah means 'الدليل والشاهد' (proof and witness). The plural 'بصائر' is defined as 'حجج وبينات' (proofs and evidences).
3
Examine the plural بصائر
The plural form is used in several Quranic verses and consistently means clear proofs that distinguish guidance from misguidance.
Example: 'بَصَائِرُ مِن رَّبِّكُم' (insights from your Lord)
4
Quote the exegetes
Al-Zamakhshari and Ibn Atiyyah define basirah as 'ما ينقب عن تحصيل العقل للأشياء المنظور فيها بالاعتبار' (what uncovers the intellect's acquisition of things considered through reflection). This links basirah to rational inquiry.
5
Conclude the lexical foundation
Basirah's lexical range allows it to serve both as physical sight and spiritual insight, but the Quran prioritizes the latter, making it a key term in Islamic epistemology.

Epistemological Function of Basirah
Author explains how basirah distinguishes truth from falsehood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how basirah functions as a tool for distinguishing haq from batil in Qur'anic exegesis.

Core Idea
Basirah is the divine light that separates guidance from misguidance.

Beyond definition, basirah serves a critical epistemological function: enabling the soul to distinguish truth from falsehood. This lesson examines how the passage presents basirah as the means by which hidden realities become clear.

Guided Path
1
Find the phrase specifying the function
Locate: 'يعرف بها الهدى من الضلال والحق من الباطل' (by which guidance is known from misguidance and truth from falsehood). This is the clearest statement of basirah's epistemological role.
2
Note the objects of insight
The passage mentions 'المطالب الإلهية' (divine demands) and 'المقاصد الإنسانية' (human purposes). Basirah provides insight into both theological and ethical domains.
3
Relate to the soul's sight
The text says: 'البصيرة للنفس كالبصر للبدن' (Basirah for the soul is like basar for the body). It reveals truth to the soul, making it see by it.
4
Integrate with reflection
Al-Zamakhshari and Ibn Atiyyah define basirah as uncovering the intellect's acquisition through reflection. Thus, basirah is not separate from rational thought but works through it.
5
Conclude the epistemological primacy
Basirah is presented as the ultimate indicator of truth, surpassing physical sight and enabling certainty in matters of faith and practice.

Defining Good and Evil in Islamic Thought
Author defines good and evil with Quranic and poetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of good and absolute good given in the passage, and distinguish between good as opposed to evil versus good as opposed to harm.

Core Idea
Good is universally desired; absolute good is desired in every situation.

This lesson examines how Islamic thinkers defined fundamental moral terms. The passage presents a definition of good and evil, introduces the concept of absolute good, and clarifies the Quranic usage where good is opposed to harm. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for analyzing ethical concepts in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined at the beginning of the passage. The author starts with 'الخير' (good) and defines it directly.
2
Extract the genus
The author places good in the broader category of 'what everyone desires' (ما يرغب فيه الكل). This is the genus.
3
Identify the differentia
The author gives examples of good: justice, virtue, and beneficial things. These are not a strict differentia but illustrate the meaning. Note that this definition is a description (rasm) rather than a strict essential definition (hadd).
4
Recognize absolute good
The passage then defines absolute good (خير مطلق) as that which is desired in every situation and by everyone, supported by a prophetic example.
5
Distinguish the two oppositions
The author notes that good is sometimes opposed to evil (شر) and sometimes to harm (ضر). The Quranic verse (6:17) illustrates the latter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخير ما يرغب فيه الكل كالعدل والفضل والشيء النافع وضده الشر قيل الخير والشر ضرتان . خير مطلق وهو ان يكون مرغوبا فيه بكل حال وعند كل احد كما وصف النبي - ص - به الجنة فقال لاخير بخير بعده النار، ولاشر بشر بعده الجنة والخير يقابل به الشر مرة والضر مرة نحو قوله تعالى ﴿وَإِنْ يَمْسَكَ اللهُ بِضُرٍّ فَلَا كَاشِفَ لَهُ إِلَّا هُوَ وَإِنْ يَمْسَسْكَ بِخَيْرٍ فَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِير يقول الشاعر إذا قيل أي الناس شر قبيلة أشارت كليب بالاكف الاصابعا
Good is what everyone desires, like justice, virtue, and beneficial things; its opposite is evil. It is said that good and evil are two opposites. Absolute good is that which is desired in every situation and by everyone, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) described Paradise: 'There is no good with which good is followed by the Fire, and no evil with which evil is followed by Paradise.' Good is sometimes opposed to evil and sometimes to harm, as in His statement: 'And if Allah touches you with harm, there is no remover of it except Him; and if He touches you with good, He is over all things competent.' The poet says: When it is said which tribe is the worst of people, the fingers of Kulaib point with the palms.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines good (al-khayr) and evil (al-sharr) from an Islamic perspective, using both a general definition and an absolute category. The author begins by stating that good is what everyone desires, citing justice, virtue, and beneficial things, while evil is simply the opposite. He then introduces a saying that good and evil are two opposites (darratan). Next, he explains 'absolute good' (khayr mutlaq), which is desired in all circumstances and by everyone. As an example, he quotes the Prophet describing Paradise in a way that emphasizes that no good is worth having if it leads to the Fire, and no evil is too great if it leads to Paradise. The author then notes that the term 'good' can be opposed to either 'evil' or 'harm', illustrating the latter with a Quranic verse from Surah al-An'am (6:17) that contrasts harm (durr) and good (khayr). Finally, he includes a poetic example from the pre-Islamic tradition where the tribe of Kulaib is pointed out as the worst, showing how evil is identified in social context.

The Conceptual Expansion of al-Najdayn
Analyzing the expansion of the term 'al-najdayn'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the author expands the Quranic term 'al-najdayn' to encompass all binary opposites.

Core Idea
The two paths become a universal category for all conceptual pairs.

In this passage, the author argues that the Quranic term 'al-najdayn' is not limited to the specific paths of good and evil, but includes all mental opposites. This expansion is supported by linguistic analysis and examples. We will trace the author's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Locate the author's initial claim in the Arabic where he says 'cannot be confined to a single specific concept'. This sets up the expansion.
2
List the examples
Find the list of opposite pairs: halal/haram, good/evil, evil/good, error/correctness. These illustrate the scope of 'najdayn'.
3
Analyse the linguistic evidence
Examine the dictionary definitions and poetry cited. Note how 'najd' means a clear path and something elevated, conveying clarity and guidance.
The poetic quotes show that 'najd' is associated with guidance and clear direction.
4
Connect to insight
The author then links 'najd' to 'basirat al-halal wa al-haram', implying that the clarity of the path is reflected in the innate ability to discern halal from haram.
5
Summarise the expansion
Write a concise summary of how the author moves from the Quranic term to a universal framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان مصطلح النجدين الوارد في القرآن الكريم لا يمكن حصره بمفهوم واحد محدد بل يمكن القول انه مصطلح شامل لجميع المتناقضات العقلية ومنها : الحلال والحرام ، الخير والشر . الخبث والطيب . الخطأ والصواب ، وهكذا يمكن تعميم مصطلح النجدين الى آلاف المتناقضات في دنيا الحياة وعالم التفكير والشعور التي يمكن تسميتها نجدين . وفي كتب اللغة العربية (النجد: ما خالف الغور . وكل شرف من الأرض استوى ظهره فهو نجد، ويجمع على أنجاد، والنّجادُ في مثل هذه الصفة أرض فيها ارتفاع وصلابة، قال: قلائص إذا علون فدفدا ... رمين بالطرف النجاد الأبعدا ويقال: ها هنا الطريق الواضح، والطريق الواضح يُسمى نجداً، وأمر نجد واضح، وطريق نجد هاد، قال أمية: وقد جاكمُ النّجدُ النذسر محمد ... دليل على طرق الهدى ليس يهمدُ ويقال: هو ابن نجدتها للدليل الهادي الذي كأنه ولد ونشأ بها) ومن النجد بصيرة الحلال والحرام قال تعالى ( وَلَا تَقُولُوا لِمَا تَصِفُ أَلْسِنَتُكُمُ الْكَذِبَ هَذَا حَلَال وَهَذَا حَرَامٌ فالحلال والحرام صفات سرمدية ملازمة للنفس البشرية في منظور القرآن وقد تعاملت الشريعة الاسلامية مع موضوع الحلال والحرام كحقائق بدهية أو مسلمات ورد في الحديث الشريف للنبي قوله (الحلال بين والحرام بين
Indeed, the term 'al-najdayn' (the two paths) occurring in the Noble Qur'an cannot be confined to a single specific concept; rather, it can be said that it is a comprehensive term for all mental opposites, including: halal and haram, good and evil, evil and good, error and correctness. Thus, the term 'al-najdayn' can be generalized to thousands of opposites in the world of life and the realm of thought and feeling, which can be called 'najdayn'. And in Arabic language books: (Al-najd: what is opposite to al-ghawr (lowland). And every elevation of land whose surface is level is called najd, plural anjad. And al-najad in this description is land with height and hardness. The poet said: ... And it is said: here is the clear path, and the clear path is called najd. And a clear matter is najd, and a guiding path is najd. Umayya said: Indeed, the najd al-nadhir Muhammad has come to you ... a guide on the paths of guidance, never fading. And it is said: he is the son of its najda, for the guiding guide who was born and raised in it.) And from al-najd is the insight of halal and haram. Allah said: 'And do not say concerning what your tongues falsely describe: This is halal and this is haram.' Thus halal and haram are eternal attributes inherent to the human soul in the Quranic perspective. And Islamic law has dealt with the subject of halal and haram as self-evident truths or postulates. It is reported in the noble hadith of the Prophet, his saying: 'Halal is clear and haram is clear.'
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'najdayn' and applies it to halal and haram. It begins by stating that the Quranic term 'najdayn' (the two ways, from Surah Al-Balad 90:10) is not limited to one meaning but encompasses all binary opposites in the mind, including halal/haram, good/evil, etc. It then gives a linguistic explanation of 'najd' from classical Arabic dictionaries: it means highland, a clear path, a clear matter. The author quotes poetry and sayings to show that 'najd' carries connotations of clarity and guidance. Then the text connects this to the 'insight of halal and haram' (basirat al-halal wa al-haram). It cites Quran 16:116, which forbids falsely declaring things halal or haram, showing that these categories are fixed and inherent in the human soul. Finally, it notes that Islamic law treats halal and haram as self-evident truths, citing the famous hadith 'Halal is clear and haram is clear'. This passage thus establishes that the ability to distinguish halal from haram is an innate human insight, grounded in language and revelation.

Key Terms
النجدين (al-najdayn): A Quranic term denoting two clear paths (good and evil) that humans are guided to discern; author expands it to mean all binary conceptual opposites. [Note: In Quran, it specifically refers to the two ways of good and evil; author generalizes it to all mental opposites.]
بصيرة (basira): Inner insight or perception, an innate faculty for discerning moral and conceptual distinctions. [Note: In earlier philosophical texts, basira often means 'proof' or 'evidence'; here it is a cognitive faculty.]
الحلال والحرام (al-halal wa al-haram): Divine legal categories that are inherent to human nature and self-evident, not merely customary. [Note: Stable across Islamic legal theory.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'al-najdayn' primarily refer to?
A) Only the paths of good and evil
B) All mental opposites
C) Only halal and haram
D) Physical elevations

Key Takeaway
The Quranic term 'al-najdayn' is expanded by the author to cover all binary conceptual opposites, not just moral ones, based on linguistic and textual evidence.

The Innate Insight of Halal and Haram
Exploring the evidence for halal and haram as innate categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the evidences the author uses to argue that halal and haram are innate categories known through basira.

Core Idea
Halal and haram are not man-made laws but eternal attributes recognized by innate human insight.

The second part of the passage focuses on 'basirat al-halal wa al-haram', the insight of halal and haram. The author quotes Quran and hadith to show that these categories are self-evident. This lesson examines how the author builds his argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic verse

2

3
Locate the hadith
Find the hadith: 'Halal is clear and haram is clear'. This supports the idea of clarity and self-evidence.
4
Connect basira to najd
Explain how the author links 'basira' (insight) to 'najd' (clear path), implying that insight is the faculty that perceives these clear categories.
5
Evaluate the argument
Assess whether the textual evidence (Quran and hadith) sufficiently supports the claim of innate eternal attributes.

Defining Rushd and Ghayy: Clarity by Opposition and Revelation
Author defines the innate ability to distinguish right guidance (rushd) from error (ghayy).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the terms rushd and ghayy are defined through opposition and scriptural confirmation.

Core Idea
Guidance and error are not ambiguous; the Quran declares their distinction is inherently clear.

In Islamic thought, the ability to distinguish right from wrong is innate but also reinforced by revelation. This passage introduces the key terms rushd (right guidance) and ghayy (error) as opposites, and immediately grounds that distinction in a Quranic verse. Understanding this definitional method is the first step in recognizing how Islamic logic treats clear and unambiguous concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
The passage defines al-rushd (and its variant al-rashad) in relation to al-ghayy (error). These are the definienda.
2
Note the method of definition
The definition is by opposition: 'al-rushd khilaf al-ghayy' (rushd is the opposite of error). This is a common technique to delimit a term by its contrary.
In logic, definition by opposition can be a form of rasm (descriptive definition) when the opposite is well-known.
3
Observe the usage equivalence
The author states that rushd is used like hidaya (guidance). This synonym provides further clarification.
This equates rushd with the broader concept of guidance, linking it to a familiar term.
4
Examine the scriptural support
The Quranic verse 'qad tabayyana al-rushd min al-ghayy' is cited as evidence that the distinction is clear and manifest.
The verse itself asserts that the distinction is already apparent; revelation only confirms what is innate.
5
Recognize the implication for Islamic logic
This passage shows that definitions can rely on both linguistic opposition and divine confirmation. The clarity of the distinction is a given, not a matter of speculation.
This underpins the concept of 'basira' (insight) as an innate capacity for recognizing truth and falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بصيرة الرشد والغي الرِّشَدُ والرُشدُ خِلاف الغي . يستعمل استعمال الهداية يقال رَشَدَ يرشد قال تعالى : قَد تبين الرشد من الغي)
The insight of right guidance and error. Al-rashad and al-rushd are the opposite of al-ghayy (error). It is used in the same way as guidance (hidaya). It is said: rashada yarshudu. He said, exalted: 'Indeed, right guidance has become distinct from error.'
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the insight of right guidance (rushd) versus error (ghayy). It begins by stating that 'rushd' (in both forms rashad and rushd) is the opposite of 'ghayy'. It explains that the term is used similarly to 'guidance' (hidaya). To illustrate, it cites the well-known Quranic verse from Surah Al-Baqarah (2:256): 'There is no compulsion in religion; indeed, right guidance has become distinct from error.' The verse itself asserts that the distinction is clear and manifest. The passage thus emphasizes that humans possess an innate ability to recognize guidance and error, a clarity that comes from divine instillation.

Key Terms
الرشد والغي

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Definitions of Beauty and Ugliness
Definitional exposition of beauty and ugliness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of beauty (specific to self vs. transmitted) and define ugliness as what the eye and soul reject.

Core Idea
Beauty is abundant goodness; ugliness is what the eye and soul shun.

The author begins by defining beauty and ugliness in a way that grounds them in both physical and moral dimensions. Understanding these definitions is essential for grasping the subsequent argument about innate moral insight.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of beauty
Locate the sentence where beauty is defined as 'abundant goodness' (الحسن الكثير). Note that it includes two types.
2
Identify the two types of beauty
The first type is beauty specific to a person in self, body, or action. The second is beauty that flows from him to others. The hadith about God's beauty exemplifies the second type.
3
Find the definition of ugliness
Ugliness is defined as what the eye shuns from physical objects and the soul shuns from actions and states. Note the linguistic derivation (قبح يقبح قبحاً).
4
Examine examples from the text
The author gives Quranic examples of ugliness (descriptions of disbelievers) and aesthetic examples. Reflect on how these examples fit the definitions.

بصيرة الجمال والقبح (1+9) .... الجمال : الحسن الكثير وذلك قسمان أحدهما جمال يختص الانسان به في نفسه أو بدنه أو فعله . والثاني ما يوصل منه الى غيره وعلى هذا الوجه ما روي عن النبي الاكرم محمد - ص - انه قال : ان الله جميل يحب الجمال . تنبيها انه منه تفيض الخيرات الكثيرة ١ القبح : القبيح ؛ ما ينبو عنه البصر من الاعيان و ماتنبو عنه النفس من الاعمال والاحوال وقد قبح قباحة فهو قبيح وقوله: من المقبوحين. أي من الموسومين بحالة منكرة وذلك اشارة الى ماوصف الله تعالى به الكفار من الرجاسة والنجاسة الى غير ذلك من الصفات وما وصفهم به يوم القيامة من سواد الوجوه وزرقة العيون وسحبهم بالاغلال والسلاسل ونحو ذلك يقال قبحه الله عن الخير أي نحاه ويقال لعظم الساعد مما يلي النصف منه الى المرفق (قبيح ۲ ان النشاط الجمالي ملكة خلاقة عند الانسان وهو على نوعين (١ جمال الطبيعة المادي كجمال جسد المرأة وجمال المنظر الطبيعي والجمال المعنوي كجمال المثل العقلية الصدق جميل ، الحب جميل ، العدل جميل ۰۰۰) ٢) النشاط الجمالي : كالشعر والرسم والموسيقى والفنون ان التدبر في آيات الذكر الحكيم يجعلنا ندرك وجود بصيرة التمييز بين الجميل والقبيح (إنّ اللهَ يَأْمُرُ بِالْعَدْلِ وَالْإِحْسَانِ وَإِيتَاءِ ذِي الْقُرْبَى وَيَنْهَى عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ وَالْمُنْكَرِ وَالْبَغْيِ يَعِظُكُمْ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَذَكَّرُونَ)) (يَأْمُرُهُمْ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وينهَاهُمْ عَنِ الْمُنكَرِ) فالانسان يجد اتصاف الافعال بأحدها ناشئا من صميم ذاته كما يعرف سائر المواضيع كالماء والتراب وانما دور الشارع هو الامر بالحسن والنهي عن القبيح . فالحسن والقبيح من البصائر العقلية الثابتة يدركها الانسان بالحجة الباطنية من دون الحاجة الى حجة ظاهرية . فالعدل حسن والظلم قبيح . اعانة الضعيف حسن السكوت عن الظالم قبيح . اما جمال الطبيعة فهو صنعة الهية وهو كذلك من البصائر قال تعالى (وَالْأَنْعَامَ خَلَقَهَا لَكُمْ فِيهَا دفء ومنافع ومِنْهَا تَأكُلُونَ ، ولَكُمْ فِيهَا جَمَالٌ حِينَ تُرِيحُونَ وَحِينَ تَسْرَحُونَ)
Insight into beauty and ugliness. Beauty: abundant goodness, and it is of two types: one is beauty specific to a person in his self, body, or action; the second is that which is transmitted from him to others. In this regard, it is narrated from the Noble Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Indeed, God is beautiful and loves beauty.' As a reminder that from Him abundant good flows. Ugliness: the ugly; what the eye shuns from physical objects and what the soul shuns from actions and states. It is said 'qabuha' (he was ugly) so it is 'qabeeh'. And His saying: 'among the ugly ones' means those marked with an objectionable state. That is an indication of what God Almighty described the disbelievers with: impurity, uncleanliness, and other attributes, and what He described them with on the Day of Resurrection: blackened faces, blue eyes, and being dragged with fetters and chains, and so on. It is said: 'May God remove him from goodness' i.e., distance him. It is said of the thick of the forearm from the middle to the elbow (ugly). Aesthetic activity is a creative faculty in man, and it is of two types: (1) material natural beauty like the beauty of a woman's body and the beauty of a natural scene, and spiritual beauty like the beauty of intellectual ideals: truth is beautiful, love is beautiful, justice is beautiful... (2) Aesthetic activity: like poetry, painting, music, and arts. Reflection on the verses of the Wise Reminder makes us realize the existence of an insight distinguishing between the beautiful and the ugly. 'Indeed, Allah commands justice, good conduct, and giving to relatives, and forbids immorality, bad conduct, and oppression. He instructs you that perhaps you will be reminded.' (Quran 16:90) 'He commands them to what is right and forbids them from what is wrong.' Man finds the description of actions by one of them arising from his very essence, just as he knows other subjects like water and earth. The role of the Lawgiver is only to command the good and forbid the ugly. So the good and the ugly are from the fixed rational insights that man perceives through internal proof without need for external proof. So justice is good, oppression is ugly. Helping the weak is good, silence towards the oppressor is ugly. As for natural beauty, it is a divine craftsmanship, and it too is among the insights. He said, 'And the grazing livestock He has created for you; in them is warmth and benefits, and from them you eat. And for you in them is beauty when you bring them in and when you send them out.' (Quran 16:5-6)
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and epistemological exposition. It first defines beauty (jamaal) as abundant goodness, dividing it into two types: beauty specific to a person (in self, body, or action) and beauty that extends to others. It then cites a prophetic hadith to emphasize that beauty originates from God. Next, it defines ugliness (qubh) as what the eye and soul reject, whether in objects or actions, and provides linguistic and scriptural examples, including descriptions of the disbelievers' punishment. The author then introduces a modern classification of aesthetic activity into material and spiritual beauty, and lists arts. Crucially, the passage argues that humans have an innate rational insight (basira) to distinguish between good and evil, citing Quranic verses (16:90 and 7:157) to show that divine commands confirm this innate perception. The author asserts that good and evil are self-evident rational truths (basa'ir 'aqliyya thabita) perceived through internal proof (hujja batinia) without need for external proof. Examples: justice is good, oppression is ugly; helping the weak is good, silence towards the oppressor is ugly. Finally, the beauty of nature is presented as divine craftsmanship, also an object of insight, with Quranic reference (16:5-6).

Key Terms
الجمال
القبح
الحسن الكثير
المقبوحين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary characteristic of ugliness?
A) It is what the eye and soul reject.
B) It is what society deems inappropriate.
C) It is only related to physical appearance.
D) It is what causes harm.

Key Takeaway
Beauty is abundant goodness, either inherent or transmitted; ugliness is what the eye and soul reject.

Innate Moral Insight (Basira)
Epistemological argument for innate moral insight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's argument that humans perceive good and evil through innate rational insight, and distinguish this from the role of revelation.

Core Idea
Good and evil are known by internal rational proof, not by external proof.

After defining beauty and ugliness, the author makes a crucial epistemological claim: humans have an innate insight (basira) to distinguish moral good from evil. This lesson unpacks that claim and its implications for the relationship between reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core epistemological claim
Find the sentence stating that good and ugly are from the fixed rational insights perceived by internal proof without need for external proof.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence presented
The author quotes 16:90 and 7:157 to show that divine commands align with innate moral perception, but argues that man already perceives good and evil from his very essence.
3
Understand the role of the Lawgiver (al-shari')
The passage says the role of the Lawgiver is only to command the good and forbid the ugly, implying that the moral values are known before revelation.
4
Apply the concept to concrete examples
The author gives examples: justice is good, oppression is ugly; helping the weak is good, silence towards the oppressor is ugly. Check your own rational intuition on these.

Lexical Foundations of Truth and Falsehood
Lexical, poetic, and Quranic definitions of truth and falsehood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the lexical definition of truth and falsehood and recognize their use in Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Truth and falsehood are fixed categories in speech, defined by correspondence with reality.

This passage begins with a clear lexical definition: lying is reporting something contrary to what it is, regardless of intention. It then reinforces this with poetic verses and elevates it to a Quranic axiom. Understanding this foundational definition is crucial before exploring deeper theological dimensions.

Guided Path
1
Extract the lexical definition of lying
Read the sentence: 'الكذب هو الإخبار عن الشيء بخلاف ما هو'. Identify the genus (الإخبار) and differentia (بخلاف ما هو). Note the inclusive phrase 'سواء فيه العمد والخطأ' which shows intention does not change the truth value.
This definition is purely factual, not theological.
2
Analyze the poetic evidence
The verse by al-Lihyani uses 'كذاب' in a farewell context. This shows the lexical usage of the word in classical Arabic poetry.
Poetry serves as a linguistic witness in Arabic lexicography.
3
Understand the Quranic grounding
The passage states that truth and falsehood are fixed Quranic categories. It cites verses like 'وَمَنْ أصدق من الله قيلا' to affirm truth as a divine attribute, and threatens liars. This establishes truth and falsehood as moral absolutes in Islam.
The Quran does not define these terms but treats them as self-evident.
4
Define truth as the opposite of lying
The passage simply says 'الصدق نقيض الكذب' (truth is the opposite of lying). This is a minimal definition that works with the lexical framework.
The lexical definition of truth is directly derived from that of lying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بصيرة الصدق والكذب الْكَذِبُ هُوَ الْإِخْبَارُ عَن الشَّيْء بِخِلَافِ مَا هُوَ سَوَاءٌ فِيهِ الْعَمْدُ وَالْخَطَأَ) أنشد اللحياني نادت حليمة بالوداع وآذَنَتْ ... أهْلَ الصَّفَاءِ وَوَدَعَتْ بكذاب) الصدق) نقيض الكذب قال أبو الهيثم وفي الحلم إدهان وفي العفو درسـة ... وفي الصدق منجاة من الشر فاصدق قال الشاعر يا معشر المردِ إني ناصح لَكُم ... والمرء في القول بين الصدق والكذب) الصدق والكذب من اصناف الكلام التي قررها القرآن الكريم كمسلمات ثابتة . قال تعالى (وَمَنْ أصدق من الله قيلا ) الّذينَ يَفْتَرُونَ عَلَى اللهِ الْكَذِبَ لَا يُفْلِحُونَ) والصديق من كثر منه الصدق وقيل لمن لا يكذب قط واذكر في الكتاب إِبْرَاهِيمَ إِنّهُ كَانَ صِدِّيقًا نَبِيا ؛ قال الامام علي عليه السلام الصدق) مطابقة المنطق للوضع الالهي والكذب زوال المنطق عن الوضع الالهي)
Insight of truth and falsehood: Lying is reporting something contrary to what it is, whether intentionally or by mistake. Al-Lihyani recited: 'Hulayma called out farewell and announced... the people of purity and said goodbye with a lie.' Truth is the opposite of lying. Abu al-Haytham said: 'In forbearance there is flattery, in forgiveness there is defect, and in truth there is salvation from evil, so be truthful.' The poet said: 'O group of boys, I am a sincere adviser to you... and a man in speech is between truth and falsehood.' Truth and falsehood are among the types of speech that the Noble Qur'an established as fixed axioms. He said: 'And who is more truthful than Allah in speech?' 'Those who invent lies about Allah will not succeed.' And 'al-siddiq' is one who abounds in truth, and is said of one who never lies. 'And mention in the Book Abraham; indeed he was a truthful prophet.' Imam Ali, peace be upon him, said: 'Truth is the conformity of speech with the divine norm, and lying is the deviation of speech from the divine norm.'
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines truth and falsehood (sidq and kidhb) from multiple perspectives. It begins by giving a lexical definition: lying is reporting something contrary to its reality, whether the error is intentional or accidental. It then cites a poetic example (by al-Lihyani) illustrating the use of the word 'kadhab' (lie). It then defines truth as the opposite of lying, and quotes Abu al-Haytham's verse praising truth as a means of salvation. The passage then moves to Quranic evidence, stating that truth and falsehood are fixed categories in the Quran, citing verses that affirm Allah's truthfulness and threaten liars. The term 'siddiq' is explained as one who is habitually truthful. Finally, Imam Ali gives a theological definition: truth is conformity of speech with the divine norm (al-wad' al-ilahi), and falsehood is deviation from that norm. This shifts the criterion from mere factual correspondence to alignment with God's standard.

Key Terms
الكذب
الصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lexical definition in the passage, what is lying (al-kidhb)?
A) Intentionally reporting something false
B) Reporting something contrary to what it is, whether intentionally or by mistake
C) Any speech that contradicts the Quran
D) A statement made with bad intentions

Key Takeaway
The lexical definition of lying is reporting contrary to reality, regardless of intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Imam Ali's Theological Redefinition of Truth
Imam Ali's theological redefinition of truth and falsehood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Imam Ali's theological definition from the lexical definition and explain the concept of al-wad' al-ilahi.

Core Idea
Truth is not just factual accuracy but alignment with God's standard.

After presenting the lexical and Quranic foundations, the passage concludes with a profound redefinition by Imam Ali. He introduces a new criterion: the divine norm (al-wad' al-ilahi). This lesson unpacks that shift and explores its implications for how we evaluate truth in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Locate Imam Ali's definition
Find the sentence: 'الصدق مطابقة المنطق للوضع الإلهي والكذب زوال المنطق عن الوضع الإلهي'. This is set apart from the rest by its formal structure.
The Arabic uses 'مطابقة' (conformity) and 'زوال' (deviation) as key terms.
2
Identify the new term: الوضع الإلهي
This term means 'the divine norm' or 'the divine standard'. It refers to God's established order, including His commands, wisdom, and purpose. It elevates truth from mere factual correspondence to alignment with God's design.
This term is not found in the earlier lexical or Quranic parts of the passage.
3
Compare with lexical definition
The lexical definition uses 'بخلاف ما هو' (contrary to what it is) which refers to objective reality. Imam Ali uses 'زوال عن الوضع الإلهي' (deviation from divine norm) which is broader: a statement may be factually accurate but still false if it violates God's purpose.
This does not negate the lexical definition but adds a higher dimension.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
Consider a witness who testifies truthfully about a fact, but that testimony supports an unjust tyrant. According to Imam Ali, this testimony would be 'false' because it deviates from the divine norm of justice, even though it is factually correct.
Islamic ethics condemns such testimony; truth-telling is praiseworthy only when aligned with divine objectives.

بصيرة الصدق والكذب الْكَذِبُ هُوَ الْإِخْبَارُ عَن الشَّيْء بِخِلَافِ مَا هُوَ سَوَاءٌ فِيهِ الْعَمْدُ وَالْخَطَأَ) أنشد اللحياني نادت حليمة بالوداع وآذَنَتْ ... أهْلَ الصَّفَاءِ وَوَدَعَتْ بكذاب) الصدق) نقيض الكذب قال أبو الهيثم وفي الحلم إدهان وفي العفو درسـة ... وفي الصدق منجاة من الشر فاصدق قال الشاعر يا معشر المردِ إني ناصح لَكُم ... والمرء في القول بين الصدق والكذب) الصدق والكذب من اصناف الكلام التي قررها القرآن الكريم كمسلمات ثابتة . قال تعالى (وَمَنْ أصدق من الله قيلا ) الّذينَ يَفْتَرُونَ عَلَى اللهِ الْكَذِبَ لَا يُفْلِحُونَ) والصديق من كثر منه الصدق وقيل لمن لا يكذب قط واذكر في الكتاب إِبْرَاهِيمَ إِنّهُ كَانَ صِدِّيقًا نَبِيا ؛ قال الامام علي عليه السلام الصدق) مطابقة المنطق للوضع الالهي والكذب زوال المنطق عن الوضع الالهي)
Insight of truth and falsehood: Lying is reporting something contrary to what it is, whether intentionally or by mistake. Al-Lihyani recited: 'Hulayma called out farewell and announced... the people of purity and said goodbye with a lie.' Truth is the opposite of lying. Abu al-Haytham said: 'In forbearance there is flattery, in forgiveness there is defect, and in truth there is salvation from evil, so be truthful.' The poet said: 'O group of boys, I am a sincere adviser to you... and a man in speech is between truth and falsehood.' Truth and falsehood are among the types of speech that the Noble Qur'an established as fixed axioms. He said: 'And who is more truthful than Allah in speech?' 'Those who invent lies about Allah will not succeed.' And 'al-siddiq' is one who abounds in truth, and is said of one who never lies. 'And mention in the Book Abraham; indeed he was a truthful prophet.' Imam Ali, peace be upon him, said: 'Truth is the conformity of speech with the divine norm, and lying is the deviation of speech from the divine norm.'
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines truth and falsehood (sidq and kidhb) from multiple perspectives. It begins by giving a lexical definition: lying is reporting something contrary to its reality, whether the error is intentional or accidental. It then cites a poetic example (by al-Lihyani) illustrating the use of the word 'kadhab' (lie). It then defines truth as the opposite of lying, and quotes Abu al-Haytham's verse praising truth as a means of salvation. The passage then moves to Quranic evidence, stating that truth and falsehood are fixed categories in the Quran, citing verses that affirm Allah's truthfulness and threaten liars. The term 'siddiq' is explained as one who is habitually truthful. Finally, Imam Ali gives a theological definition: truth is conformity of speech with the divine norm (al-wad' al-ilahi), and falsehood is deviation from that norm. This shifts the criterion from mere factual correspondence to alignment with God's standard.

Key Terms
الوضع الإلهي
مطابقة المنطق للوضع الإلهي

Imam Ali elevates truth to a theological virtue: conformity with the divine norm.

The Four Meanings of al-Haqq (Truth)
Author enumerates four senses of haqq with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four meanings of al-haqq (truth) as enumerated in the passage and match each to its supporting Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Truth is polysemous in the Quran; 'haqq' carries ontological, divine, epistemological, and ethical meanings.

In this passage, the author clarifies that the term 'al-haqq' (truth) is not used univocally. Instead, it has four distinct senses, each grounded in Quranic usage. Understanding these distinctions is essential for accurate interpretation of Islamic texts. Let us parse each meaning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first meaning
The first meaning is ontological: al-haqq refers to the Creator (Allah) as the one who brings things into existence with wisdom. The Quranic verse is 'That is Allah, your Lord, the Truth.' Here, haqq is a name of Allah.
2
Extract the second meaning
The second meaning relates to divine actions: everything Allah does is haqq because it is done according to wisdom. The verse 'Is it true? Say: Yes, by my Lord, indeed it is truth' refers to the truth of divine actions, such as the resurrection.
3
Analyze the third meaning
The third meaning is epistemological: haqq in belief means correspondence with reality. Example: belief in resurrection and reward is haqq if it matches the actual state of affairs. The verse 'Then Allah guided those who believed to the truth concerning that over which they had differed' illustrates this sense.
4
Understand the fourth meaning
The fourth meaning is ethical: haqq in speech and action means they are performed as they ought to be, in the right measure and time. The verse 'The word from Me has come true: I will surely fill Hell' shows divine decree as haqq.
5
Compare and contrast the senses
Notice how each sense applies to a different domain: Creator, actions, beliefs, and actions/speech. Recognizing the correct sense in context prevents equivocation and misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحق على أوجه هي: الاول: يقال لموجد الشيء بسبب ما تقتضيه الحكمة كقوله تعالى (فَذَلِكُمْ الله ربِّكُمُ الْحَقِّ) الثاني : يقال للموجد بحسب مقتضى الحكمة ولهذا يقال فعل الله تعالى كله حق . كقوله تعالى : ( ويستنبِئُونَكَ أحَق هُوَ قُلْ إِي وَرَبِّي إِنَّهُ لَحَقِّ) الثالث : في الاعتقاد للشيء المطابق لما عليه ذلك الشيء في نفسه كقولنا اعتقاد فلان في البعث والثواب والعقاب والجنة والنار حق . قال تعالى : فهدى الله الذين ءامنوا لما اختلفوا فيه من الحق الرابع : للفعل والقول الواقع بحسب ما يجب وبقدر ما يجب وفي الوقت الذي يجب كقولنا فعلك حق وقولك حق ، كقوله تعالى : حَقِّ الْقَوْلُ مِنِّي لَأمْلَأَنْ جَهَنَّمَ مِنَ الْجِنَّةِ وَالنَّاسِ أَجْمَعِينَ
Truth has aspects: First: It is said of the creator of a thing due to what wisdom requires, as His saying 'That is Allah, your Lord, the Truth.' Second: It is said of the creator according to the requirement of wisdom, and therefore it is said that all of Allah's action is truth, as His saying 'And they ask you to inform them: Is it true? Say: Yes, by my Lord, indeed it is truth.' Third: In belief, for something conforming to what that thing is in itself, as our saying 'So-and-so's belief in resurrection, reward, punishment, paradise, and hell is truth.' Allah said: 'Then Allah guided those who believed to the truth concerning that over which they had differed.' Fourth: For action and speech occurring according to what is obligatory, in the measure that is obligatory, and at the time that is obligatory, as our saying 'Your action is truth and your speech is truth,' as His saying: 'The word from Me has come true: I will surely fill Hell with jinn and people all together.'
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'haqq' (truth) in four distinct senses. The first sense is ontological: haqq refers to the creator (Allah) as the one who brings things into existence in accordance with wisdom. The second sense is about divine actions: everything Allah does is haqq because it proceeds from wisdom. The third sense is epistemological: haqq in belief means a proposition that corresponds to reality, such as belief in the afterlife. The fourth sense is ethical: haqq in speech and action means they are performed as they ought to be, in the right measure and time. Each sense is supported by Quranic verses. This passage is from a work that likely discusses the Quranic concept of truth.

الحق

A) First sense
B) Second sense
C) Third sense
D) Fourth sense

Key Takeaway
Al-haqq has four distinct meanings: ontological (the Creator), divine action, belief correspondence to reality, and ethical propriety in speech and action.

Definition of Impurity: Sensory and Spiritual
Author defines najasa and divides it into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of impurity (najasa) and distinguish between sensory and spiritual impurity.

Core Idea
Impurity is not only physical; spiritual impurity is also recognized in the Quran.

The author defines najasa broadly as filth, divided into physical (perceptible by senses) and spiritual (perceptible by insight). Understanding this dual division is crucial for interpreting Quranic verses and fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching definition of najasa
Find the common element in the definition: 'najasa is filth'. What does 'filth' include?
2
Extract the first type: sensory impurity
Look for the phrase 'perceived by the senses'. What examples come to mind?
Physical filth that can be seen, smelled, etc.
3
Extract the second type: spiritual impurity
Find the Quranic evidence: 'Indeed the polytheists are unclean'. This is not physical filth.
Spiritual impurity of shirk.
4
Note the opposite meanings of the root 'najasa'
The verb can mean both to make impure and to remove impurity. How does this affect the concept?
Demonstrates the linguistic richness of Arabic.
5
Connect to fiqh
Compare this broad definition to the narrower fiqh definition restricted to specific physical impurities.
Why might the author include spiritual impurity?

النجاسة هي القذارة وذلك قسمان قسم يدرك بالحاسة وضرب يدرك بالبصيرة . والثاني وصف الله تعالى به المشركين فقال (إِنَّمَا الْمُشْرِكُونَ نَجَسٌ ) ويقال نجسه أي جعله نجسا . ونجسه أيضا أزال نجسه ومنه تنجيس العرب وهو شيء كانوا يفعلونه من تعليق عودة على الصبي ليدفعوا عنه نجاسة الشيطان والناجس والنجيس داء خبيث لا دواء له. الطهارة قسمان طهارة جسم وطهارة نفس وحمل عليهما الايات عامة. يقال طهرته فطهر وتطهر واطهر فهو طاهر ومتطهر قال { وَإِنْ كُنتُمْ جنبا فاطهروا - المائدة ٦ } أي استعملوا الماء أو ما يقوم مقامه قال {وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ فَإِذَا تَطَهَّرْنَ فَأْتُوهُنَّ مِنْ حيْثُ أَمَرَكُمُ اللهُ إِنَّ اللهَ يُحِبُّ التَّوَّابِينَ وَيُحِبُّ الْمُتَطَهِّرِينَ - البقرة ۲۲۲ } أي يفعلن الطهارة التي هي الغسل وقال رِجَالٌ يُحِبُّونَ أَنْ يَتَطَهَرُوا وَاللهُ يُحِبُّ الْمُطَهِّرِينَ - التوبة ١٠٨ فانه يعني تطهير النفس وقوله في صفة القرآن مرفوعة مطهرة أن الطاهر قسمان قسم لا يتعداه الطهارة كطهارة الثوب فانه طاهر غير مطهر به . وضرب يتعداه فيجعل غيره طاهرا به • فوصف الله تعالى بأنه طهور تنبيها على هذا المعنى
Impurity is filth. It is of two types: a type perceived by the senses and a type perceived by insight. The second is what Allah described the polytheists with when He said: 'Indeed the polytheists are unclean.' And it is said 'najjasahu' meaning he made it impure. And 'najjasahu' also means he removed its impurity. And from this is the 'tajnīs' of the Arabs, which is something they used to do of hanging a stick on a boy to ward off the impurity of the devil. And al-nājis and al-najīs is a malignant disease for which there is no cure. Purity is of two types: purity of the body and purity of the soul, and the verses are general upon both. It is said 'tahhartuhu fatahira wa tatahhara wa ittahara' so he is tāhir and mutatahhir. He said: 'And if you are in a state of janāba, then purify yourselves' (5:6) meaning use water or what stands in its place. He said: 'And do not approach them until they are pure. And when they have purified themselves, then come to them from where Allah has commanded you. Indeed Allah loves those who are constantly repentant and loves those who purify themselves' (2:222) meaning they perform purification which is washing. And He said: 'Men who love to purify themselves, and Allah loves those who purify themselves' (9:108) for it means purification of the soul. And His saying in the description of the Qur'an: 'Exalted, purified' — that the pure is of two types: a type whose purity does not extend (to others), like the purity of a garment, for it is pure but not purifying. And a type whose purity extends, making something else pure by it. So Allah described Himself as 'Tahūr' as a notification of this meaning.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines impurity (najāsa) and purity (tahāra). Najāsa is divided into physical impurity (perceived by senses) and spiritual impurity (perceived by insight), such as the impurity of polytheism. The root 'najasa' has opposite meanings: to make impure and to remove impurity. Similarly, tahāra is divided into physical (body) and spiritual (soul). The Quranic verses on tahāra are interpreted to cover both. The passage also introduces a distinction between 'tāhir' (pure but not purifying) and 'tahūr' (purifying), and notes that Allah is described as 'Tahūr' to indicate that His purity is effective.

القذارة
المشركون
تنجيس

Definition of Purity: Body, Soul, and Tāhir vs Tahūr
Author defines tahara and distinguishes tāhir from tahūr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tāhir and tahūr and explain the two types of purity (physical and spiritual).

Core Idea
Purity is of two kinds: bodily and spiritual, and among the pure, only tahūr has the power to purify others.

The author defines purity (tahāra) as twofold: purity of the body and purity of the soul, and uses Quranic verses to support both. He then introduces a crucial distinction between tāhir (pure but not purifying) and tahūr (purifying), and applies it to Allah's attribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of purity
The passage states: 'purity of the body and purity of the soul'. What Quranic verses are cited for each?
2
Find Quranic evidence for each type

Notice the different contexts.
3
Extract the distinction between tāhir and tahūr
Look for the phrase: 'a type whose purity does not extend... and a type whose purity extends'. What example is given?
Tāhir: garment; tahūr: water.
4
Understand how this applies to Allah
Allah is described as 'Tahūr'. What does this indicate about His attribute of purity?
His purity is effective and purifies others.
5
Reflect on the linguistic root
The verb 'tahhara' and its forms (tāhir, mutatahhir, tahūr). How do these forms relate to the meaning?

Basa'ir: The Innate Foundations of Knowledge
Author describes basa'ir as eternal, infallible, foundational truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of basa'ir and distinguish them from empirical or derived knowledge using the criteria given in the passage.

Core Idea
Basa'ir are the a priori, infallible truths that underpin all human knowledge and moral judgment.

In this passage, the author describes 'basa'ir' (insights) as eternal, certain principles that every human possesses. These include ethical truths, mathematical axioms, emotional reactions, and logical laws. Importantly, they are not sensory or empirical; they are directly known to the mind and require no proof. Understanding basa'ir is crucial for recognizing the foundations of knowledge in Islamic sciences like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of basa'ir
Find the sentence where the author states what basa'ir are. Notice the attributes: ثابتة خالدة (eternal), خارجة عن تصرف الشياطين (beyond devilish influence), معصومة عن الخطأ (infallible).
The definition is spread across the passage; the key is that they are self-evident and certain.
2
List the categories of examples
Identify the different types of examples given: ethical values (الصدق والكذب), emotional judgments (النفس تحب من أحسن إليها), innate laws (الكل أكبر من الجزء), logical foundations (شروق الشمس دليل بداية النهار).
Each category illustrates a different domain of self-evident truth.
3
Identify common features
From the description, extract the shared properties: they have no external existence, are not perceived by senses, are infallible, and the soul assents to them without proof.
These features distinguish basa'ir from empirical facts or derived knowledge.
4
Contrast with empirical knowledge
Compare basa'ir to sensory knowledge. The passage says they are not perceived by الحواس الظاهرية (apparent senses). Think of an example like 'the whole is greater than the part' which cannot be seen but is known rationally.
This contrast helps clarify the epistemological status of basa'ir.

ان هذه البصائر ثابتة خالدة لجميع البشر من دون استثناء وهي خارجة عن تصرف وقدرة الشياطين . ان قدرة العقل على معرفة القيم الاخلاقية ونقائضها لا يمكن انكارها كالصدق والكذب ، الوفاء والخيانة ، الحق والباطل ، والقوانين البدهية الموجود لا يكون معدوما والمباديء الرياضية (٤٢٢) والقرارات الوجدانية ( النفس تحب من احسن اليها وتبغض من اساء اليها ) والقوانين الفطرية (الكل اكبر من الجزء) والاسس المنطقية (شروق الشمس دليل بداية النهار) ان هذه الثوابت العقلية هي البصائر التي قصدها القرآن الكريم وفي هذا الاتجاه قال الامام علي السلام ( نظر البصر لا يجدي اذا عميت البصيرة ) ان هذه البصائر ليس لها وجود في الخارج ولا تدرك بالحواس الظاهرية وهي أي البصائر معصومة عن الخطأ وعارية عن احتمال عدم المطابقة مع الواقع وتضطر النفس للاذعان لها من دون ان تطالب بدليل أو برهان على صحتها وهي الحجر الاساس للعلوم والاكتشافات والاختراعات
Indeed these insights are established and eternal for all human beings without exception, and they are outside the control and power of devils. Indeed the mind's ability to know ethical values and their opposites cannot be denied, such as truth and falsehood, faithfulness and betrayal, right and wrong, and the self-evident laws: the existent is not non-existent, and the mathematical principles (422), and the emotional judgments (the soul loves one who does good to it and hates one who does evil to it), and the innate laws (the whole is greater than the part), and the logical foundations (sunrise is evidence of the beginning of the day). Indeed these mental constants are the insights intended by the Noble Quran. And in this direction, Imam Ali (peace be upon him) said: 'The sight of the eye is of no avail if the insight is blind.' Indeed these insights have no existence in the external world and are not perceived by the apparent senses, and they, i.e., the insights, are infallible from error and free from the possibility of non-correspondence with reality, and the soul is compelled to assent to them without demanding proof or evidence of their correctness, and they are the foundation stone for the sciences, discoveries, and inventions.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of 'basa'ir' (insights), which are defined as innate, self-evident truths that are universally known. They include ethical judgments (good/evil), mathematical axioms, emotional reactions, and logical principles. These insights are said to be eternal, beyond the influence of devils, and the basis of all knowledge. The author cites Imam Ali to emphasize that external sight is useless without inner insight. Importantly, these insights have no physical existence and are not sensed; they are directly known to the mind with certainty, needing no proof. This is an epistemological claim about foundational knowledge.

Key Terms
البصائر (al-basā'ir): Insights; self-evident, innate, certain principles that form the foundation of knowledge. [Note: In Islamic thought, basīra often means spiritual insight; here it is used in a philosophical sense as a priori principles.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an example of basa'ir?
A) The whole is greater than the part
B) Sunrise is evidence of day
C) The soul loves its benefactor
D) The specific weight of gold

Key Takeaway
Basa'ir are the infallible, non-sensory, self-evident truths that form the foundation of all knowledge, requiring no proof and compelling assent.

Definitions of Fitra (Innate Disposition)
Author presents linguistic and terminological definitions of fitra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four terminological definitions of fitra and identify their key differences and common theme.

Core Idea
Fitra is not only religious; it encompasses innate disposition, natural instincts, and a priori knowledge.

This passage from a philosophical logic textbook presents the concept of fitra, grounded in a Quranic verse. The author moves from the linguistic root to four distinct terminological definitions. Understanding these definitions is crucial for grasping how classical Islamic thought conceptualizes innate human nature and its epistemic foundation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Quranic foundation
Identify the Quranic verse (Surah Ar-Rum, 30:30) used to establish fitra as the original creation by Allah. Note how the verse links fitra to the religion of monotheism.
2
Analyse the linguistic definition
Examine the root الفطر (fatr) meaning 'splitting lengthwise' and how the author extends it to the original creation of something in a predisposed state for an action.
3
Extract and categorize each terminological definition
Read definitions 1 through 4 carefully. Note what each emphasizes: subjective feeling (1), sound nature before defect (2), information linked to instincts (3), a priori principles (4).
4
Compare and contrast the definitions
Identify the common thread: all describe an original, uncorrupted state endowed by Allah. Then note differences: some focus on psychological feeling, others on physical perfection, instincts, or abstract ideas.
Consider the shift from classical view (fitra as inclination to monotheism) to a broader epistemic concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - الفطرة قال تعالى (فأقم وجهك للدين حنيفا فطرة الله التي فطر الناس عليها لا تبديل لخلق الله ماهي الفطرة؟ ورد في كتب الشريعة الاسلامية شروحات عديدة وآفاق واسعة لشرح الفطرة وسنعرض أهمها (الفطرة لغة: أصل الفطر: الشق طولا. يقال فطر فلان كذا فطرا وأفطر هو فطورا وانفطر انفطارا. قال: هل ترى من فطور؟ أي اختلال. وفطر الله الخلق وهو ايجاده الشيء وابداعه على هيئة مترشحة لفعل من الافعال فقوله: فطرت الله التي فطر الناس عليها. فاشارة منه تعالى الى ما فطر أي أبدع وركز في الانسان من طبائع). الفطرة اصطلاحا: وردت تعريفات عديدة أهمها الآتي: ١ - (هي الشعور الذاتي الذي يحسه الانسان من نفسه، ولا يعرف له مصدرا ولا تفسيرا كالواخز الذي يشعر به حين يفعل أو يحاول أن يفعل أمر ما) ٢ - هي الطبيعة السليمة التي يكون عليها كل موجود اول خلقه قبل أن ينشب بعيب. ٣ - هي معلومات يمتلكها الإنسان مع قيام وجوده في هذه الدنيا وترتبط بالغرائز والميول، نظير أن يكون جائعا فيأكل حتى يشبع وعطشانا فيشرب الماء حتى يرتوي ٤ - هي المبادئ والأفكار الموجودة في النفس قبل التجربة.
6 - Fitra. Allah Almighty said: 'So set your face to the religion, inclining to truth, [adhere to] the fitra of Allah upon which He has created [all] people. No change should there be in the creation of Allah.' What is fitra? In the books of Islamic law, many explanations and broad horizons have been mentioned to explain fitra, and we will present the most important of them. (Fitra linguistically: The root of f-t-r is splitting lengthwise. It is said: so-and-so split something, faturan, and it split, futuran, and it split open, infitara. He said: 'Do you see any fissures?' meaning any defect. And 'Allah created (fatara) the creation' means His bringing the thing into existence and originating it in a state predisposed for some action. So His saying: 'The fitra of Allah upon which He created people' is an indication from Him to what He has originated and placed as innate dispositions in humans.) Fitra terminologically: Numerous definitions have been mentioned, the most important of which are: 1 - (It is the subjective feeling that a person feels from himself, and does not know its source or explanation, like the prick he feels when he does or attempts to do something.) 2 - It is the sound nature upon which every existent is at the time of its first creation before it becomes afflicted with a defect. 3 - It is information that a person possesses from the moment of his existence in this world and is linked to instincts and inclinations, such as when he is hungry he eats until he is full, and when he is thirsty he drinks water until he quenches his thirst. 4 - It are the principles and ideas present in the soul prior to experience.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section on the concept of fitra (innate disposition). It begins with a Quranic verse (Surah Ar-Rum, 30:30) that establishes fitra as the original creation by Allah. The author then poses the question 'What is fitra?' and indicates that there are many explanations in Islamic legal and scholarly books. The passage first gives the linguistic definition, citing the root meaning of 'f-t-r' as 'splitting lengthwise' and then relating it to Allah's act of creating things in a state predisposed for certain actions. After the linguistic definition, the author presents several terminological definitions, listed as numbers 1 through 4. Definition 1 describes fitra as a subjective feeling or pricking of conscience that a person experiences without knowing its source. Definition 2 identifies fitra as the pristine nature of a being before any defect. Definition 3 links fitra to innate information tied to instincts, like the natural drives to eat when hungry and drink when thirsty. Definition 4 describes fitra as principles and ideas present in the soul before any experience (a priori ideas). These definitions collectively portray fitra as the natural, uncorrupted state and innate knowledge endowed by Allah.

Key Terms
الفطرة
الفطر
الشعور الذاتي
الطبيعة السليمة
المبادئ والأفكار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Fitra is a multifaceted concept combining linguistic origin, innate disposition, natural instincts, and a priori knowledge, as seen in these four definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
